THE RED PAGE.
PUT TO SLEEP.
Back and forth in the rocker,
Lost in a reverie deep,
The mother rocked while trying
To sing the baby to sleep.
The baby began a-crowing,
For silent he couldn’t keep,
Aud after a while the baby
Had crowed his mother to sleep.
B. K. Munkittrick.
CAME away from the Loan Exhibition at Syd-
ney Art Gallery with enhanced respect for
local artists. It is my habitto complain that, for the
most part, these are not artists, but students and
technicalists, destitute of illumination, of inspira-
tion, of intellectual or emotional stimulus. But
among the 670 exhibits which the Sydney Trustees
have borrowed from all quarters of the globe there
is, on the average, no whit more of these desirable
qualities. The conclusion set me wondering
whether, after all, the fault lay at home. In
literature, by many tests and comparisons, I have
fairly satisfied myself that my mind is well hung,
as it were—delicately poised—that it vibrates
easily to a breath of exalted feeling, of natural
pathos, and remains stiff to conventional artifice
and pretty puppet-play. And, every time I heard
Miss Regina Nagel sing ‘Ben Bolt,’ despite the
mawkish words tears came into my eyes and
expansive altruistio yearnings into my brain—
which was by way of being ridiculous, no doubt,
as 1 saw five minutes afterwards. But it argues
some artistic affectability, I think, that for the
life of me I could not see it at the time. Had
Miss Nagel blossomed into a new Joan of Arc,
and led Australian battalions against boodle-politi-
cians and greedy employers of servile labor, I felt
capable of heroic deeds quite foreign to my ordi-
nary character as a rather tame and commonplace
citizen. But she would have had to keep on sing-
ing ‘ Ben Bolt ’ continuously—or with not more
than five-minute intervals.
• * * •
So, if literature could thrill me and exalt me, if
a dozen moments after Miss Nagel opened that
admirable mouth my heart-strings shook like
harp-strings, how was it that (said-to-be) beautiful
combinations of form and color could not move me
as did beautiful combinations of words or sounds ?
Could it be that I was not sufficiently familiar
with the technique of painting ? (For I do not
undervalue technique generally : I delight in the
clear, full, true note, in the subtle meetings and
partings of a well-made harmony, its tantalising
pauses and anticipations and the satisfaction of
the close ; and, while preferring thought plus
phrase to phrase merely or phrase mainly, I re-
ceive keen pleasure from Poe’s
Banners yellow, glorious, golden
On its roof did float and flow,
(This—all this—was in the olden
Time, long ago),
or Verlaine’s
La lune blanohe
Luit dans les bois ;
De chaque branche
Part une voix
Dans la ram£e . .
O bien aimSe 1
or Sohiller’s
O S zarte Sehnsuoht, siiszes H often,
Der ersten Liebe gold’ne Zeit,
Das Auge sieht den Himmel often,
Es sohweigt das Herz in Seligkeit—
where the poetry seems rather breathed from the
mellifluous vowels than uttered by the sentiment
whioh the words carry.)
• » *
But I do appreciate technique in painting—
not only technique whioh is artistic by virtue of
the forceful presentation of a subjective idea, but
also technique which is artistic by virtue of
beautiful presentation of an objective scene—just
as, in the fragments quoted, it is less the con-
tained thought which stirs me than the external
sound, look, flavor of the words themselves. For
example, I get from a photogravure illustration
of ‘ Silence ’ (in Poe’s works, Ingram’s ed., John
Nimmo, 1884, vol. iv.), by F. M6aulle, almost
precisely the impression which Verlaine’s lyric
makes. (It is fairer to quote the other stanzas ;
though a something-hackneyed touch oomes in the
close.)
reflate
—Profonde miroir—
La silhouette
Du saule noir
Oil le vent pleure . . .
R&vons, c’est l'heure.
Un vaste et tendre
Apaisement
Semble descendre
Du firmament
Que l’astre irise . .
C’est l’heure exquise.
The difference is that Mdaulle’s engraving has
thought in it as well as beauty, and leaves a pro-
founder memory. And, if you leave the humanity
out of Verlaine’s poem, I get almost exactly his
artistic effect from Louis Douzette’s picture
* Moonlight ’ (No. 135 in the Sydney Gallery).
That is something like painting—not because of
the technique, but because of the result of the
technique—because, being mere colour on canvas,
it sings music, speaks poetry—in a word, because
it is Art. I might ask local pretty-posers and
dabby-daubers to take a hint from Douzette;
but hints are seeds that never germinate unless
you have the right soil of men to plant them in.
To be an artist, you want not only an artist’s
fingers, but an artist’s brain—which is a very rare
bird in any latitude. And the mischief of it is
that the finger-man can rarely or never see the
difference between his own work and art. * There
is the colouring,’ he says; ‘ there is the drawing ;
there is the composition. If anything, the com-
bination of artistic qualities is more effective in
my picture than in that which you praise, while
damning mine.’ Very likely, Mr. Finger-man,
very likely; but you see your combination is
mechanical ; the other fellow’s is chemical, and his
chemistry is the chemistry of the Soul. It
is just the same in literature, in music. There
are the words, there are the notes, at everyone’s
disposal. But how few can combine them to
satisfy even an ordinary taste? and who, that
has any sense at all of the delicate differences
between impressions, ever combined them to
satisfy his own.
* * . *
And so throughout the field of effort.
Take two flowers, and three bits of lace,
and a scrap of chiffon, and set a whole
workroomful of nimble feminine fingers to
try and transform them into a milliner’s
masterpiece. Likely enough, after hours of
elaboration, the result will be nothing all round —
fashionable dust and ashes, so to speak. Then up
oomes a small person with an Eye—takes the most
promising, but still imperfect result—rends and
tears; replaces; sticks a pin here, a pin there;
gives four pulls, a twist, and a dexterous pat—
and the workroom ohs! with admiration at
three guineas’ worth of fal-lal and frippery— alias
bonnet. Admire the artistic brain of the small
person with the Eye—her fingers were no better
than anybody else’s. N.B. —Deducting a little
male exaggeration, pardonable under the circum-
stances, I have seen this done; and the small
person is a friend of mine. How did she do it ?
By Emu, I don’t know; though with a little
tuition I began to perceive the difference between
the masterpiece and the failure. Then I
understood why a Bond - street milliner
gives ten guineas apiece and more for her
Paris models at the beginning of every season.
And I admired the vast sheep-crowd of women,
in that, not having brains enough to create fasci-
nating headgear for themselves, they yet have
enough to pay through their noses—or their hus-
bands’ noses—for the confection that suits them ;
while most of the dabby-daubers doubtfully see
the difference between their own creations and an
artist’s. But lam aware that here thegeneralisation
is shaky ; for many women obviously do not know
what suits them. Yet the workwomen knew the
difference between my friend’s result and theirs.
* * * •
I should like to know whether the creators of
Paris models are themselves satisfied with them.
For I judge that, the better the artistic brain, the
greater its dissatisfaction with its own products.
I know a man of some3s or so, who in his twenties
used to write very decent verse, but who has not
composed a line for years. Some time ago I asked
why. He explained that his faculty of artistic
appreciation had outstripped his faculty of artistic
creation, so that he was perforce sterile. ‘ Take a
simple case,’ he said, ‘ which applies either in verse
or prose. Say I wish to express the idea, “ Twice
two is four.” Is twice two four ? I have intellec-
tual doubts about it myself: at times I have
seemed on the verge of understanding how twice
two might be three, or five, or any number ; but
the doubts are not strong enough to hinder me
from affirming the idea. Granted the idea’s accu-
racy, what about the grammatical accuracy of the
phrase? Should I say, “ Twice two is four ”? or
“ Twice two are four ” ? With a little hesitation
I decide in favor of “Twioe two is four.”
* * * •
‘ And now comes my real difficulty. The idea
of “Twice two is four” does not present
itself to my mind in either that form-of-
sounds or form-of-letters. Take the sounds first.
The sound of “ twice ” does not represent for me
the idea of “ twice.” As nearly as I can image
and phrase it, 1 want for the idea “twice” a
sound like agur. “Two” and “is” I pass as
sufficient, but for the idea of “four” I want a
sound like fone. So that, to represent the idea
of “twice two is four,” I want something like
“agur two is fone.” But obviously this would
not be understood, so I reluctantly sacrifice my
own sound-conception of the idea to the conven-
tional sound-conception, and write “ twice two is
four.” But now my ear takes up the quarrel—or
rather, that part of my brain which deals with
sounds in se, without reference to implied ideas.
I pass “twioe two” with some difficulty ; but I
cannot endure the hiss-f of “is four.” My ear
wants some combination like “Twice two dalone.”
(There is the same ear-objection to “ agur two ”
and “ is fone.”)
* • • *
‘Well, suppose I do violence to myself
once more, and write down “Twice two is
four.” What a horrible array of meaningless
signs ! how they outrage the eye ! The idea of
“twice two is four” presents itself to my
eye as something like this ’—and he sketched
two strings of sausages hanging from a ceil-
ing, with two sausages in each string,
while from their joined lower - ends hung
a very large hot-cross-bun. ‘But here again,
if I represented my ideas truly, I should
not be understood. And remember that every
word I add to “Twice two is four ” increases my
difficulty in geometrical ratio, since each word
must be considered in relation to its harmony of
sense and sound and sight with all the words pre-
ceding—and you will comprehend how impossible
it is to me to compose a line. On several occa-
sions, by resolutely enduring acute mental agony,
I have got, metaphorically speaking, as far as
“Twice two”—no farther. So, since my intelli-
gence matured, I speak as little as possible, and
write —never 1 ’
Does this read fantastic? In essence it is fact
—fact familiar to every reader whose power or
acquisition and perception is stronger tlian ms
power of creation —that is, whose sensoiy I ” u _
areas have been unduly developed at the cost ot
his motor brain-areas. There is a Sydney artist
who is so learned in theory, so particular in prac-
tice, that it takes him a month to make one
small pen-and-ink drawing. It is usually by no
means a bad drawing, either; but it is so tar
below his ideal of perfection that he has a load on
his mind till he has destroyed it and started a,ll
over again. He has been starting all over again
all his life-time—never gets any forwarder. (*es,
Beau Brummel did tie his cravat right at last
superbly right in the eyes of ordinary people ; but,
depend upon it, it was never right in the eyes ot
Beau Brummel.)
• « * #
Returning to my artistic sheep—and dispensing
with elaboration of the points that (1), after all,
Louis Douzette’s picture doesnot give the highest
artistic result (because Verlaine’s quoted lyric is
poetically inferior to Matthew Arnold 8 Ibe
Buried Life’); and that (2) the similarity of re-
sult attained by Douzette and Verlaine comes
only to a small extent because they are both
treating moonlight subjects —is nearly all due to
the similarity of their minds, which, acted on by
similar causes, have produced similar effects I
drop by a superficial association of ideas to Julian
Ashton’s ‘Moonlight ’ (188), from which one gains
a much better opinion of his talents than from
the expressionless posing Magdalen (93), An
advantage of judging by artistic results, and not
by technique, is that media make no difference
in a comparison—you can mentally put oil by
water-color, or water alongside pastel, and ad-
measure total impressions, just as you can measure
the relative impression made on your palate by
clear Julienne or thick turtle soup. I prefer clear
Julian myself —mingled with moonlight and
poetry, but unmixed with baser models ; and
could accentuate the preference if No. 188 were
in a smaller frame, with a thinner card mount-
ing. There is another moonlight picture close by
—Terry’s ‘North Head ’ (178) —a fine picture with
an arched shape which is ugly anyway, and uglier
in the square frame. Yet another moonlighter —
Goeterick’s ‘Lake ’ (417)—looks good, but is hung
too high to be comprehended. I would like those
three pictures—aud Douzette’s —hung together
(after the frames, &c., of Ashton’s and Terry’s
had been altered) so that I could stand and ‘ draw
the quiet night into my blood.’ And I think I
would then catch something of the same sad-
exultant feeling that I get when Miss Nagel sings
‘Ben Bolt.’
* • • •
Of all the pictures in the Loan collection, those
most Nagelish in effect are two by Conrad Mar-
tens — ‘An Explorer’s Camp ’ (217) and ‘Scene in
Patagonia ’ (229) —the latter for choice. It may
not be Patagonia, but it is poetry. Probably it
isn’t Patagonia—is a shade too warm-brown for
Patagonia, but one can pass a warm-brown Pata-
gonia, and all’s well. A warm-brown Aus-
tralia one cannot pass—Australia isn’t warm-
brown, even in the interior; and on the eastern
coast she is frankly blue and purple, though not
so purple as Minns makes her. (Yes, a generalisa-
tion is possible ; for there is chiefly question of the
quality of the light and the tints of ground and
foliage, water and hills.) Conrad Martens is warm-
brown nearly all the time, and where he is most
warm-brown, as in ‘At Apsley Falls ’ (237) or
‘ Water-wheel, Liverpool Plains ’ (238) he is least
Australian; and where he is least warm-brown, as
in ‘An Explorer’s Camp’ or ‘Old Washpool’
(239) he is most Australian. It looks as if Conrad
had brought from Europe a mind full of warm-
brown autumn tints (you can see them in Copley
Fielding’s ‘ Loch Tay ’ (326) beaming naturally)
and scattered them over this our country regard-
less. Hut he had lucid interspaces, and the clan-
gathering of his work is the Loan collection’s
strong point.
* * * •
O. W. Brierly is a numerous and rather dis-
appointing performer. Two or three of his land-
scapes are pleasant; most are undistinguished
sketches; and he makes no artistic impression. One
realises by comparison with the other hundreds
of unremarkable lan Iscapes -nearly all the Loan
collectors’ tastes seem to have run to landscapes :
figures are few bv comparison- how admirable
Piguenit’s and some of Lister Lister a work really
is :it would shine any where. But I would like
Piguenit to liven up a bit, and try some moving
scenes like Peter Graham’s ‘Rising Mists’ (per-
manent collection 113). P. seems always so
placid : his water hardly ripples, and never a gale
has ruffled his pa ; nt in my recollection. Arthur
Suker paints water-subjects well, and something
in Piguenit’s manner ; but I prefer Piguenit.
There are three foreign figure-pictures in the col-
lection which stand out above the others—not as
Art, but as superb representations of Nature—
Bellei’B ‘ Grandmother ’ (29), and Bechi’s ‘Knit-
ting’ (75) and ‘ltalian Children’ (80). They
please me—particularly the fine old grandmother
(I can see her now) - but do not stir me. ’Cause
why ?—because they are mere objective represen-
tations, destitute of subjective idea. Humanity
is all very well in its way ; but it is the Idea
that salts humanity—the Idea is sublime.
* * * •
Passing the oleographs, and the photographs,
and the portraits, and the pretty-pretlies ; and the
dead fowls hanging on a string (why on earth does
anybody want to paint dead fowls hanging on a
string?); and the flowers (which are uninteresting,
and not so real as Miss Meston’s—though one
hopes Miss Meston won’t stop all the time
at flowers) ; and Arthur Streeton’s remark-
able productions (whioh look as if he had
made a mistake when he stuck the fork in
and taken them out before they were baked); and’
Val Prinsep’s ‘Linen Gatherers’ (with all their
pretty heads bent the same pretty way, as if they
had an Alexandra limp in the neck); and Lan-
delie’s masterly ‘ Algerienne ’; and the copy of
Rossetti’s ‘ Annunciation’ (which gives the cata-
logue a chance to twice mis-spell his name very suc-
cessfully, though it isn’t a patch on the perma-
nent-collection catalogue—which is a terror for
execrable printing and abominable process-repro-
duction) ; and the square chunk of Gloom with
the crooked hole in the middle labelled ‘ Wreck of
the Duncan Dunbar’; and Bernard Evans’s fine
landscape, reminiscent of Sydney Cocks’s recent
‘Showers’; and the pictures too small to make
any impression ; and the pictures which (like the
enormous ‘Outside Paris Fortifications’ in the
permanent collection) would make just as good an
impression if they were reduced to one-sixth the
size; and the rest—passing all these, one
reaches the Old Masters, and gives their
God thanks.
* * * •
How any rightly - constituted person oan
denounce the Old Masters is what puzzles this
particular Quaker. I got more pure enjoy-
ment out of the Old Masters than I ever got
before out of anyone over the age of 26— which
figure is quite personal and arbitrary. They are
really much more amusing than ‘ The Sign of the
Cross.’ The 24 sq. feet of rusty female (fancy
treasuring 24 sq. feet of rusty female when there
are so many fresh females on two nice pink feet
‘syin,’ as A. Ward remarked, ‘to place their
heds agin weskits which kiver honest, manly
harts ’ —and even harts that aren’t honest, as long
as they’re more or less manly !) is good in her
way; and so is the St. Peter who wants some-
body’s maroon pills; and so is the ‘ Morning Hymn
in Paradise,’ with two arms up-stretched, and
two wisps of Eve’s hair (one apiece) wriggling
round to make the garb of Paradise decent in our
purer day ; and so are the wooden women and the
grinning women and the rest ; —these certainly
take well and give pleasure to me. But the gem
of the collection is Wolgemuth’s picture, with
everybody solemn except the subject (who has
an extra joint in his right arm) and the
operating-surgeon looking extra-solemn with a
knife about twenty-nine sizes too big for . . . for
the operation, in fact) —and Job Trotter, Jingle’s
Job Trotter. Sam Weller’s Job Trotter, in the
back-ground with his hat down over his eyes. I
think that, as an example of ‘high art,’Wolge-
muth’s picture is the lowest comedy which I can
at this moment remember.

Appear in dress entirely new,
It will be bold and fearless still.

A pretty chroino, “ Howdy do ?”

’Twiil give to dangle from a screw.
All kinds of printing neatly done.
The local maw ’twill weekly fill
With local gossip always true.
As, for example, “ Simon Hill
His henhouse lately painted blue.
Mark Quigley's horse has cast a shoe.
Take note, at Music Hall next Mon-
Day night the “ Taming of the Shrew."
All kinds of printing neatly done.
No cabbages can pay a bill;
For gold its course it will pursue !
The wisdom of its old goose-quill
Will be its rival’s bugaboo.
Besides short‘stories, and a few
Sweet poems, and the prankful pun
There’ll be “ Home Notes ” by Aunty Lou
All kinds of printing neatly done.

if you are troubled with

society, stupidness, despondency, loss of energy, ambi-

Various Verses.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
THE LUTE-PLAYER.
(Inspired by Collier’s beautiful picture in N.S.W.
National Art Gallery.)
Who art thou waiting yonder,
With eager watching eyes ?
What half-heard sound, I wonder,
Hath brought thee sweet surmise ?
I wonder what men call thee —
What sweet sonorous name
To match thy radiant beauty ?
What word of olden fame—
Of brave old memories gleaming
From long-dead ages’ lore —
To set the spirit dreaming—
Elaine, or Elinore ?
Thy half-turned head poised lightly
Upon thy curved neck,
Like moonlit snow all whitely
That gleam without a fleck,
Is diademed with roses
Of deep empurpled red,
That on thy cheek’s soft damask
A warmth of color shed.
Thy smitten lute still lingers
Within thy hand ; from strings
Yet quivering from thy fingers
A trembling echo rings.
Ah ! in what bygone ballad
They throbbed harmoniously,
Tuned to thy voice’s cadence
Of dulcet melody ?
Art thou a strange enchantress,
Who, with wild witching airs,
Doth make all men thy bondsmen
That hear thee unawares ?
And is that carven demon
Crouched to thine elbow nigh,
Thy fawning fierce familiar,
Obedient to thine eye ?
Who, swift as the red levin.
Through sea and earth and air
Doth hie to bring thee tidings
Of gallants gay that fare
Where lone thy tower lieth,
’Mid aged, hoary trees ;
With airy, whispering voices
He lures them to thy knees.
Yet who would give them pity
Since thou dost hear their sighs ?
’Twere worth a kingly ransom
To gaze once in thine eyes !
Nay ! thou art but a woman
Like Mother Eve of old ;
Say “ Beauty ” and “ A Woman,”
And all thy spell is told !
Nor hast thou need of other ;
Therein is charm to bind
A thousand hearts in homage.
I wonder did’st thou find,
With all who came a-wooing,
A gallant youth and free
To work thy heart’s undoing,
And, conquered, conquer thee 1
Ah ! surely ’twas his footstep
Upon the oaken stair,
That stayed thy pealing music ;
And thou art waiting there
With wayward lips that smiling
Dissembled to disdain—
Would mask the tender rapture
That beats through every vein.
I would I were that lover,
To hear thy red lips speak
The love he may discover
Betrayed upon thy cheek !
Yet still thy lover lingers
Who lingered ne’er before,
And ever thou art waiting,
And waiting evermore,
With eager lips asunder—
Bright face and rose-crowned hair.
I watch thee, filled with wonder,
And leave thee waiting there !
ZADIG.
THE WHIP OF LOVE.
The whip of Love, if deftly laid
On the heart of man or of saucy maid,
Will cut its mark in a crimson groove—
Oh ! a glorious goad is the whip of Love !
It will drive a man, though all else fail,
When his breath comes short, and his red cheeks
pale;
At his children’s moan he will forward move—
There’s a scorpion-sting in the whip of Love !
Though the miser snarl a defiant curse,
It will cut right in till it rips his purse ;
Not the nine-tailed cat by the hangman wove
Can cut so deep as the whip of Love !
Let the stockman sing of his stockwhip stout,
Or the bullocky boast of his slashing knout,
I could draft from hell to the plains above
A mob of fiends, with the whip of Love !

degree of Doctor of Laws upon the Hon.
Wilfrid Laurier (Premier of Canada). Sir
George Turner (Premier of Victoria), Mr. R.
J. Seddon (Premier of New-Zealand), Sir
Edward Braddon (Premier of Tasmania),
Sir John. Eorrest (Premier of West Aus-
tralia), Chief Justice Way, of South Aus-
tralia, and Mr. H. Escombe, Attorney-!
General c-f Natal.
The ceremony was an imposing one.
The Public Orator, Mr. J. E. Saudys, in pre-
senting them, eulogised each Premier
separately in a Latin address.

SATURDAY, JUNE 26, 1897.
Bull’s Reciprocity Treaty.
According to the information which drops in
by cable, the Premiers in London have al-
ready commenced to promise J. Bull about
everything he wants. At time of writing
they hadn’t all arrived, but those who had
reached London were promising with en-
thusiasm. This was before the jubilee
and the distribution of jubilee honors.
The Premiers hadn’t yet been presented
to the Queen, or seen the illumina-
tions, or been banqueted in state; the
gorgeous free board-and-lodging at the Hotel
Cecil, and the flunkeys, and the “Royal
carriages,” and the escort hadn’t had time to
soak in, and Chamberlain’s persuasive eye-
glass hadn’t properly settled upon them.
And, even at that early stage of affairs, all
the available Australian politicians were re-
ported as yearning after commercial treaties
with Britain and the other parts of the Em-
pire, and urgently desiring Chamberlain
to fix up a scheme of reciprocity whereby
Australia’s trade with England could be
“fostered” and “encouraged.” And, as
this desire to foster and encourage trade is
the greatest yearning of J. Bull’s soul, the
plan of a reciprocity treaty will probably be
forthcoming. What kind of a plan it will
be, and what condition the Premiers will be
in when it comes before them, and what will
happen thereafter,, are matters for the future.
* * * *
The two questions on which the British
Government was most particularly anxious
to “confer” with the seven lost grovellers
from Australasia were a defence scheme
whereby this country would do more towards
paying for Britain’s guns and ships and its
Tommy Atkins, and a scheme of commercial
reciprocity whereby it would buy more of
Britain’s exports. It may be assumed at
once, to commence with, that the only reci-
procity any country wants is that by which
the other country will take more goods from
it and pay more money for them. No
country is troubled at all as to from whom
it will buy goods ; there are no restrictive
tariffs on exports, and any country will sell
goods to its worst enemy if its worst enemy
pays for them, and, except as regards a very
few articles in limited supply, every nation
can buy as much of anything as it wants and
more, without any commercial treaties
whatever The trouble lies not in buying
but in selling, and the object of all commer-
cial treaties is to so arrange things that the
other party to the transaction will become a
better customer. Britain’s only possible
object in wanting to establish a reciprocity
system with Australia is to sell more British
goods in Australia, and, considering how
matters stand at present, the enthusiasm of
the Premiers on this subject is rather mis-
placed.
It is a great Freetrade axiom that imports
are paid for by exports, and that, in conse-
quence, foreign-trade can’t possibly be dis-
astrous, or tend to impoverish any country.
The axiom is true —in part. Imports of
goods or money are mostly paid for by exports
of goods or money. If a country has no
foreign debt, it has only to export as much
as it imports to keep solvent ; if it has a
foreign debt, then it must export enough to
pay for its imports and also to pay the inter-
est on its foreign debt, else its debt must
keep increasing, and its ultimate destination
is bankruptcy. If it proposes to reduce its
foreign debt, it must export enough to pay
for its imports, to pay interest on its liabili-
ties, and also to pay off part of the principal.
A country may import more goods than it
exports by borrowing money abroad to pay
for some of the imports, as Australia does ;
or it may pay for part of its imported goods
for a while out of accumulated gold on hand,
as Australia generally does when it isn’t bor-
rowing. Apart from all these reservations, a
country whose imports and exports are about
even may be considered solvent.
* * * *
The plaint of the aggregate Briton is that
his trade with Australia has dropped more
and more until it has become a miserably
one-sided affair. In 1891 the seven provinces
bought £30,000,000 worth of goods from J.
Bull ; in 1894 they only bought £19,000,000
worth. And as J. Bull, in 1894, bought
£30,000,000 worth of goods from Australia,
the trade certainly does appear rather one-
sided. The Briton’s desire for a commercial
treaty is based on his desire to make the trade
more even, and his general attitude is that, if
Australia doesnotbuy more from him it has no
earthly right to expect him to continue so good
a customer. Unluckily, however, Australia
is likely to be a worse customer rather than
a better one. On a rough estimate, it takes
£15,000,000 a year to pay interest on money
borrowed in England and to pay the incomes
of Australian absentees residing in the land
of Bull. Therefore, if this country
doesn’t borrow any more money, and
if J. Bull buys £30,000,000 worth of Aus-
tralian produce per annum, it is only possible
for Australia to buy £15,000,000 worth in
return. The other £15,000,000 worth of
wool, and wheat, and frozen meat, and
sundries go in paying interest, and can’t be
used for the purpose of buying British pro-
duce. Australia only contrives to buy as
much as it does by borrowing part of the
money to pay for it ; when it stops borrow-
ing then it will be a great deal worse a cus-
tomer than it is. There is very little to be
done by diverting the trade done with other
countries to Britain ; the amount which
Australia buys from regions outside the
empire doesn’t amount to anything worth
mentioning, and it consists mostly of goods
which can’t be procured from the great Bull-
land. The proposal to “ foster ” the trade
with England brings up short against the
fact that Australia already takes more goods
from England than it can honestly pay for.
* » * *
Australia already exports everything it can
raise and which it doesn’t want for its own
consumption, and as it exports more in pro-
portion to population than any other country
on earth except Belgium, the prospects of
any improvement in this direction seem to
be nil. If Britain will pay more for the
goods sent, then Australia will, no doubt,
buy more in return—that is to say, if Britain
will give twice as many yards of calico in
exchange for the same amount of frozen
mutton, this country will, doubtless, take
the calico and say nothing about it. But, if
this country buys more calico and other
things without getting more for the frozen
meat, &c., and keeps on doing so perman-
ently, then, in the end, it must stop paying
the interest, or part of the interest, due to
the British creditor. The common British
demand is that as J. Bull admits all Aus-
tralian produce free, save liquor and tobacco
and one or two microscopic sundries, the six
Protectionist provinces should admit British
produce free, or comparatively free, in re-
turn. This looks plausible, but it isn’t. It
is only by Australia sitting down as hard as
possible on goods from Britain and dis-
couraging the British trader all it knows,
while taking all possible advantage of a free
market for its goods in England, that it can
keep its imports from England so far below
its exports to that country that the difference
will cover the interest on its debts. If this
country opened all its ports freely to English
goods the margin would probably disappear
and it would ultimately go broke. If
J. Bull imposed duties on this country’s
produce, in retaliation for the duties levied
on his produce in Australia, and thus par-
tially closed this country’s only big market,
the only possible result would again be that
Australia would ultimately go broke. If
Australia went broke from either cause,
J. Bull would lose his principal and in cies .
Under present conditions he can t but g e
his interest and sell more goods m us
tralia. The situation may seem a hard one
for Bull aforesaid, but all the reciproci y
treaties on earth can’t alter it.
* * * '
There are several ways in which recipro-
city might be tried, though none of them are
likely to lead to any results worth mention-
ing. Australia might give England an
advantage of, say, 10 per cent, either y
adding 10 per cent, to its duties on goods
from outside the empire or by taking the
same amount off its duties on goods from
inside the empire, always provided Britain
gave a similar preference. The only way
could give such a preference would be by
putting 10 per cent, duties on imports from
foreign countries. This might, by shutting
out foreign competitors, create a better
market for Australian produce; in other
words, it might compel Bull to give more
money for the same amount of produce, and
with that money this country might buy
more from him, and thus get more calico for
the same amount of meat, which would be
practically the same as getting the extra
calico gratis. In that case some of the people
who were shut out, as all of them owe
money to Bull, would probably go broke
and stop payment. Everybody owes money
to Bull, more or less, and if Bull stops
taking everybody’s goods in payment of the
interest he must go without the interest.
What Bull complains of is that while he
buys £440,000,000 of goods per annum from
foreigners, these ungrateful people only buy
£290,000,000 worth in return, and whereas
his purchases from abroad are constantly in-
creasing his sales are diminishing. But the
trouble is that all the nations of the world
are more or less mortgaged to Bull, and if
he doesn’t take the interest in goods he can’t
get it at all. Through this tremendous in-
flux of raw material and ready-made
goods, his manufactures are declining and
his agriculture is practically dead ; he can
only resuscitate them by shutting out
part of the influx, and if he does that his
weaker debtors must go broke and stop pay-
ing the interest as aforesaid. J. Bull’s
ports are open to all the world, while all the
world shuts its ports, as far as possible,
against him ; this is only the natural result
of the necessity to sell much to Bull and
buy little from him, so that the difference
will cover the eternal interest bill. The
situation is a curious one, and all the in-
genuity on earth can’t fix up any kind of
reciprocity which seems likely to improve
the situation, so far as Bull is concerned.
PLAIN ENGLISH.
Free Justics.
There’s something in an ideal, or an imagi-
nary line, or a steering point, though you may
never get there exactly. And in the pre-
sent chaotic state of the law, anything that will
serve to shape the course of reform, that will
effectually prevent any backward curving of
the stream that is cleansing out the Augean
compost-heaps from the stalls of justice, de-
serves consideration. Herbert Spencer’s well-
argued claim for Free Justice serves this pur-
pose admirably, and the law reformers at
present on the fidget in S.A. and Victoria can
hardly go wrong in insisting, since they cannot
expect complete success, that anything at-
tempted shall be in at least the direction of
Free Justice. There has always been a maxim
in English law to the effect that checks should
be placed on litigation, the result of which has
been that the law has rather encouraged
high-priced justice than otherwise. The ideal
of Free Justice is requisite to fight this deeply-
ingrained prejudice of lawyers and Judges,
apart from its intrinsic merit of being the real
object of all true law-reform. Till it is in the
way of being reached, Law is, in the final result,
a millionaire’s luxury, and Justice a million-
aire’s prostitute. Here’s a hint of a step to-
wards Free Justice. Establish a legal enquiry
office, on a small scale at first, in connection
with every law department in Australia, at
which office anybody could receive any infor-
mation on ordinary legal matters he might re-
quire, and from which lie could procure at about
printing cost all the forms necessary in any
legal proceeding. At present, in Victoria, in
connection with proceedings before justices,
an informal arrangement of this sort
already exists, clerks of courts being sup-
posed to give information to intending liti-
gants about the necessary procedure. Further,
in the same province, legislative provision has
been made for giving advice relating to probate
and supplying forms to the widows of persons
leaving estates under the value of .£5OO. This
particular provision has saved many a poor
family from having to pay its last £2O ready
money to some cormorant. Why cannot the
system be extended? It would cost little or
nothing at the outset, for there are a number
of barristers in every civil service who would
be quite capable of answering the vast majority
of the questions the ordinary litigant wants
answered. The more complicated matters would
have to be dealt with as at present, until the
system developed and funds were available.
But such a system is quite capable of orderly
development into a great State department of
Justice, with its twin staffs, for plaintiff and
defendant respectively, gradually absorbing as
it grew the available legal acumen of the com-
munity, and gradually and innocuously killing
the separate legal profession. This last result
alone would probably be worth the experiment,
for while a Civil Service can be controlled, a
legal profession, wdiich is practically an im-
perium in imperio, which is steeped in the
glamour of precedent, which has the framing
as well as the interpretation of the laws, and
which is out of touch with the community, is a
grave menace to public liberty. The attention
of law-reformers then may well be called to
this idea of a Legal Enquiry Office, gradually
developing into a competent staff of experts,
simplifying the work of Judges as well as
cleaning the pathway of litigants, and finally
resulting in something like an ideal system of
Free Justice.
Three Months.
A few days ago, at Richmond (Melbourne), an
unspeakably brutal ruffian was convicted of
flogging his five-year-old daughter almost to
death and breaking both her arms, and the law,
in an outburst of honest indignation, gave him
three months’ gaol. Almost on the same day
another man, for doing a kindly act by throw-
ing some tobacco over the gaol-wall to a mate
who was “ doing his little bit ” inside, was sent
to prison for a year. The law was quite calm
in this second case ; it didn’t lose its temper
and make dreadful remarks to the prisoner as it
did to the fiend who broke the arms of his little
daughter ; if it had lost its temper in the same
degree the man who threw the tobacco over the
wall would probably have got seven years. In
the case of the man who tortured his child
almost to death, it was a second conviction,
otherwise he would probably have got off with
a month, or been fined 40s. In Sydney, about
the same time, a man who stole a purse
on a tram-car got three years, and a
ruffian who horribly ill - used a child of
years and kicked its head with his
boots, was sentenced by a justly - infuri-
ated legal system to six months in gaol.
This, when set against Judge Gibson’s recent
sentence of 36 months for stealing nine-penny
worth of baking-powder, is a fair example
of the extreme cheapness of humanity as
against the extreme costliness of property-
even baking-powder —in the land of the free
and enlightened Cornstalk. According to the
N.S.W. brand of justice, it is just as awful an
act to embezzle l|d. worth of baking-powder
as to almost murder an infant; according to
the Victorian brand the same crime about
equals fd. worth of baking-powder, and is only
one-quarter as horrible as throwing a chew of
tobacco over the wall to brother Bill in gaol.
Therefore, if some person of misguided sym-
pathies throws tobacco over to the man who is
doing three months for breaking his child’s
arms, he will probably get four times as heavy
a punishment as that person himself. If these
were isolated instances they would merely cast
discredit on a casual, hog-like magistrate ; but
they are a part of the regular system, and it is
the law which is responsible. And as such
sentences as these are distributed every day, in
every Australian city, without arousing any
outbreak of righteous indignation, the final
responsibility rests on a hog-like community.
All over Australasia —in fact, all over Anglo-
Saxondom—the law deliberately recognises that
it is about 10 tim§s more awful to steal
from 6d. to 20s. worth of goods than to habitu-
ally torture a child or a woman almost to
death, or to kick a casual stranger to the
verge of murder—provided the latter crime
isn’t aggravated by taking threepence from the
victim. Anything short of murder, if done in
a casual, off-hand way, with boots or road-
metal or a beer-bottle, means something vary-
ing from 40s. fine to six months’ imprisonment;
though a comparatively mild assault, accom-
panied by the removal of threepence, is worth
at least five years. Moreover, the person who
is convicted for the second or third time of
stealing a few coppers gets a vastly increased
sentence, on the ground of “ previous convic-
tions,” but the individual who is brought up
for his second or third or tenth assault, gets
the same old 40s. or one month, or something
to the same effect. Even the most infuriated
society of old women of either sex never
demands that the brutal-assault person shall be
flogged—unless he aggravates his crime by
taking threepence. If the average sentence
which the average judge passes on the casual
thief is fair and justifiable, then a reasonable
allowance for the two persons mentioned above
would be 50 years’ imprisonment, mostly
“solitary.” The utter inability of the com-
munity to recognise the relative importance of
human life, as set against 9d. worth of baking-
powder, is one of the strangest problems of
civilisation.
Jubilee Drivel.
A quaint Victorian publication is the School
Paper, a record of things in general, which is
issued by the Government for the instruction
of State scholars. In this publication Minister
Peacock, the kookaburra of the Cabinet (who
specially commends this particular number
to the attention of teachers), grovels hard
for his prospective title and disseminates
Godsavethequeen among the rising veneration.
The last two numbers start with the Unnatural
Anthem, and are filled with pious whoop about
Victoria, and pictures of that monarch and all
her relatives, and statements about how she
once gave a copper to a beggar. It all leaves a
vague impression that Victoria personally con-
structed railroads, invented the locomotive and
the telegraph, built steamships, dug tanks,
colonised Australia, made the dirt-road over
the Blue Mountains, and buried the Duke of
Wellington. One particularly beautiful extract
(quoted from something written in a book by
“ Mrs. Tooley ”) :—
Queen Victoria is honored in the Courts of Europe as
no English monarch has been before. It seems, indeed,
that the Continent is rapidly coming under the sphere of
British influence through the alliances made by her child-
ren, grandchildren, and great-grandchildren. When
Russian Ministers propose any course of action likely to
upset the peace of Europe and bring England into the
fray, the young Czar and Czarina proudly reply, “ It can-
not be ; we cannot have Grandmamma worried.”
Peacock further states that “ no other monarch
of whom there is a record in history has reigned
so long,” which is rather rough on about eight
deceased kings that are too much decayed to
defend themselves. Probably Peacock didn’t
know any better. The School Paper is mostly
written in a shriek of superlatives, and its
general tenor is that for 60 years the British
nation has been in a constant state of joy be-
cause of something that Victoria was doing.
The nation was “ rejoiced ” to hear of her mar-
riage. She and Albert went on frequent tours,
“to the delight of the people.” “Joy was
expressed on every face ” when she opened the
Exhibition. The popular interest in Wales’s
marriage was “ unbounded,” and the people


“ showed their delight.” At her jubilee “ the re-
joicings were unparalleled in history ” —this is a
specially fine shriek, showing that the kookaburra
man has investigated all history back to the year
1, and knows exactly how glad everybody has
been on every possible occasion. Just here,
Peacock mentions proudly that matches were
recently invented—apparently by Victoria—
so that people have no longer to get a light
with a flint and steel when the baby screams in
the night. It is stated, at least four times,
that the Queen wept, and once that her eyes
were “swollen with tears, but great happiness
was shown in her countenance.” Peacock also
draws attention to the fact that Victoria was
the first British royal person to be vaccinated,
that she was hustled out of bed to hear of her
accession, and hadn’t time to dress herself ; and
that the blessed Anthem is a “ bond between
English-speaking people wherever throughout
the wide world they are to be found ; ” and a
whole article, with a big heading, sets forth
how Victoria once reproved a lady for being
10 minutes late. If this weird collection of
baby-drivel doesn’t bring Peacock a title, then
there is no gratitude in this world.
Thus the cable :
London, June 16.
A meeting of the Royal Colonial Institute was held last
night, at which Sir Edward Braddon, the Premier of Tas-
mania ; Mr. T. J. Byrnes, the Attorney-General of Queens-
land ; and Mr. F. S. Grimwade, M.L.C., Victoria, were
present. . . ~ .
Sir Edward Braddon protested against a double income
tax being imposed on colonists resident in England in re-
spect to their property in the colonies. He asserted that
Great Britain gained only £lBO,OOO annually from the tax,
and that the colonies would prefer a prompt concession
by Great Britain to wresting it from the Imperial Govern-
"'Mr. Byrnes strongly endorsed the remarks of Sir Edward
Braddon.
If Braddon thinks that Australia will inter-
fere on behalf of absentees in London he is quite
wrong—just as wrong as a Tasmanian Tory must
always naturally be. The only people who
worry about the double income-tax are the
absentees, and the Australian politicians and
others who eat at the absentees’ dinner-tables.
Sometime ago the Agents-General were induced
to deputationise the Chancellor of the Ex-
chequer, but he refused, to The Bulletin’s
great joy. The Bulletin now moves a vote
of thanks to the Chancellor for his action on
that occasion, and hopes he will do it again
some more, whenever the Braddons and the
Byrneses take up the cudgels for Australia’s
enemies—the absentees.

following:
(JONUK VHILATORY ADDRESS OF THE PAUPERS OP THE EM-
PIRE to Her Imperial Majesty on the Attainment
of a Royal Record. . .
Your Majesty,—We, your people, perhaps the majority
of vour people, called your children not only by writers
of 'poetry but on occasions by yourself, tender all we
possess, that is hope, at the foot of your throne and the
doors of your palaces. It has been said and many times
printed that your Majesty is the richest widow in the
world We deplore the widowhood and rejoice in the
riches. And now, in one great army of expectant misery,
we thank you in advance for the release of your accumu-
lated millions, which we know you are about to shower
on us in the Christian manner which your religion and pro-
fession render necessary to the safety of your immortal soul.
That you cannot take your treasure with you does not
render the gift less valuable. That your own palaces at
Cowes and Balmoral are about to be handed over and en-
dowed for the shelter of the poor of England and Scotland
does not diminish the pure, godly feelings which prompt
you so to do. Finally, your Majesty, our Mother, we
pray that the remainder of your earthly life may be
happy and its sequel heavenly, and we guarantee that
your children will never want for food, clothing, and
lodging such as we can never hope to obtain. Amen.
It is announced by cable that Arabi, the
Egyptian, will not be released from his long semi-
imprisonment in Ceylon by way of honoring the
jubilee. Did anyone suppose he would he ;
J. Bull reverences Garibaldi and Kosciusko
and Kossuth— the patriots who fought against
the other fellow. Arabi, the patriot who
fought against the invading Bull, is beyond
forgiveness. Matters have changed so com-
pletely that the Egyptian’s return to his
native land wouldn’t create a ripple. Ihe party
he led is nearly extinct; the order of things
which he knew is as dead as Barabbas. out
even the jubilee makes no difference in the case
of the old man who, years ago, when he was
still comparatively young, held Alexandria
against J. Bull. Probably Victoria’s personal
interference would do something for him, u
the Gracious Queen doesn’t forgive any more
than does Bull in general. Still, worse things
than imprisonment might happen to the ancient
soldier. He might be made a K.C.M.G., for
instance.
“ I NEVER warned a prisoner in my life,” said
detective Roche when barrister Edmunds en-
deavored to corner him over the loquacity of
Butler. Is it not time that Parliament fixed
the principle on which judicial authorities seem
to be so divided? Three or four N.S.W.
Judges hold that it is the duty of the detective,
or other public officer, to warn a prisoner that
any confession he makes may be used at the
trial against him, but as many others hold that
such a warning is unnecessary. In other parts
of Australia the same trouble is met with, and
generally over the whole region of trial by jury
Judges differ as to which is the proper practice
on such occasions. It is just m vital matters o
this kind that each country should fix its owi
criminal processes. Everyone can see the vast
difference that exists between mducnig or sed

self, and meeting his first incriminatorysyla e
with the official reminder that he taiks at his
peril. The inducement or seduction may con
sist of no overt act or verbal invitation, it
may be merely a real or simulated bonhomie or
camaraderie of behaviour. But if J
follow no fixed rule on the point detectives an
other policemen are left to make heir w
rule Hence it happens that there are pill
who always warn Zl police who never wa n.
Where the practice on so momentous a <tt
so varies Parliament should lay down a law to
guide all.

The British Tory Government has introduced
an Old Age Pensions Bill in the Commons. It
proposes to provide the ancient Briton, aged
over 65 and in low circumstances, with an in-
come of ss. a week, subject to certain cast-iron
regulations about respectability. This, at
least, is a great improvement on the old style of
Bumbleism, and, considering the vast number of
aged poor to be dealt with, it is at least a good
beginning. Ard, coming from the British Tory
party, it is very significant. It is the fashion
among the Fat Men of Austi’alia to weep about
Maoriland as a country “ ruined by Socialistic
legislation.” Yet Britain, under the control of
the old Church and State Party, is beginning to
run it very close in some respects. And what
becomes of the dreary jibe about Maoriland’s
Socialistic policy being the natural and disas-
trous result of government by the Labor-party,
when the Marquis of Salisbury and the Duke
of Devonshire, and Digger Dick Seddon and
boiler-maker Jenkinson are all more or less on
the same road !
“ The jury ought to agree; if there is no agree-
ment the jury has not discharged its duty,”
said the N.S.W. Chief Justice in the recent
Norton libel-case. In the name of all that is
wonderful, wrought the jury to agree ? When
three Judges sit on the Bench to form a Full
Court, they often disagree —probably as often
as not. They dissent about points of law,
which is a matter they are supposed to know
thoroughly ; and if three Judges can’t be of one
mind about their own special subject why
should they arrogantly demand that twelve jury-
men should agree about a subject of which they
have no special knowledge? The Judges dis-
agree about law, morals, politics, religion,
science ; they get their hair cut differently ;
have different modes of amusement; construe
the same Act in a dozen different ways ; give
widely-different sentences for the same offence ;
and some of them believe in ghosts while others
don’t; also, you never see one Judge walking
down street with another Judge. And the
only thing they agree about is that for a jury
to disagree is something awful. And they are
not quite agreed even about that.
Frederick Lagii, of Hawthorn, Melbourne, is
a victim of the local diphtheria scare. He luns
a sausage-factory, and the police pried into the
mysteries of his profession one day last week,
and subsequently said things in court deroga-
tory to the character of Lagh’s bolognas, and
tending to bring his common or kitchen sausage
into disrepute. The police found Lagh s
sausages hanging out to ripen over heaps of
ancient manure in a shed, and other sausages
and implements were associated with manure
in other places. In fact, there appeared to be
tnanureevery where, and here and there were fires
on top of it, smoking large, plump, luscious Ger-
mans. There were also much filth and many stinks
about the coppers and other weapons of trade,
and the place was infested with fatted rats of
great ferocity. On the whole, the inspection of
Lagh’s premises has destroyed confidence in
the local sausage, and made the bologna a by-
word. Perhaps the choicest item in the news-
paper report is the sausage-man’s naive plea.
“ The defendant said he had only recently ar-
rived from Germany, and did not know he had
committed any offence.” What with pestilent
dairies and rotten abattoirs, and stinks, and
stomach - turning etceteras, Melbourne’s food-
supply is so ghastly a thing to think of that
sensitive people will soon have to take their
meals under chloroform.

The recent attack by M.L.O. barristers on the
N.S.W. Taxation Commissioners, in connection
with their appointment of Mr. Lockhart as
their solicitor, savors strongly of professional
jealousy. The idea that such an important de-
partment can get all the law it wants for .£350
a year from a common or garden solicitor must
be gall and wormwood to the higher branch of
the profession. On the score of convenience,
economy, and efficiency, probably the Commis-
sioners took a proper step, though they went
about it in an irregular way. All the
big English and Scotch railway com-
panies haye a special solicitor, like the N.S. V .
Railway Solicitor, and the iN.S.W. Lands
Department is always represented by the
same barrister. And them three legal members
of the Council-Attorney-Genera Want, K. L.
O’Connor, and Heydon, all Q.C. s, and all le-
sponsible for this slovenly piece of legislation -
fall foul of the Commissioners for dropping into
the trap laid for them by their legal critics.
There is no want of legal criticism after an Act
is passed—it takes, on an average, seven years
to construe new legislation.
The N.S.W. Registrar-General sends along a
book containing some really cheerful informa-
tion. According to this volume, one child out
of every ten born in Sydney and its suburbs in
1895 was illegitimate, and one child out ot
every twenty in the country districts. The
result is that one out of every fifteen Corn-
stalk infants of the ’95 crop was born out of
wedlock. Seventeen years earlier the propor-
tion was one out of every twenty-five, so ortho-
dox morality is not improving. Light unlawful
mothers were aged 14 ; 38 were years of age ;
68 had passed their sixteenth birthday ; 148
were just over 17, and 220 were just over 8.
Then 230 were between 19 and 20, ana after
that the numbers at each age decline
rapidly, though no less than 117 mothers were
aged between 25 and 26. Further, one un-
married mother gave way to her wild, youthful
indiscretion at the age of 501 so it seems
doubtful where the “age of consent” should
be fixed. Apparently, about 26 is the right
place, though there are grave doubts if even
this is sufficient. Marriages seem to be getting
out of fashion ; in 1886 there were 12 weddings
for every 1000 people in Sydney and suburbs ;
in 1895 there were only 8. Births are also grow-
ing fewer ; in 1886 Sydney had 43 children
born for every 1000 people living; in 1895
there were only 30. Deaths are decreasing
likewise. The net result seems to be that mar-
riages are growing fewer, and births less
numerous, and that through the decrease in
deaths N.S.W. is getting to be much more a
country of old men and old women than it used
to be. In fact, barring the circumstance that
illegitimacy keeps up vigorously, and that in
1895 nearly three times as many unlawful off-
spring were born as in 1886, almost everything
seems to be decreasing. Amid so much solemn
decrease it is almost cheering to note that some-
thing is going ahead.
Editor Bulletin,— Parson Fordyce, “on be-
half of the Council of the N.S.W. Churches,”
keeps on insisting, with an exceeding great
I emphasis, that Cod shall be recognised in the
preamble to the new Federal Constitution. And
on July 4th a big preachment to that text is to
resound from Australian pulpits. Without a
preamble-recognition, duly endorsed by Fordyce
and Co., God will be set at nought and con-
temned ! Christ, whose teachings the Churches
profess to follow, once asked the parsons of the
people, who in preambles did a pretty con-
siderable bit of CoD-recognition, “ Why call ye
me Lord, Lord, and do not the things which I
say ? ” By all means let us have God recog-
nised —in the enactment of righteous laws, in
national effort to make it hot for the sweater,
the boodler, and the religious humbug. But
recognition of God in a preamble , with the
boodler, the sweater, and the religious humbug
not vigorously warred against, but (as at pre-
sent) quasi-connived at by the “ Council of the
Churches,” would be calculated to give fresh
point to that tremendous denunciation: —
“ Well hath Esaias prophesied of you, hypo-
crites : This people honored me with their lips,
but their heart is far from me.”—J.S.
It is easy to find a feasible-looking reason for
the unholy faith in the lash so consistently
displayed by a certain Australian Judge. His
Honor was an amateur pugilist in his pre-
judicial days ; a patron of the murderous-look-
ing “Chicken” who smashes his adversary’s
jaw, and administers damnable punishment to
his adversary’s stomach for a 425 purse. His
Honor in his time has provided the inducement
for two broken-nosed beasts to baiter one
another. The lust for personal violence is in
his blood to almost as great an extent probably
as the lust for women is in the blood of the
undisciplined young men whom he turns over
to the dogger. If any reader doubts that that
Judge has encouraged in himself and others the
brutal instincts common to us all, let the
doubter acquaint himself with the class of
“ sport ” that his Honor used to revel in, and
then let the doubter tell The Bulletin whether
any admirer of “Chickens '’ should have con-
trol over a public instrument of torture?
Magistrate Panton, of Melbourne, estimates
that “ not more than one per cent, of the persons
committed to gaol for vagrancy are ‘ deserving,’
in the sense of being free from criminal taint,
and guilty of nothing worse than poverty.”
The estimate seems absurdly low, and, anyway,
Mr. Panton forgot to suggest that criminal
taint in many of these cases may have been
acquired through a long acquaintance with
abject poverty. It is so easy for deserving un-
employed to drift into the outer circle of the
criminal classes. This is a bad world. Mr.
Panton, at his leisure, might prepare an esti-
mate of the percentage of frock-coated agents
and financiers in the Collins-street district who
are undeserving, in the sense of being infected
with land-boom roguishness, and guilty of
something worse than jubilee grovel.
Brisbane (Q.) Courier, which is urging
Queensland to join the Australian Federation
Convention, by means of TozER-selected dele-
gates, said editorially, the other day, that
“perhaps” North Queensland “is the only
division of tropical Australia destined to sustain
in health and prosperity a numerous European
community.” Which appears to be a sly and
cowardly way of hinting to Northern Wes-
tralia and the Northern Territory that Queens-
land will wink at their keeping the back and
side doors of Australia open to the black, brown,
and yellow men. Curiously, too, it was in an
editorial especially glorifying Cairns that this
statement occurred—Cairns, that is under the
heel of the Yellow Man. Cairns gets a large
proportion of iU smuggled-in Chinese from
Port Darwin, per jog-trot overland. Butthere
are worse cooliephiles than the Courier. Theie
are many Kanakaland politicians who jar-
gonise that the Australian Federation must
conserve full “State rights,” and that Queens-
land must have power to employ coolie labor
for its tropical area, or that area will be doomed !
Brisbane Telegraph— some of whose staff seem
kept as near as possible on the level of coolie-
laborers—is naturally the spokesman of that
breed of politicians.

During the 60 years of the record reign, accord-
ing to a contemporary of Her Gracious,
“ morals, in the main, have vastly improved ” ;
the number of convicts has enormously de-
creased ; the treatment of lunatics has become
humane and rational ; the condition of woman
has been greatly ameliorated ; “ drunkenness
has ceased to be good style; the nation lias
become comparatively clean ot mind and body ;
and it would be difficult to find a man or
woman in society who is not connected with
some endeavor to better the condition of
others.” And all this, be it remembered, side-
by-side with the “lamentable decay of the
religious spirit” (Anglican bishop) —the “alarm-
spread of infidelity” (Baptist parson)—the
“rapid growth of the new paganism ” (Catholic
cardinal). What escape is there from the con-
clusion that creed is the curse of practical
religion ?
The Temporary Insanity of a
Nation.
A SLICE OF FUTURE HISTORY.
[Foe The Bulletin.]
“ Towards the middle of 1897, when it became
probable that the Queen-Empress's reign would
exceed in duration even that of her grandfather,
the w T ish suitably to commemorate the occasion—
reasonable enough in itself- became intensified,
through a certain ignobility of the national
character, into a factitious enthusiasm little short
of insanity. The ‘ Record-Reign ' —as, in the
sporting-jai’gon of the time, it came to be called —
exploited, as they had never been exploited before,
the depths of British vulgarity and bad taste.
It presented to the world a display of puerility,
snobbery and servility never paralleled even
in the decadence of that ancient empire with
which it became at this time a literary common-
Elace advantageously to compare tbe British.
hakespeare—even in that age, strange to say, in
some degree appreciated—had, two centuries
before, hit off exactly the madness of the time
with his ‘ Fantastic tricks before high heaven.’
“ The nation, in a word, had intoxicated itself
with a sentiment which it was pleased to call
‘loyalty.’ Dispassionately considered from a
distance of three centuries, it appears to have
been but the joint product of personal ineptitude,
national self-conceit, and despicable reverence for
a distinction which was in this sovereign’s case so
entirely adventitious that it is difficult to note
without a smile, amongst other imbecile conceits,
it was actually proposed to confer upon her the
popular agnomen of ‘Great.’ Even the sugges-
tion to label her ‘Good,’ though less preposter-
ously foolish, had no adequate basis in the fact
that —like thousands of her female subjects—she
had brought up a large family respectably, and
had never committed any breach of the conven-
tional proprieties. For no greater reason than
these, nevertheless, it was with damnable itera-
tion insisted upon that, in the words of her official
panegyrist, she had ‘ worn the white flower of a
blameless life in that fierce light which beats upon
a throne.’
“ That Victoria would, in any case, have suc-
ceeded in conforming to the not very exacting
standai’d of virtue aforesaid, there is no reason to
doubt, but it does not seem to have occurred to
tho eulogist in question—a graceful rhymester
enough—that the completeness of the illumination
he refers to was certainly not favorable to any laxity
of conduct. Further, tho public opinion of the
period was so smugly sanctimonious as to
render perfectly futile the comparisons which
were at the time cheaply and frequently insti-
tuted, as regards ostensible morality, between
Victoria’s court and that of the king who made
the record reign of France. The morals of Louis
XIV. were the morals of his age, and the morals
of Victoria were the morals —real or pretended—
of hers.
“Great, therefore, in no sense of the one word,
and Good only in the negative signification of the
other—Victoria was not even personally, at this
time, of a type to inspire any shadow, of the
chivalrous enthusiasm to which an illogical but
generous instinct has, at times, impelled whole
nations whose female sovereigns had a life-
flower very far indeed from immaculate ‘white-
ness.’ At no time remarkable for personal attrac-
tions or for sweetness of disposition—the Queen
was, when this epidemic of spurious fervor set the
angels a-weeping, of an extremely acerb and
capricious temper, and of a presence, generally,
which could not by any stretch of the imagination
be described as imperial.
“ Nor, when we turn to the events of her long
incumbency, do we find much justification for the
fan fa ronnade thereanent which dominated even
the slavishly personal element of the national
craze. The scientific progress of the 60 years, in-
deed—inconsiderable though it may seem to us —
may pass muster : it was progress, at all events ;
but in matters intellectual, sociological, and indus-
trial, the 60th year was, in many respects, no whit
of an advance upon the first.
“ The Deity was still supposed to be continually
reconsidering His own arrangements, and tinker-
ing in an intermittent and erratic kind of way
with the natural sequence of events, and was still
blasphemously besought to bless to Britain tho
results of her peddling little, wars of Bible and
grab, and ‘ civilisation ’and extermination. The
soldier’s profession was still looked upon as in
some mysterious way more honorable than the
hangman’s, and the hangman himself was still in
frequent requisition for the punishment of murder
on a small scale, and even- in that part of the
present Australian Republic- which appears to have
been then oddly called New South Wales—of
offences due to that sexual form of insanity which
is now dealt with like any other description of
mania. In that corner of what was then the Em-
pire, also, the possibility of curing brutality with
brutality was still so firmly believed in that the
lash was in habitual use as a moral agent !
“In England itself the most wanton and inso-
lent luxury still flourished side-by-side with a
misery and degradation too abject and horrible to
be easily comprehended by us of the 22nd century.
While hundreds of thousands of pounds were
paid, in the aggregate, for the occupancy for
an hour of vantage-points from which to gape at
the tawdry celebration of the fact that a rnoroso
old lady and her swarm of relatives had fattened'
upon the peo le for 60 years—there could have
been collected from the self-same city a hundred
thousand pen-sons who were absolutely striving —
and very often failing—merely to keep body and
soul together! ‘Let us compare this pageant,’
says a writer of the time, ‘ with a triumphal pro-
cession of Old Rome, and in tbe latter we shall
find, behind the victor’s ear, a multitude of
chained and sullen prisoners, destined to slavery
or death. But in the new World's Capital of our
happier age it will be no throng of doomed and
dejected captives who will form the long array,
but glad and loyal representatives of that won-
drous assemblage of free peoples that form the
British Empire.’ No '.—but the doomed and tho
dejected were not far to find. Swarming like
rabbits in the rotten houses of the dark and filthy
courts whereinto neither hope nor the sun ever shed
a ray. Crowded like pig’s litters—men, women,
youths and girls together—into miserable dens
where common decency was impossible and sexual
degradation a certainty. Condemned, amidst the
flaunting abundance of that ‘ happier age,' to such
a hell of existence that only the blind instinct of
self-preservation could have prevented most of
them from removing themselves with their own
hopeless hands from the list of ‘glad and loyal
representatives of the free peoples that form tho
British Empire.’
“ And, that Empire now ! If the cheap enthu-
siasts who maundered forth thus comparisons
with Rome had not cut short the parallel where it
suited them, they might have remembered that
efflorescence heralds always decay. They might
perhaps have understood that in all this pomp and
splendour and luxury and arrogance lurked tin-
beginning of the end, and that the British Empire
—like the Roman, monstrously over-grown -
also like the Roman, about to fall to pieces le
own weight 1 ”
