Alexandra “The Grand Duchess.”
Maggie Moore, just returned from the
land of the spread eagle and the huckleberry pie,
Opened her season on Saturday at Sydney Royal.
There was a large audience, and considerable
applause, and much blossom streamed across the
footlights. Nevertheless, “ Johanna ” isn’t a pro-
duction which calls for great enthusiasm. It is a
mixture of the songand-dance show and the four-
aet horror ; a species of giddy frivol, like the “ Trip
to Chinatown ” without the evening-dress or the
glitter, and with a good deal of the humor taken
out and the dead body of a pawnbroker inserted
m its stead. A thread of plot runs through the
song and jump aspect of the play ; in between the
music it keeps trannpiring that everybody is some-
body's long-lost brother or daughter or father ; or
somebody bursts in to 1 tell that the pawnbroker
has been found dead with his head resting on the
unredeemed pledges. Said pawnbroker, it seems,
lent £6 on Johanna when she was a damp, hungry
infant, and dragged her up by hand ; and for ten
years she danced and tore things in TJncle Harrises
premises by day I( and listened to the interest accu-
mulating in the night. Then -by a loud explosion
of circumstances she becomes maid to a lady
whose uncle is mortgaged to Uncle H., and wins
the heart of everybody by breaking dishes all
over the premises. Later on, it transpires that her
new employer is her father, only her mother took
her away in infancy and threw her in the river for
reasons which don’t appear. It is also explained
that all her troubles ancTher father’s troubles and
the anguish of pawnbroker Harris arose through a
baleful clerk who has all his life been writing
letters to everybody with everybody else’s signa-
ture on them, is away just then murder-
ing Harris. Why he wrote the letters is a
mystery, and his purpose in murdering Harris re-
mains in profound gloom. A good deal of horror
arises out of somebody, not defined, having led
away a wife who remains unaccounted for, and
done something with her that is not revealed.
This lady is understood to be buried all over the
drama, and when Uncle Harris thinks of her he
goes mad and writes out cheques at a dreadful
rate.
* * * •
These matters, loosely interspersed with
songs about “Sister Mary Jane’s top-note,” and
other cheerful irrelevancies, are about all there is
of “ Johanna.” Miss Moore plays that stout and
frivolous waif for all she is worth, and sings a
great deal in a voice which was heard on Satur-
day at its best; and Harry Roberts fills another
irresponsible part a great deal better than it
deserves. The giddy mirthfulness of this pair,
the revels they get up on their own, the crockery
wre °k» an d the bright, off-hand manner in
which they pervade things generally, keep
V °hauma ” going. The other people, barring
Miss I itzmaurice Gill, who is always interesting,
and Miss Laura Roberts, are a lugubrious crowd
with forged cheques, and anonymous letters, and
lost wives, and the pawnbroker’s dead body
weighing on their minds. J. P. West and John-
son Weir are the best among these serious-minded
people. The others have tons of woe on their
heads, and come to an end which is not too well-
defined at rather a late hour.
** * *
The Potter-Bellew. farewell season of one
week commences at Her Majesty’s on Saturday
with “Romeoand Juliet.” Tuesday and Wednes-
day are devoted to the “Merchant of Venice,”
and “ Francillon,” a play in which Mrs. Potter ’is
probably better liked and will be more kindly re-
membered than any other, fills in the rest of the
week. Cecil Ward and the other necessary
materials to make up a good company have been
engaged for the occasion. Meanwhile “ The Sign
of the Cross ” moves Melbourne-wards. This play
has been so great a’ success that a second co. is
being formed to take r it around the smaller towns
with Scot Inglis as Marcus Superbus. By way of
an introductory canter the new Marcus was
announced to replace Julius Knight-in the part on
Tuesday night, but' the exigencies of a paper
which has to go to press on Tuesday and is already
considerably disarranged by uncalled-for Jubila-
tion among the staff, make it impossible to say
how the new Roman got on. Nevertheless, this
paper backs Scot- Inglis to make Caesar sit up
brilliantly 1840 years ago, and to scare that pam-
pered party off his purple dais if need be.
** * #
The Brough farewell benefit at the Lyceum
was a great event. “ Dandy Dick ” was the bill
for the occasion, and it was played respectably
though the absence of Dot Boucicault was felt a
good deal, and Cecil Ward’s disappearance left a
great gap in the programme. But the flaring
head of Brough’s policeman was still an oriflamme
m the front of the battle, and with Mrs. Brough
and Titheradge in their old parts nothing very bad
could possibly-happen. The audience was a gor-
geous affair, and the cash receipts are said to have
been something beyond the dreams of avarice ;
and as the committee wisely sat on Dibbs and
kept him down as an ordinary committee-man
and put up Edmund Barton to make the farewell
speech, all was well. Barton’s speech was not
especially brilliant, but it was neat and to the
point, and Brough’s reply was at least moderately
successful, and the patent glass tear of sympathy
was dropped at exactly the right place by every-
body, So the Brough co. disappears, so far as
Sydney is concerned, and as it gets away just in
time to avoid the illuminations its luck was in to
the last.
Edward Farley struck the truly brilliant
idea of putting up “Maritana” for the holiday
week. The absolute freshness of “ Maritana,”
and its dazzling novelty, and the utter impos-
sibility of anyone ever having seen it before, are
so obvious that this amounted to a stroke of
genius. Rickards also jubilated in a plain, or-
dinary way with the Selbinis as his chief attrac-
tion. The Tivoli, however, is so full at ordinary
times that it would be mere irony to put up a
special holiday bill with a view to making it any
fuller. So the Selbinis were only a little madder
than usual on their bikes, and rode around on one
spoke, and the loose-legged comedian was a trifle
looser and more boneless than his ordinary bone-
lessness, to the satisfaction of everybody.
** * *
Miss Elsie Adair’s season was advertised
to commence at Sydney Criterion on Wednesday,
and all the nice things which her case calls for
will be duly set forth in next week’s issue.
** * *
At time of writing, however, most of the
Sydney amusements consisted of “V.R.,” seen
dimly through a drizzle of rain and a breath of
beer. In all the great illuminated city there was
hardly anything in the way of inscriptions except
“ V.R.,” and occasionally “God Bless our Queen.’
A great deal of British flag and transparency, and
miscellaneous illumination, could be seen, but in
the way of legible observation hardly anybody
got beyond “ Y.R.’’ The want of originality and
eloquence, and flow of soul, among the people who
combusted on that occasion was painful to con-
template. The Bulletin had an idea of striking
a different note by illuminating its office-front with
the name of the paper, and a statement that it was
still a Republican organ and that the price was
6d. or £1 a year in advance to subscribers within
N.S.W., and that the Xmas edition would put up
a record, and then breaking off short without any
“V.R.”atall; but, after all, it hardly seemed
worth the trouble. By the time there is another
Record Reign, The Bulletin proprietor will pro-
bably be a duke or au alderman, and a huge plush
flunkey will stand behind him while he edits the
paper in a room with a Turkey carpet on the floor,
and his coronet will habitually repose in the ink-
stand, and then it will be as “V.”as its neigh-
bors, and perhaps more wildly “ R.” than any of
them.
#* * *
“ My Friend from India” seems to have
braced up the Paulton-Stanley season at Melb.
Princess’s, therefore the approaching departure of
the short man and the long lady promises to be a
sorrowful event. Paulton will prowl around in
his yellow gown until Tuesday next (29th), after
which two more revivals of “ Lelamine ” and Lord
Shaftesbury. In the impossible event of Her
Gracious Majesty creating Paulton a jubilee dook
this arrangement might be altered, no doubt,
because a cheerful, farcical comedy with a duke in
the cast would have more charms for society than
the sick opera aforesaid. And “My Friend from
India ”is very cheerful. It tails off considerably
in the last act, but at its weakest moment the
members of George Lauri’s family come forward,
one at a time, and prove the influence of mind
over matter by bouncing their old man into the
belief that he is hopelessly drunk, whereupon the
deluded victim staggers up a flight of steps and
saves the situation.
** * *
“A Life of Pleasure” is still doing its
duty by the Bland Holt exchequer, at Melb.
Royal. The bitter curse of forsaken Norah con-
tinues to descend upon Albert Norman when he
bids her take up Sin as a profession ; likewise
Miss Watson persists in smashing a champagne
glass every evening, and this reckless destruction
of property suggests questions as to whether
the glassware is new goods, or cracked when
it comes to table, and whether the smasher
has to provide her own glass, or pay for it
out of her salary like the average Norah, in
whose domestic life in the kitchen there
is no pleasure worth mentioning. Bland Holt’s
dramas are full of food for conjecture. Where
does he raise the loan of those heathen supers who
drop dead whilst the British Army is firing into
space, and what would happen to the horse and
rider if they were jointly to fall down the chasm,
and why doesn’t Charlie Brown placate his trucu-
lent voice with a jujube ? All these are matters to
ponder over between the acts.
** * *
At Melb. Bijou, last week, the A. A. Opera
Club, under James Ure’s direction, gave a charity
performance (three times) of “The Gondoliers,”
which appeared quite professional by comparison
with rougher amateur treatments of Gilbert and
Sullivan, and yet quite amateurish, in everything
save stage-management, when judged by the
highest W. and M. standard. None of the princi-
pals—who included at least two ex-pros., and
were mostly well-seasoned in their parts—shaped
absolutely feebly, whereas the majority of them
both sang and acted, or acted if they couldn’t sing
in good style. Notable successes were George
Tutton, a mellifluous, nimble tenor, with feet that
have not diminished since he last shone in public •
a promising comedian named Lambcroft as the’
Duke, and Miss S. Broughton, who came along at
the last moment in the grey wig of the Fos-
ter - Mother. A buxom Casilda was per-
haps somewhat less satisfactory than several
others, but she didn’t amount to a matter
for regret. Having said these nice things, The
Bulletin feels no compunction in remarking that
operatic amateurs, as a rule—and this performance
was no exception to the rule—are a pictorial
failure. The men have been cast by Nature in
the bank-clerk mould, and the girls, even the dear
girls, are not more striking than their female
relatives at large. After careful inspection of
sundry commonplace faces and unimportant
figures m The Gondoliers” chorus, a cynic would
be naturally disposed to wonder why an all-ruling
Providence is so apt to scatter stage-fever germs
upon the least suitable kind of virgin soil.
ife
“The Grand Duchess” struggles gamely
at the Alexandra, with justifiable hope of smiting
an unsympathetic public a good, hard knock upon
the jaw. In other words, Howard Vernon is
giving the merits of his show a chance to get
talked about. It has several merits in addition to
Miss Fanny Liddiard, and, if that adorable lead-
ing lady were the only good point in the pro-
ceedings, she would be full value for the audience s
money, anyhow. Miss Liddiard, who is said to
have played the name part a hundred times in
America, puts vivacity, warmth and just enough
of French spice into her work. Howard Vernon
doesn’t fit his tenor character, vocally or otherwise,
but he is always an amusing comedian, whilst
Miss Nellie Young, neat, slim, graceful, and
a pleasant singer, is an admirable Prince Paul.
Now that Florence Young has gone ’Ome,
sister Millie may as well receive credit for being
the brighter artist of the pair. The pantomimic
thirsters for Vernon’s blood get through credit-
ably, and the four maids of honor, who warble a
verse a-piece in the second act, are a particular
success. Harcourt Lee, as the musical head of
the show, is a guarantee of steady improvement
in the orchestra and chorus. Produced at any
other theatre, “The Grand Duchess ” would have
shown a profit for three or four weeks. Industry
and perseverance may possibly reclaim the Alex-
andra swamp for the present company. They will
try “ Rip Van Winkle ” on Saturday, unless busi-
ness has started to flourish in the meantime.
** * *
The Rickards variety programme at Melb.
Opera House is embellished with a special Jubilee
first-part to celebrate the record reign of a Person-
age who shall here be nameless, because she has
received far too much free advertisement already.
The Haytors, Allisons, Bellman and Moore, and
the Pickaninnies are appearing until further
notice, and Philip Newbury’s robust tenor was to
start creating a furore on Monday night. Philip
bursts upon the scene in the character of a Jubilee
firework.
** * *
The Bulletin has previously mentioned
that a Diamond Fete (ye gods !) at Melb. Exhi-
bition Building will commemorate the aforesaid
Person’s tenacious grip of existence. The fete is
to happen on Friday and Saturday. On Satur-
day evening, too, Mrs. Andrews will pinch the
Jubilee’s tail, so to speak, with a Charles Dickens
recital at the Athenteum Hall. Many other enter-
tainments, including a monster procession of
loyalists on bikes, are to express public joy and
gratitude at about the same time. The Waxworks,
for instance, has a pair of giant sisters on view,
and those huge masses of flesh will jubilate, if
possible. What with one thing and another, this
is a terrible week to talk about.

The Howard Conservatoire of Music an-
nounces a series of four subscription concerts, to
be given at the Elsternwick (Melb.) Hall between
now and December next. The first concert is
fixed for Monday (28th), and by way of attracting
public attention to an excellent musical enterprise,
Governor Brassey, likewise Lady Brassey and
suite, will assemble in the front seats. Governor
8., under the excitement of Shaftesbury’s operatic
outbreaks, seems quite likely to go in for the big
drum, or some other important instrument, at an
early date.
At Poverty Point.
Frank Lincoln, the well-beloved maker of sound-and-
fury cocktails, is at present doing- Maoriland. A new
story of his : —I was entertaining (or thought I was) in
North Carolina one night, when a man in squeaky boots
got up and walked down the hall towards the door. This
annoyed me, and started everybody off laughing, so I
thought I would get even with him. As he reached the
doorway I called out to him : “ You must be the doctor
of this town, that you have to leave in such a hurry?”
“ No,’ he called back, “ I’m the undertaker, and business
is brisk now that you’ve arrived.”
When Charles Godfrey was benefited in Perth, W.A., at
the beginning of the month, on the rising of the curtain
before a full house a doctor’s certificate was read to the
effect that the beneficiary had been stricken with para-
lysis. When poor Godfrey’s “ turn ” arrived he was
seated in a chair, and tried to sing “ Half-past Nine ” but
failed in the attempt.

Two of Howard Vernon’s offspring—a son and a
daughter—are showing in the cotnic opera at Melb
Alexandra They are both very young, and the bov
(Richard Prince) is very promising. Already he fools like

Melbourne nowadays probably contains a larger per-
centage of capable amateur singers and dancers than any
other city in the world. The climate accounts for the
voices, and the rival training schools run by Mrs. Green
and the Misses Hyams are turning out dozens of re-
spectable stage-dancers every year.

Dear Bulletin,—lf you want a creepy sensation, next
time you are near a kinetoscope get the operator to
reverse the photos, and run them through the machine.
Take a man smoking a cigar : watch the ash get from the
ground to his hand ; then watch him smoke the cigar
while all the time it gets bigger and bigger, till, finally, a
complete cigar is returned to his pocket. Or watch a
railway train passing. It steams away all right, the
wheels can be seen to move in the right direction, yet it
ultimately slows down and pulls up at its starting point.
It gave me a sort of “ lost ” feeling, somewhat similar to
what I experienced when I first saw a phonograph, on a
table in a lecture-hall, and heard the diabolical wooden-
box speak- to me.—F.C.
Letter received by an actor in New York:—“Jan. 4,
1897. Mr. Henry Miller, Garden Theatre. —Dear Sir, —You
are probably aware that nowadays it is sensation and not
talent that wins. As you are to make your first stellar
appearance in New York, it is almost necessary that you
do something to attract attention, and I have a scheme to
propose. On Sunday night, 10th January, your house
will be entered by burglars. They will turn the place
upside down, and upon discovery pistol-shots will be
fired. They will escape, leaving blood-stains on the floor.
You will get the credit of fighting single-handed two
desperate robbers. The New York Herald's and other
morning daily police reporters will get the story, and the
whole town will be talking about you. I will furnish the
burglars and take all chances, and will only charge you
one hundred dollars for the scheme. I can refer you to a
dozen managers. I am the author of the runaway scheme
used by ‘ The Sporting Duchess ’ last week, and I got
them the dead horse, which we put in the alley for the
reporters to look at. Address, Empire Press Association,
General Delivery, Post Office.”
“How few of the stage-stricken,’ writes a Bulletin
correspondent, “and especially how few of the stockinged
sex, ever show any humor or originality in their bill-
names. So many Montmorencies and Monlagues and
Capulets crowd the floor, that it is quite a high relief to
find a Miss Ellas Dee playing the apprentice in “ La
Poupbe.” There s a fortune in store for the man who
will calmly adopt the stage-name of John Smith. That
would stick out in a playbill like the shadow of a great
land on a weary rock.
Norfolk : “ Emmett, a good ventriloquist of the show
order, now appears in Melb. with Miss Adair, but I don’t
think we ever had a real one in public, the genuine Val.
Vox. Where I came from, in England, there was one, a
clergyman named Stewart. He stood in a crowd and
made groans come from inside a wall, so that it was
pulled down.”
S.S. : “The five black coons at Rickards’ Melb. show
are deeply interesting ; they are undeniably human docu-
ments. Their singing may' evoke a supercilious snort
from the superior person, and I have been told that their
dancing' is not ‘ art, but ten minutes of these niggers is
as good as all the descriptive stuff about happy planta-
tion life in ‘ Uncle Tom’s Cabin.’ These melodies for the
most part were never made, they ‘ just growed,’ and are
wonderfully suggestive of the farm-yard, and the corn-
ticld, and de ole banjo.’ Their dancing, too, has come
down from the slavery days, and is full of the spirit of
American humor, which owes not a little to the nigger.
Really, if the plantation slave had nearly as bad a time of
it as Mrs. Beecher-Stowe would have us believe how
account for these rollicking, fantastical dances and’these
quaint melodies in the blood of the American darkey of
to-day ? ”

The WILD CAT
COLUMN
According to Coghlan’s newly-published figures,
the slump in N.S.W. property goes on as gaily as ever
The only figures available are the values of land and build-
ings in municipalities, and though the municipally-
governed area is steadily increasing and new buildings
continue to go up more or less, these have declined from
£151,000,000 in 1895—the year when values reached their
maximum—to £127,000,000 in 1897, a fall of £24,000,000
in two years. In Sydney, property reached its highest
total value in 1892, and then began to decline slowly. In
the suburbs it was at its maximum in 1893, after which
the slump set in, though at first very gradually. But
until 1895 the steady rise in the value of country munici-
palities more than counterbalanced the decline of Sydney
and its surroundings, and it was only in 1896 and ’97 that
everything began to go down together with a dismal
crash. The great fall was practically coincident with the
beginning of Freetrade ; since that policy came into vogue
£24,000,000 of cornstalk wealth has evaporated and left
not a trace behind. This doesn’t necessarily prove that
Freetrade caused the evaporation, but it does prove most
conclusively that it didn’t prevent it; and, as the Free-
trade party promised effusively that it was going to restore
prosperity, the result is highly interesting. As regards the
two instalments of the slump, it appears that £15,000,000
disappeared in 1896 and £9,000,000 in 1897. Whether
there will be another big falling off next year time only
can show ; anyhow, the return of prosperity hasn’t begun
yet, and so far the progress is still downwards.
A point on which the Reid Government whooped
especially was that its policy had made a stupendous and
wonderful increase in the shipping trade of N.S.W. Up
to the end of 1896, anyhow, the statement seems to be
a gratuitous fiction. In 1896 the vessels which entered and
left all N.S.W. ports represented 6,189,760 tons—an in-
crease of 329,000 tons on the record for 1895. In four out
of the last ten years a larger increase was shown, so the
improvement is nothing remarkable. Even the ship-
building trade doesn’t seem to hare boomed much ; the
total number of vessels built in N.S.W. in 1896 was 15,
and although this is better than 1894 and ’95, it is far
behind the 1891 record, when 40 vessels were constructed.
• * » •
The Queensland 3 per cent, loan of J>1,500,000
floated in London at £97 Is. 5d., so that, after deducting
expenses, the province will probably get about £95 10s.
Discounts on loans are one of the unconsidered losses of
the borrowing system. In the last nine years Queensland
has sold bonds to the amount of £11,197,134, and they
have realised in cash £10,413,410, so it has made a dead
loss of £783',724 on these transactions.
• • * •
At the end of 1895 Sydney and suburbs were
insured against fire for £59,720,000. Of this £25,500,000
was done with English and other companies from outside
Australasia, and £36,000,000 was done with companies
from outside N.S. Wales.
• * * •
The report of the Maoriland State Life Insur-
ance Department for 1896 is a particularly good one. The
renewaLpremiums are £230,324 against £230,052 last year,
and £219,307 the one before. New premiums and con-
sideration for annuities are £40,395, compared with
£35,980 and £34,897 for two previous years. Interest on
investments (£124,255) is £5OOO more than last year and
£lO,OOO more than the one previous. Among private
offices the A.M.P., Colonial Mutual, Mutual of Victoria,
and City Mutual all showed a decrease this year in their
revenue from interest —in other words the slump struck
their revenues so heavily that despite a large increase in
their invested funds the yield from interest and rents
diminished. The fact that the M.L. Government Depart-
ment shows an increase under this heading is proof that
its securities got through a particularly bad year unin-
jured. (The Mutual Life Association, National Mutual,
and Widows’ Fund are almost the only private offices
which can be quoted as having done likewise.) Out of a
total revenue of £394,984 the sum of £163,097 was added
to the insurance fund, making it £2,591,342 —the second
largest insurance fund in Australasia.
The department has taken many precautions to
keep itself absolutely solid. It writes off £29,500 for
depreciation, and carries £lO,OOO to reserve, which makes
£24,000 available for further depreciation. It also reduces
its assumed future rate of interest at one swoop from £4
to £3 12s. 6d. per cent., which necessitated providing a
reserve of £81,OIK). Out of £2,648,000 worth of assets
“properties acquired by foreclosure” represent only
£29,906, which shows that a remarkably small proportion
of the borrowers have failed to pay up. Also, with an
annual income of £124,000 from interest, only £760 is
overdue and uncollected. As a rule, the smaller the
amount of interest overdue the safer are the securities—
when interest becomes more or less irrecoverable it
generally means that the principal is getting into the
same state ; and no insurance office in Australasia shows
so good a record in this respect as the Maoriland State
Department. The investments yield within a fraction of
5 per cent., and, considering that the Department now
bases it calculations on receiving £3 12s. 6d. per cent.,
this is a sufficiently wide margin. One Australian office,
which last year only earned about 4)4 per cent., bases
its estimates on 4 per cent., and several Ameri-
can institutions earning 4£ or 4| per cent, also base
their calculations on the assumption that interest won’t
fall below 4 per cent., so the Department’s margin is very
liberal. Among the large private offices, so far as The
Bulletin is aware, only the A.M.P. and the Mutual Life
Association have made similar reductions in their esti-
mates. The Department’s expenses for 1896 absorbed
about 14 per cent, of its total income—a shade more
than last year—yet the lowest rate shown by any
office doing business in Australasia, the A.M.P. only ex-
cepted. After making the large reserves already men-
tioned, there remains £444,000 cash surplus for division
which, considering the unusually low premium rates
charged, is a very handsome amount. Altogether, the
Maoriland State Insurance office shows no weak points
anywhere, and its balance-sheet is a remarkably clear and
explicit document, which is so framed as to throw all
possible light on its affairs.
Apropos of insurance matters, two subjects on
which- The Bulletin desires to continue making itself
highly obnoxious are (1) the practice of not showing
separately properties acquired by foreclosure, and (2) that
of not -showing arrears of interest uncollected, as distin-
guished from interest accrued but not due. The first
item is essential to a proper understanding as regards the
safety of the mortgage investments ; when property is
abandoned to the society it generally means that the
owner doesn’t think it worth redeeming, and too much
foreclosed bricks and mortar is a significant feature. The
second is equally essential, for a huge mass of arrears of
interest is an asset that may be worth very little, and the
more arrears the more likelihood that a lot of the invested
money is irrecoverable. Among offices doing business in
Australasia, the A.M.P., Maoriland Government Depart-
ment M utua l Life Association, Australian Widows’ Fund,
and the Mutual of Victoria are honorable exceptions which
supply both these essential items of information. The
Citizens Co. (of Sydney) is explicit on the subject of
interest arrears only. The City Mutual is explicit in the
matter of foreclosed properties only. The National
Mutual, Temperance and General, New York Life,
Equitable of New York, and Colonial Mutual give
whatever upon either point, and
the advisability of a change cannot be too strongly in-
sisted upon. Moreover, there is always the possibility
that just as much arrears of interest will be shown as the
management thinks advisable, and the balance —if it is
too large and awful to be revealed —judiciously hidden
away by lumping it in with the mortgages. One institu-
tion at least is strongly suspected of doing this, and there
are rumors about another one. Under these circum-
stances, it would be a good idea for the institutions which
are above suspicion to state specifically that the in vestments
under various headings in their balance-sheets are the
capital sums only, and that all arrears and all foreclosed
properties appear under their proper headings. This
would at least draw a sharp line between them and any
institutions which dare not make such a statement lest
they should get too close to fraud and misrepresentation,
and go sliding on the thin edge of perjury.
* » * *
The London Bank of Australia, for the half-
year ended 31st December, 1896, shows a loss of £4396,
and dividends are suspended. Last half-year it made a
loss of £231, and for the six months ended December,
1895, it showed a profit of £5673, so in two half-years the
profits have dropped by over £lO,OOO. In other respects
things look moderately prosperous. In the last twelve
months the reconstructed deposits have been reduced
from £3,861,000 to £3,716,000, a reduction of
£145,000. The new deposits have decreased £6000;
the cash and liquid assets have decreased £200,000;
and nothing very remarkable has happened in
any other departments of the business. The £360,000
reserve of the old London Chartered Bank, which
was set aside to cover losses and depreciation, has
now been entirely written off, and no further depreciation
can be provided for out of this source. Also, the new
bank, having paid away most of its profits in 5\ per cent,
dividends to preference shareholders, has no reserve
fund, and the whole accumulation which it has to show
for many years of toil is £lBl2 remaining at the credit of
profit and loss. This isn’t a strong position by any
means, though it is vastly better than that of three or
four other reconstructs.
The second reconstruction scheme has met with
only moderate luck, so far. The London Bank, being
a British concern, had to reconstruct laboriously by
special Act of Parliament, and the House of Lords hacked
its proposed arrangement about remorselessly. As it now
stands, the interest on deposits is still to be 4)£ per cent.,
but if the profits of any half-year are insufficient to meet
this in full the bank may pay any rate down to 3 per
cent., which is the cast-iron minimum. But in that case
the difference between the amount paid and the 4) per
cent, accumulates as a debt due to the depositors, and
until this is cleared off the institution must pay no
dividends, nor is it allowed to put more than £lO,OOO to
reserve fund. The only visible advantage, therefore, is
that the London Bank, if things continue bad, instead of
being called on to pay more than it earns is allowed to
owe more than it earns. The fact that the arrears don’t
accumulate at compound interest is a negative benefit
that is too small to be appreciable. Another trifling
change is that the dates of repayment now run from 1899
to 1917. Altogether the London Bank has got worse terms
than any other reconstructed institution, and unless
interest goes up and the value of property improves, file
only prospect of things being much better seems to lie in
calling up more capital and paying off deposits with the
proceeds.
* * * •
The weary New Oriental Bank liquidation
finally closes about August, and the creditors will get
10s. lid. in the £ all round. The Federal Bank pays
another dividend, making Bs. 6d. in the £ so far, and the
City Bank of Melbourne pays another Is. in August,
making ss. 7d. in the £.
* * * •
The proposed amalgamation of the Perpetual
Executors Co. (Melb.) and the Trustees, Executors and
Agency Co. has fallen through at the last moment. The
shareholders of the Perpetual refuse to part with their
hopeless-looking little concern, so the frantic struggle of
five trustee companies to live on business that is only
sufficient for two must continue indefinitely.
* • * *
National Bank of Tasmania shows a profit of
£4012 for the half-year ended 31st May, and, as the usual
5 per cent, dividend absorbs £3991, there is £2l re-
maining. Last half-year the profit was £3534, and, as
the same dividend was declared, the National of Tasmania
paid away £457 more than it earned. The best that has
happened to this institution for a long time is that it
sometimes had a few pounds left after paying its divi-
dends ; at other times it hadn’t. A wiser management
would have cut the div. down to 4 per cent, three years
ago, and kept it there ever since, with a view to
strengthening the reserve. A reserve of £22,500 is a very
weak line of defence in these days of portentous slump.
• * • *
Tasmania is about to receive another lot of
foreign capital, and the latest enterprise is colossal both
in its aims and its possibilities. The Great Northern
Railway of Tasmania (capital, £450,000 in shares, and
£450,000 in debentures) has just been launched in Lon-
don, and, The Bulletin is told, the whole of the stock is
underwritten. The project provides for taking over the
existing line from Emu Bay to Bischoff, and extending
same to Rosebery ; thence one branch will be made to
Mt. Dundas and Zeehan, and another to Mt. Read, Her-
cules, Mt. Tyndal, Mt. Sedgewick to Mt. Lyell. This com-
prehensive system will bring Mt. Lyell, the southern ter-
minus, within 90 miles of splendid harbor accommodation
with deep water (Emu Bay) where material may be trans-
ferred direct from trucks to ship’s slings. But the idea
of starting an enterprise of these dimensions with only
£450,000 capital and a huge dead-weight of borrowed
money (on which interest, during the construction
period, will have to be paid out of capital) looks utterly
hopeless. The M.L. Midland 00. tried exactly the same
game, and collapsed. If the co. really intends railway
building it wants £1,000,000 of its own to start with. The
present capital will hardly keep the London office going
the way J. Bull runs things.
* • • *
About the most notable new feature of the
British B.H. statements, nominally for the half-year to
Dec. 31, ’96, only just now received from London, is
Turquand, Youngs and Co.’s auditor’s report directing
attention to the fact that “ the stock of concentrates at
the mine and in course of realisation has been taken at
the estimated selling price after making allowance for all
charges thereon, whereas, in our opinion, all stocks not
actually sold at the date of closing the accounts should be
taken at cost price.” This policy is not, however,
adopted in connection with any B.H. company. Indeed,
the British “ working account ” is unusually explicit: the
crude ore is “ valued at cost,” and the concentrates are
“estimated to realise” so-and-so. No doubt the line
“estimated surpluses” commonly found in B.H. com-
panies’ statements would permit of almost unlimited
juggling with figures; but up to date none of this appears
to have been actually done. If these leading London
accountants object to what the British has done, what
would they say, though, to the “ estimated sur-
pluses ” line ? All the same, Turquand, Youngs
and Co.’s proposed plan is not any too safe
where such unstable products as silver and lead are
being handled ; for one may be raised which, by a fluctua-
tion in the market, or from some other cause, is worth
at the time the statement is made a good deal less than
cost. Even an accompanying statement of the metal
value of the ore or concentrates on hand would be value-
less as a guide, because for some ore not 50 per cent, of
the metal can be extracted. In fact, if sulphide-mine
directors want to delude the shareholders they can easily
do it—in no industry perhaps more easily ; but up to date
they have probably deluded themselves more than they
have the shareholders.
• * * *
B.H. British directors say, in their half-yearly
report, that the explorations from the Junction North, at
the 550 ft. level, “proved disappointing, the lodes pinching
out as they reached the boundary of Block 16.” Pleasant
reading for those North shareholders who, spite of many
warnings, were induced to hope and expect that they
would get the Junction’s western lode! Of course, the
occurrence in the Junction and Junction North is a mere
bulging pocket; and the chances are that the lode for
some distance on either side has been reduced to pro-
vide material for the bulge.
* » * *
During the British mine’s last half-year, the
mining (including deadwork and filling up) cost 10s. B|d.
per ton of ore, the ore-dressing 4s. 9Jd. (which is more
than it ought to be), and management on the mine s)t>d.
The profit was just over Bs. per ton of ore. But of the
44,707 tons of ore handled, 30,546 tons were carbonates, of
which class very little is unfortunately now being got.
The directors thought, in view of the more constant
running of the mill, that they might be able to declare in
interim div., as well as the first ordinary one of Is., at
the meeting in London on May 12; but the returns
weren’t up.
* * * *
Ashcroft (Sulphide Corporation) claims, apropos
Block 10’s declaration that the co.’s mill is doing as good
sulphide treatment as any, that whereas Block 10’s profit
was 10s. per ton of ore, the profit derivable from the
electrolytio treatment of the same ore would amount to
about 455. He declares this “ from the work done during
the last three months at Cockle Creek.” All the same,
things are not quite right everywhere in connection with
the Ashcroft works and treatment, ami an engineer has
been specially reporting upon the feasibility of producing
a different form of concentrate from the mill on the mine.
But the greatest difficulty that has arisen has been due to
the rapid corrosion of some of the parts at the Cockle
Creek works.
• • • •
Block 10 has made a sale of a big parcel of so-
called tailings to Frankfort-on-Main Works. The material
assays 13oz. silver, 5’75 per cent, lead and 34 per cent,
zinc per ton. The transaction does not mean much of a
lift to the co. financially ; but it is nevertheless import-
ant as a substantial practical beginning in the utilisation
of the zinc in the sulphides (apart, that is, from the Ash-
croft process). Block 10 is, however, better situated, with
regard to these “ tailings,” than any other B.H. mine ex-
cept the Central (the Ashcroft people’s mine); no other
“ tailings ” (really the ore from which 50 per cent, of the
silver, 70 to 80 per cent, of the lead, and 20 per cent, of
the zinc has been taken) are so rich in either silver or
zinc, while, generally speaking, the others contain a little
more lead, which is the zinc-distillers’ curse. Block 10
intends to strive to develop this zino-“ tailings” market
by roughly re-treating the mat erial with a view to elimin-
ating some of the silica and so reducing the bulk.
• • * •
South mine is under one of its periodical clouds.
The ore available for breaking has for some weeks been so
hard that the mill returns have been seriously affected ;
and the faces show but little improvement.
• • * •
The interest now taken in N.S.W. copper-min-
ing has caused the paper-men in the country to give the
subject their best attention. Thus S.M. Herald’s In-
verell correspondent on the Bundarra copper-mine—
“ They have driven sft. into the lode without striking
the hanging-wail. It is peacock ore, and assays 51)4 P er
cent, copper. The ore is improving. Some ore on the
top assays 46 per cent. Some assays have shown 48 per
cent, copper, 250 z. silver and 4dwts. gold.” As Mt. Lyell,
with an average under 4 per cent., is worth much more
than a million, and Cobar, at 4)4, is worth about half a
million, how many millions is this mine worth ? The
S.M.H. should put a lot of its mining news under the
heading “ Wit and Humor.”
• * * •
Mt. David gold-mine, N.S.W., tons for 31oz.
What the object was in sending 3)40z. stone to Sydney at
a cost of at least an oz., while 30 heads of stamps are
going up, is not quite clear. An interim report issued
recently indicates a really good mine somewhat burdened
with debt. The title was long in dispute. That is a good
sign—the titles of most mines worth having in N.S.W.
generally are. Crozier, the prospector, crushed 105 tons
for 2700 z., and sold right out for £lO,OOO. The reef runs
from sft. to 20ft., and if it will average an oz., and the
ore-chutes are any length, then it is a mighty fine mine,
and Crozier is a fool for selling at such a price. The pre-
sent co. is preparing for an output of 1500 tons a month,
and, as usual in the province, the mill is ahead of the mine,
• * * •
Once more — “ Some very rich ore is now being
broken at the Jingera Proprietary mine, near Pambula,
N.S.W., the output being steady and constant and the
owners well satisfied with the results.” But never a line
as to amount, value, or divs.
* * * *
The discovery of native amalgam by Prof.
Modest Maryanski in the Boulder Main Reef, adds another
to the already remarkable features of the Great Boulder
locale. Gold, silver, telluride, native amalgam. What
next ? Bulong is also keeping its end up in rare minerals.
In addition to mustard gold—sure precursor of telluride—
recently exposed at the Great Eastern, what was thought
to be some valueless substance has been proved to be
slugs of pure gold, coated with a mineral somewhat re-
sembling platinum, but as yet unnamed.
• • * •
The chairman of Hannan’s P.D. Co., the Mar-
quis of Tweeddale, in throwing up the sponge, said he was
sorry, especially as the prospects of the co. were at a very
low ebb, but pressing engagements, etc. By the sale, over
a year ago, of two of the leases, the capital expenditure
had been reduced from £400,000 to £352,084. The bring-
ing-out of the water co. had been delayed by the state of
the market, also the sale of leases and the formatiion of
subsidiary cos. What followed showed that possibly the
non-finding of gold by the parent co. may have had some-
thing to do with it. Altogether, they have sunk 6600 ft.,
driven crosscuts and levels 12,000 ft., and they have done an
enormous amount of “ surface scratching.” He explained
that the various ore-bodies, which assayed^so high, had
proved to be “ segregated bunches of ore ” which did not
develop into continuous chutes. And even that went
down with Ass, alias Bull. “ He was not aware that
George Gray was a more expensive manager than others,
and, up to the present moment, they had not seen their
way to dispense with his services.” How pleasant for
Gray, who reported :—“ Am confident our property, at a
greater depth, will disclose valuable ore-bodies ” ! W.A.
Irwin also thinks the policy of sinking will be rewarded
sooner or later. Meanwhile, the co. is sinking in deadly
earnest, for, of a working capital of £150,000, it has left
only £37,000. And yet a year ago this co. had a market-
value of nearly two millions I
• * * »
At the starting of the mills for the associated
group of mines at Hannan’s: W. G. Brookman—“The
field had already turned out something like 12 tons of
gold, but had as yet only been scratched on the surface.”
Still impossible to leave well alone. Only scratched on
the surface ! There is no field in the world that has
received so much prospecting in so short a time as Han-
nan’s. The sinking, costeening, rf and driving, could only
be measured by miles. The Hannan’s Proprietary De-
velopment Co. must have spent on its leases alone up-
wards of £lOO,OOO in prospecting. Other Bull co’s have
prospected themselves into reconstruction. Few have
had the good luck of the Associated—to find two really
good mines in about a dozen. Brookman, no doubt, also
omitted to say that the working of these other leases was
a great tax on the two gold-producers— the Australia and
Australia East.
* • » *
The men at the Lady Loch mine, Coolgardie—
owned by an English co. which started with a few good
crushings—have struck work because their wages, £3llos.
a man, had not been paid. The men of the Forest ging,
belonging to the same co., also threaten to gc out uitless
their wages, now weeks overdue, are paid. The manager
says he can neither understand nor explain the position.
He was “ heartily sick of the whole business, and
thoroughly sympathised with the men.” Perhaps the
shareholders are sick of the whole business. Six months
ago Lady Loch shares were up to £4.
• * • •
Things seem in a bad way at Menzies, W.A.
“ Nearly all the mines have reduced hands. Several keep
on only sufficient men to hold the properties. About 100
men cleared out in ten days. At Niagara, Mt. Malcolm,
and other places out back things are just as bad, and
there are a great many men wandering about Menzies
because they have not the means to get away.”
From Kalgoorlie to The Bulletin :—“ Many
shrewd and cautious mining-men think that all the big
mines are now’ too high, and I am not sure they are not
right. The lodes at the 300 ft. levels ace getting hard and
much smaller, and the public seem to'have lost sight c A
the fact that there is a great difference between mining
and treating a 20ft. lode of easily-crushed and treated
oxidized ore, at 100 ft. and 200 ft. depth, and doing same
with sft. to 6ft. of hard quartz containing a percentage of
telluride and pyrites, from below 200 ft. The fact that the
reefs contain gold and telluride seems to have been enough
for them up to date. The fact is, they want to engineer
another boom, and are making use of any excuse they can
get hold of.”

Another Westralian co. which started its career
with a flourish of trumpets will soon be crying a go—the
Austin 00., on the Murchison. Prospectus: “Reef clear
and well-defined, averaging 4ft., and, in addition, there
is Bft. of formation carrying good gold right through it,
averaging two to three ounces to the ton. 5000 oz. dollied
out of one of the claims, which had 8000 tons of rich stone
on it.” Price, £41,000, of which £16,000 was cash. True
battery test, 350 tons for 330 z. In the time absorbed in
publishing this orushing, shares fell from 275. to 10s.
Manager’s valedictory cable—“l regret not having dis-
covered any payable quartz during my term of office.”
Last directors’ report:—“The mine has been thoroughly
exploited, and it is much to be regretted that the work
done has not been productive in the uncovering of pay-
able ore.’'

Craven’s Caledonia (Towers), which has been
under a cloud for some time, is looking up again. Lately
struck nine inches of heavily mineralised stone in Vic-
toria reef. Shares hardened accordingly.
Towers scrip-dealers find their occupations gone
during “annual” period. Writing doubles is now the
only means left the “seven-weeks delivery” men—known
as the “ forward brigade”—of raising the wind.
A recent Towers “ Wild Cat ” item was spoilt
by the printer. Re the Victoria No 1 West, it was stated :
“ If a pick had been put in the ground of that mine the
shares would probably be worth £1 apiece.” The par.
should have read “ If a pick hadn’t.”

“Anti-Boom,” a advises caution
with respect to the Hercules. He says that assays of
ore broken in the lower levels don’t exactly sing the
directors’ song ; that the Rich assays lately advertised
came from hatful of ore ; that all assays published
up to now are from picked ore, and that no bulk assays
are to be had. The Hercules has no machinery to speak
of; it has only an elastio and pushing crowd behind it,
and that is why it is a brokers’ mine. To put the show in
shape will cost £BO,OOO, and this (thoug-h the Hercules has
paid its own way to date) means selling part of the mine.
The bulk ore (continues The Bulletin correspondent) will
not go more than 2dwt. to 3dwt. gold (if this), but at least
20 per cent. zinc.
Briseis tin-mine (East Coast, Tas.) shareholders
have paid £135,000 in calls, and are not yet tired. They
still hope to strike the deep lead that paid £27,000 per an-
num in divs. to Krushka Brothers Home Co. adjoining.

SOCIETY
Letter received this week from North
Shore, Sydney ,
To the Editor The Bulletin.— Sir,—Very soon (I think
this year) the Queen of England will have reigned for 60
years. As this, I should imagine, must be almost a
record, couldn’t something be done to celebrate the.fact?
I don’t tbink it’s quite fair to let a thing like that pass
without taking any notice of it, but doubtless there are
many who would take the matter up if they knew about
it. It is for this reason that I write. Hoping that an
abler pen than mine will, &c., —I am, sir, &c., J. H. Nap.
• * * *
Scheme for a jubilee celebration at Liver-
pool (N.S.W.), the cheerful town where the
HIS POINT OF VIEW.
PARSON (to Bullocky): “ Why, my good man, do you us such shocking language when
addressing those poor horned beasts?”
BULLOCKY: “ It’s all very fine for you to talk, mister; you've only ONE devil to drive, I’ve got
fourteen ! ”
asylums are, promulgated under the auspices of
Varr.ey Parkes, M.L.A., son of old man Parkes :
It is intended at night to have a torchlight procession
on a large and novel scale. You are all requested to at-
tend and give all the assistance in your power to make the
procession a success. Fifty men on white or grey horses
are required for the procession. If any of you have one
or can obtain one you are respectfully requested to attend
and take part in the procession. It may here be stated
that an affair of this description to celebrate this re-
markable event will probably never in our days again be
witnessed. The procession will be headed by three torch-
bearers and a number of men on white horses four deep.
After them the Foresters, headed by Robin Hood, then
four torch-bearers, then the Foresters in regalia with their
banner, then 100 girls in white marching four deep, the
middle girls carrying ferns and the outside ones flowers,
next 50 men on horseback forming the letter U, inside
the horsemen 25 torch-bearers, inside of these the
Liverpool Band and the Liverpool Cadets four deep.
After them will come a platform carried by 12 men, on
which is erected a throne and canopy, on each side of the
throne will march two Mounted Infantry and also two
persons bearing large fans. On the arrival of the pio-
cession at the park the girls in white will surround the
throne four deep, the Cadets two deep, the torch-bearers
in single, the Foresters four deep and the horsemen in
single file. A speech will be given by the Mayor of Liver-
pool, at which time the Queen will rise and the angels
will leave their places and stand near her. Illuminations
will then take place and bonfires be lit, all the people
singing the National Anthem. All persons desiring to
assist in defraying the expenses of the procession are re-
quested to send subscriptions to the undermentioned
committee.
Consider tbe splendor of it —the ‘ two persons
bearing large fans ; ” the angels who rise when the
Mayor has done speaking; the 50 men on horse-
back forming an I O U ; the outside girls carrying
flowers : and the appeal for coin at the end.
’*• * *
Wagga (N.S.W.) has a rusted and dilapi-
dated cannon lying on the racecourse, and the
municipal councillors decided that it would be a
proper Jubilee celebration to let it off with a bang.
Accordingly, with that independent and self-
reliant spirit which characterises the Australian,
they wired to the Government asking to be sup-
plied with the necessary ammunition and com-
bustibles gratis. They were refused, cm the
ground that the ancient implement couldn t be
trusted, and then another telegram howled along
the wires to Sydney :
Loyalty of Wagga imperilled, forward tubes and ammu-
nition immediately, Captain Sheppard will take charge of
gun, and the Mayor will fire first gun.
Wagga won’t be loyal unless some one “gibs it
tikpen’ ’ for fireworks. In fact, the whole out-
break of loyalty seems to consist in getting some
one to gib it tikpen’ to celebrate with.
* * • •
A bright jubilee suggestion sprang last
week from a Melbourne dealer in machine-oils
and other kinds of grease. His idea was to have
a cinematograph film of the local procession pre-
pared for transmission to the Queen, “ who would
appreciate an offering so up-to-date, and so vividly
exhibiting the loyalty of her subjects m the
colony bearing her name.” A good enough pro-
posal. But wouldn’t it be better to send the loil
man ’Ome, armed with a small tub of liquid fat
wherevvith to anoint the head of the Gracious
Lady whose name adorns the colony in which he
is accumulating grease ?
The oil merchant’s proposal that Melbourne s
Jubilee ‘ loyalty ’ should be einematographed and
sent along to the Queen, raises questions as to
who would be the fittest persons to point the
apparatus at. The Bulletin is in favor of form-
ing the Leg. Councillors in a semi-circle around
Herkomer’s picture of Her Gracious, which might
be borrowed from the Art Gallery and placed upon
a colossal easel. The Military Commandant
(Holled Smith) should stand and blow a cornet
blast on the right hand of the painting, as a signal
for all the property-owners to fall prostrate before
their idol, d la Ko-ko in “The Mikado.” Then
they would arise in irregular order and the picture
might conclude with a vision of them waving their
hats as they walked away.

* * * •
Judging by Col. Lassetter’s cable, there
has been some trouble with N.S.W. contingent.
The men weren’t ashamed to blackleg on a wharf—
The Bulletin doesn’t know whether the Spanish
unionists were right or wrong, however—but some
of them seem to have grumbled because they are
being put at the tail-end of Queen Vic’s procession
with the sepoys—their proper place as servile
warriors. For an Australian soldier’s business is
to defend Australia, and not be a millinery item
at a British show.

How can Australia jubilate 1 Just look at
this cable:—
London, June 17. —The market for Australian poultry is
flat. Victorian fowls are selling at 2s. 6d. per pair. There
is no demand for ducks.
No demand for ducks at this of all moments ! Is
that the way to draw closer the silken bonds ?

* * * *
Dear Bulletin, —A correspondent sends
this to Labouchere :
As you have sometimes expressed your readiness to be-
stow titles on all who wish for them, it occurs to me that
perhaps you would like to suggest to the general public
that, though they cannot all hope to be Peers, yet there
is nothing to prevent every male child, even of the
humblest parentage, being christened “ Lord,” which is
not an uncommon surname. If this were done, a later
and a happier generation would see countless thousands
of Lords Lord John Smith ” would have ceased to be
a distinctive name; and England’s foes might hereafter
be met by a thin red line composed exclusively of “ Lord
Thomas Atkinses.”
Australians whose democracy does not prevent
them from flying after Imperialistic prefixes might
improve on this idea by baptising their children
“Sir John ” or “ Sir Richard,” or any other com-
pound front name which strikes their fanev
C.R.W.

“ALICE: OR, THE MYSTERIES"
(A sketch by Caleb Porter.)
At Collingwood (Melb.) Court recently
Mary Ann, a spinster of 17, sued a youth of IB
for the maintenance of their joint infant. Mary
Ann stated that she had been a servant, but was
now supported by her mother, and was earning
nothing’. Her accomplice, Henry, aged 18, said
he couldn’t support the infant or marry Mary
Ann, because he only earned 6s. a week. The
Bench ordered him to pay 4s. a week, so this
arrangement leaves him 2s. for board and clothes
THE JUBILEE.
Our “statesmen’s” glance
Saw grovel chance
To miss which were a pity ;
They made a splash
With publio cash
And slobbered o’er the city.
Their loyal zeal
They would reveal
If money could display it;
And why be mean
When they can glean
The income-tax to pay it?
The merchants o’er
Each shop and store
Ilung out their decorations,
To keep a line
They cut a shine
With big illuminations.
Most thought the lot
Was utter rot
And waste of coin surprising,
But then the thought
Some comfort brought
That it was advertising.
The plumber chirped
As hard he worked ;
The gas shareholder
chuckled
To see how dight
In robes of light
The mighty city truckled.
They might have thought
It argued naught
But livid lack of gumption,
But sang, I ween,
“ God Save the Queen ”
Who booms the gas eon-
sumption.

A SONG.
[F OR THE BULLETIN.]
To me, at evening, came
A flicker from the flame
Of first love’s bliss ;
I saw her soft grey eyes—
Times are when old Time dies—
I felt her kiss,
Her clinging kiss : as yet,
All other lips I’ve met—
Their kisses flee.
When the hot mouths are reft
Apart, what is there left
Save memory ?
But hers, ah ! how to tell j
Sheer from her soul it fell,
And seemed to say :
“ Dear heart, go where thou wilt,
Through innocence, through guilt,
My kiss must stay.”
J. JINGLE.

From an Arcadia passenger’s letter :
Rpoause we took some Indian passengers on board at
Aden, we were in for a sweet time of it at Malta, where
the people are in such an unholy funk about the plague
that the quarantine suppliers wouldn’t touch our coin"ex-
cept (literally) with the tongs; popping the money imme-
diately into some cleansing concoction and handing over
whatever you bought, also with the tongs, across a board
about Bft. wide. Signing the cross, too, and invoking the
saints (I suppose) over every deal. These rock-scorpions
are still in the Middle Ages !
* * * *
Dear Bulletin, -Why not a statue to W.
J. Clarke? Among ordinary inheritors of wealth
he was Great by virtue of his superior generosity.
Compare Clarke, of Melbourne, with other titled
parvenus, whose chief ambition is to bequeath an
enormous fortune to the next earl, baron, or
baronet, as the case may be. Compare him with
well, there isn’t far to look for another mil-
lionaire son of a grasping father —and say if it is
not advisable to set up his effigy as an example to
Moneybags at large. He was a useful example
during his lifetime. But for W. J. Clarke there
might not have been a Francis Ormond to endow
a college, and otherwise emulate his friend. —
Melbourne.
* * * *
One hundred and eighty-two wreaths sent
to a recent large Melb. funeral were noticed in the
Argus, and “ many others ” were cruelly debarred
from publicity.
The outside doctors are making a big
point of the insanitary condition of Adelaide Hos-
pital, in which institution many nurses are now
down with typhoid. But, by the irony of fate,
one of the chiefs of the medical strikers and his
daughter have been prostrated by the disease,
which was contracted in their own private resi-
dence. It is stated that the deep drainage has
been thrown out of gear by the recent earthquake,
and that the large increase of typhoid is due
thereto.
« * * +
Skating, which faded when the boom
burst, is showing signs of winter revival in Melb.,
but not among the Nicest. Society wants to ride
on wheels, but not on rollers.

The S.A. Govt, is going to attempt to stop
by legislative enactment the publication in the
newspapers of “ women’s salvation and such-like
advts. The clergy have long been trying to im-
press upon their flocks that there is a special merit
in having “children nine and ten,” while they
stigmatise as wicked all attempts to limit the con-
tents of the quiver. Wesleyan ministers, by the
way, receive from the denomination, under the
Children’s Fund, a grant of about .£8 a year for
each youngster until he or she reaches the age of lb.
The financial state of this Fund is always a matter
of Weslevan anxiety.

Dear Bulletin, — On the rainy
days of Winter the dirtiest specimens of
dosserdom crowd into the noisome news-
paper-branch of Sydney Public Library,
and convert the place into a dormitory.
Yesterday I wanted to look at a country
paper, but found a ragged Dpmainer
poring intently over the sheet. Six hours
later he was apparently still studying
the same page, and closer inspection
proved him to be asleep. The poor devil
was quite indignant when the attendant
woke him. —T.D.

It would be hard to conceive any-
thing more asinine than the proposed
provisions by which the N.S. \\. Phar-
macy Bill would allow of the registra-
tion, as qualified pharmacists, of assist-
ants who have been in the business for
three years, and of persons who have
professed to practise it for two. Suffi-
cient practical qualification might pos
sibly, in either case, have been
but there shouldn’t be any “ po-sibly
in matters of life and death. As well
put every quack of two years standing
on the Medical Register, or license as
a surveyor the man who has carried the
chain for three years.
* * * *
Dear Bulletin,— A queer yam.
Blackfellows’ weather-predictions—based
chiefly on animal and insect observa-
tion —are generally ahead of our cyclonic
and barometric deductions. But we
white people have as sure a means of
knowledge even within our doors. When
the common domestic cat, in washing
its face, passes its paw behind its ear,
rain is at hand : the oftener the paw goes
behind the ear the heavier the rain to
come. For long I was a scoffing sceptic ;
but six months of close observation
have proved two cats invariably right,
and I now place the cat unhesitatingly
first, the barometer nowhere. Quiz.
» * *
At Dubbo (N.S. W.) the gallows when not
on active service, lies in the cellar of the court-
house among’ the time-expired writs, played-out
ea sa.’s, satisfied distress-warrants, and other
mementoes of by-gone litigation Its position is
exactly below the Judge s bench. Perhaps the
Department of Justice thinks it may in some
occult way harden His Honor s heart, or perhaps
it is only, after all, reckoned tu be a handy thing
to have about the house. It is now erected for
the butchering of Nosey Bob’s latest victim
Moore, the Bourke murderer—who is said to have
•occasionally practised as a hangman himself.
Mongrelia, alias Queensland, contains
about 8000 Chinese, mostly adult males, and only
some 20 or so Chinese wives. Wha’ plicee whitee
sissetah ? My wohd ! And how many slant-eyed
half-castes and quarter-castes are there in Kanaka-
land ?
* » • *
A Brisbane (Q.) penlady, in the Echo, is
shocked at the demoralisation rampant at the
International Exhibition, from 5 p.m. to 7 p.m.,
every pitch-dark evening. To save expenses m
illumination, only the basement, where all attend-
ants have tea, is lighted up, and that tea-room
gives the shocked one an idea of a “free-and-easy.”
Also, the bush-house and tea-gardens are left un-
lighted, and are promenaded by “ young people of
both sexes.” At which thought the penlady puts
her hands before her eyes and peers suspiciously
through the interstices between her fingers.
* * • *
A Vic. detective, just returned from Bris-
bane Exhibition, avers that the show is such a
frost in the matter of attendance as to starve out
the criminals who usually attend on such affairs.
He further says that Sydney streets are full of
erstwhile Vin. gaol-birds and confidence men.

and yet wanted to marry a widower with three
children, whose salary was £2 10s. a week \n un-
commercial jury awarded her <£2o damagu.

The little men are poor in fame,
The great man holds his fellows’ eyes ;
The little men are dead in name
As quickly as the Darling dries ;
But when at last the big man dies,
Then little men can advertise.
Not theirs to mourn his perished worth
Or watch him upward to the skies—
They are the offspring of the earth
And in their little ways are wise ;
And so, whene’er a Triton dies,
The minnows rush to advertise.

an act of academic comity “Books” asks : (1) If a man
is arrested through mistaken identity, on a criminal

can he obtain any redress from the Crown ? (2) How long
does a criminal warrant hold good ? No pecuniary re-
dress can be got if the constable had reasonable and prob-
able cause for making the arrest. A criminal warrant
holds good until it is executed H.S.C. : First learn to
