A WOMAN’S LETTER.
Sydney, June 22, 1897.
Mr Dear Mteh—
Mad there bepn a prize given for the best-
decorated street during the gas and bunting waste,
Oxford-street would have won it easily. In that
thoroughfare the whole scene of adornment was
consistent, and a string of gay lanterns carried
along from end to end united the Oxonians in a
decorative whole. Elsewhere, ideas were carried
out independently of neighbors, and every artist
who worked his wicked will on the royal features
had his own notion of what Queen Victoria is
like. However, the crown was always there as a
trade-mark of genuineness, and the Princess of
Wales could invariably be known by her curly
bang. On Monday a great excitement for sight-
seers was watching the workmen draping a cold,
stony-fronted bank with miles of innocent-looking
art-muslin. Decorators absorbed every scrap of
red, white and blue muslin weeks ago, and late
purchasers gave famine prices for any tints that
came anywhere near those colors. Barbers were
loyal with little cost—their striped poles and door-
posts are painted the royal hues all the year
round. Many glittering fixings were encores from
ten years ago, furbished up and a little added to.
Of the effigies displayed in shop-windows, Her
waxen and self-working Majesty, in a big boot-
shop, was a most attractive advt. ; another in
Pitt-street attracted shoals of gazers, and was
truer as a portrait.
If the recording-angef ever nods at his work, the
morning papers are all there to jog his memory.
They have got all the names of those loyal wor-
shippers who went to church last Sunday, and
have also noted the women’s “ dainty " dressing.
The Cathedral in George-street had the gaudiest
congregation, but St. Mary’s service was ahead
with ecclesiastical robing—purple and scarlet and
cloth-of-gold were gleaming everywhere. Crowds
of sightseers filled both buildings and the surround-
ing pavements to see the dignitaries arrive and
depart. At the Town Hall gates there was a
Buckingham Palace air about the two haughty
officials who kept the populace out while the car-
riages drove away from the precincts. Mean-
while, a disappointed throng of gaily-dressed
women, clasping prayer-books as a proof that they
meant to go to church, brightened the ground on
the other side of the Cathedral, and so missed the
swagger procession of “miserable sinners ” rolling
down George-street. His Ex., who must feel
pretty lonely now when he goes to church without
his cocked hat, wasn’t quite as gorgeous as he was
at the wedding, but all the military men and
judges were bright in scarlet, except one judiciary,
who wore black-and-gold. A large colonel was
balanced by a small groom in a minute dogcart.
Compared with the glittering scarlet and Mayor’s
gold chain, the Parliamentary black-and-white
was dull. The “ God-fearing ” Ministry acted the
part thoroughly on Sunday—frock-coat, tall hat,
right-hand glove and prayer-book in gloved left
hand, rolled umbrella and wife on right, all look-
ing as good as new. It was a beautifully English-
bourgeois spectacle.
Women turned up in shoals to the Titheradge
matinee, for the departing actor, popular and re-
spected among men, has always been something
of an idol with the sex, and could have been a
much bigger idol had he not coldly turned his
back on adoration and devoted himself to horti-
culture. His farewell, uttered with the co.
grouped round him, was genuinely pathetic and
saddened the general tone; all the co. seemed
depressed, especially Mr. Brough, who leaned his
head against an adjacent prop., the image of
melancholy. J. Knight and Miss Page showed in
an unremarkable playlet, in which the lady wore
a blouse offering suggestions of renovating a last
year’s bodice ; it was fixed up with velvet in
bright green and coral pink. If the tints had
been blue and purple I shouldn’t have recorded
them, but it’s refreshing this week to notice any
hues except the all-prevailing loud tones of glaring
“royal” colors. Among the musical selections,
the complexion of the sopranos who braved the
footlights without the aid of grease-paint was
rather a disastrous item; another unmade-up
soloist did not belie his appearance, for his friends
are raising funds for him on account of his failing
health. After the farewell many of the Nicest
d.c. occupants were refreshed at a fashionable tea-
room by invitation. The Titheradge dinner at
the Athenaeum Club, presided over by Mr.
Barton, Q.C., was largely attended and a great
success ; a man who was present tells me that
Titheradge's reply to the toast of his health was
the very best speech he ever heard from an actor
anywhere.
A “ woman-dinner ” —the first of these up-to-
date soirees recorded in Sydney—was given at the
Ladies’Club last week. The hostesses were mem-
bers of the original coterie out of which the pre-
sent club has emerged, and the guest was the aunt
of the proprietor, lately released from quarantine.
A hitch arose in the arrangements owing to one of
the givers of the feast (a travelled lady with short
hair who rides a bike and has written a book)
having aired in that work some facts in the life of
another member, a sassiety star, but the difficulty
was got over by the caricatured lady leaving her-
slf out of the party. Every detail was arranged
from manly models, minus spirits and smoke, and
the speeches were nervously correct and proper.
The toasts were three : “ The Queen,” “ Our
Guests,” and “ Other Women ; ” the last a suffi-
ciently comprehensive one. Nothing is more un-
like a woman than another woman, and the pro-
poser of the toast, lumping the whole sex together
in a grand universal sisterhood, had tier work cut
out to display an all-embracing goodwill, which
isn’t by any means feminine. The speeches were
short and neat, but not fireworky at all.
A few women in Sydney can rise and emit a
coherent sentence to a roomful of people without
gasping and clutching at the nearest chair-back,
but. even the fluent person who goes beyond re-
marking “ I think the price of tickets should be
two-and-six,” at a Town Hall meeting, looks
strai-ht down her nose and takes herself mighty
seriously. She never elocutes or jests or perorates,
and her vocabulary is limited to the best-worn
words and “I think.” If a woman orator is elo-
quent it is usually on her own, and only, subject
which she drags in everywhere. If some of these
one-topic women thunderers were men we should
call them simply bores.
With the winter comes the spiritualist. At
the house of a Sydney lady, who had a sensa-
sational “drop down” not long since, many
peculiar manifestations and materialisations are
said to occur. The “circle” is a very credulous
one, being composed for the most part of women
seeking the consolation of spiritualism as a balm
for the death of relatives and friends. At one>
gaHiering the spirit of a well-known sportsman
appeared bemoaning the fact that he had ruined
hundreds on this wicked sphere, and asking the
circle to pray for him. As the “ sport ” had no
friends in this pay-for-admission crowd, it was sug-
gested that his spirit ought to leave a cash con-
tribution for prayers in advance. Later on in the-
evening a Chinese spook took possession of a lady
with an Irish brogue. Had the exhortation only
been phonographed, it would make all self-respect-
ing people swear off spiritualism for ever.
Among smart folk, the talk just now is livened
by several little anecdotes about those trouble-
some insects euphemistically termed “Norfolk
Howards.” An ultra - fashionable couple was
deterred from moving into a house lately vacated
by an almost-as-high-toned family, owing to the
presence of the insects. Tongues in the Nicest,
circles wagged over this as proving that the.
departed family had not used yellow soap and
scrubbing-brushes too freely ; but then, that was;
only circumstantial evidence —though sometimes,
as Thoreau says, circumstantial evidence is con-
vincing—as, for instance, when one finds a trout
in the milk-jug. At a most exclusive club, a.
sleeper found himself one night not alone, and,
packing three of the smart insects in a pill-box, he.
sent them to the secretary, with his compliments.
But the haughty sec. got even with him by send-
ing a polite message that the little strangers must,
have been collected and brought to the club by the
grumbler from the house aforesaid. The clubman,
had been a frequent visitor there.
A performer in a certain recent operatic trouble
down East was the possessor of a quaint pair of
legs. Amateurs’ legs are always impossible, but
these were even worse than usual. Their owner
had evidently never worn tights before, and in that
comic-opera garb her limbs looked like a capital
D, for one was straight and the other curly. Her
husband on the first night sat in the d.c. gazing at
the stage with a pained expression. The full
horror of those limbs had never dawned on him
before.
The University amateurs struggled with “Ran-
dall’s Thumb” at the Standard Theatre last
Thursday evening. From the beginning the audi-
ence looked prepared for the worst, and the per-
formers’ worst showed a high degree of badness
all through. The prompter worked hard to make
the piece go, and carried the whole of the last act
on his shoulders. Owing to the fatal amateur
craving for wigs, most of the eo. were apparently
suffering from swelled head, and there was a want
of expression in their hair. One of the female
parts was taken by a youth who dressed and
looked naturally, and was only unconvincing when
he remarked “ Horace ! ” in a deep bass. Miss.
White, who played an old maid, saved most of the
situations she was in, and had an original way of
turning her hair grey by binding white chiffon
round it under her hat.
A new soprano, Miss Ridley, had a vocal trial
trip in Paling’s mirrored hall on Saturday after-
noon, and pleased a score or so of musical critics
whom she had invited to hear her. She warbled
in a clear, well-cultivated voice, with a method
made in Germany, and showed to most advantage
in her oratorio selection.
The Tempe ball is billed this year for 7th July.
An energetic committee, with Mrs. A. Cape and
Mrs. E. Simpson as hon. treasurers and Mrs.
Kirkwood Colley and Miss Jennings as hon. secs.,
is working it up, and announces as extra attrac-
tions the usual fancy sets and a military bicycle
ride by ladies and gentlemen for the amusement of
gallery onlookers.
The Baby Show, which began on Monday after-
noon, consisted of about 100 exhibits, a loud piano
to drown the wails of the same, an interested
crowd of critical women with one or two discon-
certed men inspecting the entries, and a general
atmosphere of feeding-bottles and new flannel.
Some of the penned-up exhibits were remarkably
well-grown for their months ; one bloated darling
labelled three months looked at least a year old.
The veterans of the crowd were elderly infants of
two ; a sturdy pair of twins in tartan'frocks were
among the lively features of the show, while many
cherubs slumbered in a soothing-syrupy way. One
little chap with orange-brown eyes and pure
golden hair was the beauty, but they were nearly
all such fine children that, comparing them with
the surrounding grown-ups, one wondered what
becomes of all the pretty babies.
The Brough benefit at the Lyceum on Monday
was made the most of by a society audience, with
whom the co.’s popularity has always been strong.
The d.c. was crowded, and dressy and brightened
with Gov’t’ouse uniforms and diamonds, and much
fuss and feathers on cloaks. It was a strangely
silent audience, though-they all seemed to feel
that it was a “good-bye,” and were for once sub-
dued below chatter - level. The album full of
artists’ sketches, which was handed to Mr. and
Mrs. Brough by Mr. Barton at the end of “ Dandy
Dick,” has two or three pages containing solely
the names of the plays in which they have acted
when here —a long list. The sketches are charm-
ing reminiscences of the ’arbor from many points
of view, the Cri., and other personal associations.
After the album-and-cheque presentation, Miss
Temple was given an opal ring, which surprised
her into a grateful little speech. A small invited
crowd went behind after, where, amid the ruins of
St. Marvel’s Deanery, a cloth was laid, and repre-
sentatives of art, journalism, music, the theatre
and sassiety joined in drinking good-bye and good-
luck to the Brough co.
At the wedding of Acting - Premier Tozer’s
daughter in Brisbane lately, everything was done
in the most top-lofty style. Govt. House was in-
vited, but failed to arrive in time, and “dearly
beloved ” started without His or Her Ex. When
the party did come in a prayer was proceeding, and
the distinguished arrivals clattered to their seats
so noisily that the officiating clergyman paused
until they reached their places, and then resumed
the service.
The Protectionists of Redfern held a “ social ”
in the Oddfellows’ Temple last Friday night. The
cards were issued by the lady protectionists, and
responded to by about 300 guests, including
several M’sL.A. and their families. Dancing was
kept up to a string band with much enthusiasm
till 2 a.m. _ The next light fantastio Protectionist
toes to trip in the good cause will belong to
Waterloo and Belmore. The lady president
Mrs. Graham, and sec. Mrs. Gray, were elabo-
rately gowned. Among the dresses, black was
first favorite.
Fashion scribes in writing up the costumes of 60
years should note that the philosophy of female
dress has been the gradual recognition that women
have legs. In the Queen’s childish days, even
babyish limbs were concealed by “ pantalettes ” or
“ trouserettes,” as witness the hideous little sample
garments now on show in David Jones’s window
—the shape of the nether limb was ungazed upon
at any age. Look at the ballet-girls in antique
picture papers, with full bunching skirts, and never
a kick indicated beyond an angle of 45deg. Also re-
call illustrations of riding-habits in the early fifties,
the skirt concealed everything—even the clutching
power of the right knee on the pommel was
shrouded in voluminous mystery, and flowing
yards of strong cloth billowed round the rider and
snatched at danger from every hedge and tree.
How much more workmanlike are the short skirts
and frankly-shown, natty riding-boots of to-day.
Crinoline was created in a spasm of prudery to
hide all form, but was attacked and killed by
caricature. An age that has travelled from crino-
line to cycling-dress can’t surely be accused of
retrogression sartorially.
Writes my Melbourne correspondent:—
“ The Diamond Jubilee Loan Collection of pic-
tures was opened on Saturday night at the
National Gallery by H.E., backed up by Lady
B. (in black moire antique), Mrs. Freeman Thomas
(in blue brocade), and his fragile-looking staff of
aides. In his most pronounced House of Commons
mannah His Ex. droned on for half an hour
about all things relevant and irrelevant, which had
happened anywhere on earth during the last 60
years, while the assembled guests frankly turned
their backs on the speaker, and the women criticised
one another in tones so loud that the vice-regal
voice sounded through the chattering like some
one rolling heavy furniture in the basement. The
night was wet, and the company consequently
shabby ; wet shoes and skirts made watery traces
along the polished floors, and straightened fringes
drooped over faces, giving to those who were not
wise enough to wear toupees a haggard and de-
jected air. One woman in an iridescent snaky-
looking gown of the new glass material gained the
proud eminence of being the smartest frocked
woman in the crowd.
“Undoubtedly the ‘sensation’ picture in the
show is Longstaff’s portrait of Miss Fink, though
Millais’ picture of little Cecil Webb had its group
of admirers all night. A collection of early por-
traits of Her Gracious and ‘ dear Albert ’ is so like
a collection of Leech’s and Cruikshank’s carica-
tures that it is quite impossible to take them seri-
ously. Vic., in side curls and a crinoline (wherein
the painter has painfully inserted the then fashion-
able * startled-fawn ’ expression), and Albert, with
cast-iron legs and an insignificant German face,
may bear out Winterhalter’s whole ideas of the
dignity of royalty, but it needed a Cruikshank to
do that era full justice. ‘At Osborne,’ too, shows
the Queen in her widowhood—on horseback in
something very like a crinoline and a hideous coal-
scuttle bonnet; the Princess Somebody’s wedding
points out that in those days her ma wore a turban
and looked like the old lady who played whist
with Pickwick at Bath, and that H.M. stuck her
hair full of assorted roses. It would seem that
V.R. has never been a good subject for artistic
portraiture, or that the paint-slingers of the early
Victorian era bore that much-pictured lady a
distinct grudge ; if “Albert the Good” wasn’t hen-
pecked then his portraitists libelled his expression
every time.

“ A few weeks previous to the death of a wealthy
and prominent Victorian, than whom there never
lived a better-natured man, his wife received a
letter recommending somebody’s Inebriate Re-
treat as a suitable place for the cure of her hus-
band. The lady sent a courteous reply to the
effect that her husband’s ‘ intemperance ’ was a
popular delusion. She might have stated, in
good, round terms, that a well-conducted man of
his age, who was never incapable of putting his
hand in his pocket to help somebody else, didn’t
need curing, anyway. Also, she might have been
excused if she had sent the biggest groom out of
her stable to wallop her thick-skinned correspon-
dent into a sense of common decency.
The cablegrams which so copiously chronicle
the doings of the Australian swashbucklers in
London have so far failed to give any information
on a subject highly interesting to every loyal sub-
ject of Her Majesty in Australia. Sad to relate,
when the Vic. Mounted Rifles left Port Mel-
bourne and were safely at sea, the horrible truth
was revealed that someone had blundered, for not
one of the heroes had another pair of trousers but
that in which he stood. There was no chance of
getting them sent to Adelaide, for the skipper of
the boat pleaded that the signal code didn’t pro-
vide for a request for pants, and thus any hope of
importuning a passing lighthouse was dashed to
pieces. The result was that the glorified country
yokels had to complete the voyage in those same
pants, and as they are bound to be pretty well
worn by this time, their country wants to know
whether they are escorting their Queen in their
pyjamas, or whether they have been attached to a
Highland regiment, or what ?

“ I lately had achat with a pretty girl at the
florist’s. She says that, as a rule, a successful
actress is more profitable to the trade than a big
funeral, because even if the former does not mean
big orders she lasts longer, and pays better in the
long run. She estimated that her firm had cleared
close upon £3OO out of one swell funeral, but that
was a record. The best kind of actress for busi-
ness is a young and pretty burlesque artiste with
no known male accompaniment. Indeed, it would
pay the florists to subsidise the theatres to pro-
duce Grace regularly ; but, according to
the contemptuous Flora, the trade is dreadfully
lacking in intelligent enterprise, the whole of its
brain-power being devoted to the task of getting
half-a-guinea for a shilling’s-worth of carnations
and chrysanthemums.
“ Another word or two on a well-worn subject.
When the medical doctresses of Victoria found
that the (staff’s) door of the only women’s hospi-
tal in the province was, to all intents and pur-
poses, labelled ‘Gentlemen only/ they naturally
determined to make a little h-heaven of their
own.’ The new Melb. institution, which is now
staggering under the title of Queen Victoria
Memorial Hospital for Women/ was really in
existence some months ago. It treats both
women and children, and is officered entirely by
women ; and considering that both the other insti-
tutions devoted to the same purpose are con-
stantly flying the signal ‘ full inside,’ the jubilee
undertaking vvill probably have plenty of oppor-
tunity to get into debt.
“ But, because the Women’s Hospital, Carlton,
had a long monopoly of the particular feminine
ailments it caters for, the idea of a vigorous rival
in the field seems to have ruffled the flounces of
its matronly committee. At a meeting, lately,
the angry ones declared that most of the cash
collected by canvassers for the Q.V.M.H. had
come from a long-suffering public under the mis-
apprehension that it was going towards the old
original refuge. This statement was promptly
challenged, and all the ensuing correspondence
tends to show that the first kitten was mewing on
the wrong roof. However, the controversy is
equally unfortunate for both objects,
Yours affectionately, Cleo.
After all, the Melbourne illuminations
were a somewhat prodigious display of Jubilee
grovel. _ The City Fathers, ill-famed for their
callous indifference to local charities, can be held
responsible for a lot of sinful expenditure in loyal
gas, outside of their own municipal waste of the
ratepayers’ coin. As soon as it became known
that the Corporation intended to indulge in a
Jubilee frenzy the madness spread. Menzies’
Hotel threw away .£250 upon its elaborate design ;
James Service, the Scotchman, had a £lOO flare-
up ; and all sorts of buildings at the unfrequented
end of the town gave sad illustrations of the fierce
light that beats upon a throne. The rival lighting
go’s reap the profits, and the loss will fall in-
directly upon the employes of the firms that love
Our Gracious Queen.
One of Australia’s most excited sons just
now is J. W. Fleming, of Brunswick (Melb.).
This jubilant person claims to be the “first white
native of the colony that bears our Sovereign
Lady’s name, ” and he is understood to spend much
of his time in composing letters to the Queen.
Her Nibs—or somebody else who amounts to the
same thing—is guilty of an occasional reply to her
loyal Fleming, and in consideration of his being
the first white native, &c., she has posted him a
sprig of heather cut by her own royal hand, and a
photo, of her own royal self as she appears in her
royal donkey-cart. The effect of all this royal
condescension upon Fleming had been quite
shocking up to last week. Its effect at time of
writing is probably awful in the extreme. The
jubilee condition of a native who, during his term
as Mayor of Brunswick, purchased a steam-roller
for the borough and christened it “ The Countess
of Hopetoun,” is bound to be awful. The Bulle-
tin expects to hear that Fleming exploded, along
with the other crackers, on the night of 22nd.
The death of N.S.W. Railway-Commis-
sioner Eddy removes a man whom one grudges
to the Shades. The bitterest and most pro-
minent of his antagonists have also their ig-
®pble uses, but if a score of such were swept into
the dustheap every morning the community would
never miss them. It is a disheartening feature of
human economy that the loftiest natures are also
the most delicate. Their unselfish forethought,
their concentration or exaltation, their devotion
to duty, are obtained at the cost of tension which
makes their bodies an easy prey to disease; while
coarse-grained, thick-headed types sleep eight
hours without a tremor, eat and drink noisily all
day, and roar like bulls at stump-demonstrations,
to end in the odor of a mean respectability at
ninety or so. E. M. G. Eddy was a man in a
on—rehabie, honorable, able. The reformed
iM.b.vy. railway system is his work; and he
reaped the reformer’s harvest of animosity and
slander. He has given for the public good his
brain and time, which were paid for; and his life,
which was not paid for. It is a keen grief that
such a man should die hardly past his prime.

The Son is dead and gone ;
Now, Man is crucified !

When Dr. William Macky, brother of
Presbyterian parson Dill Macky, started practice
as a sawbones in Sydney, someone wrote on the
door of the manse —“ Body and soul saved with
skill by Dr. Dill and his brother Bill.”
John Plumper Hoolan, the digger in his
natural buff, is known to the Croydon boys, but
Hoolan, M.P., with a boiled shirt and a nosegay
in the top buttonhole of his coat, is a sight reserved
for Brisbane in general, and the ’Ouse in parti-
cular.
Critchett Walker, N.S.W. Principal
Under-Sec., will be 56 on June 28. He ought to
have been born at Parramatta, but the event took
place on the steamer Arabian near the Cape of
Good Hope. He joined the N.S.W. civil service
41 years ago.
The only Australians who received the
title of G.C.M.G. were Alfred Stephen and
Henry Parkes of N.S. Wales, and Thomas Elder
and Henry Ayers, of S.A. All are now dead, the
two last having passed away during the second
quarter of this year.
In London, Judge Windeyer has read a
paper on “Technical Education in N.S.W. ’ It
probably contained a reference to the historical
six-barreled Mount Rennie gallows, constructed
by a local firm, on whom a Sydney daily paper
said it “ reflected great credit.”
At the opening of Q. Parliament, every
M.L.C. present, except Pat Perkins, wore the
regulation evening suit, white choker and white
gloves. Pat attended in his walking clobber, and
nobody was horrified. Councillors are mostly
more snore and clothes than legislation.
Henry Ayers, the recently-deceased S.A.
politician, obtained his knighthood owing to the
accident that he was Premier of the Ministry
whioh was in office when the transcontinental
telegraph-line was completed, despite the fact that
he was one of the strongest opponents of the
scheme.
In reserving certain law points raised by
Butler’s counsel, Chief Justice Darley said he
would get the Full Court to consider them on
the following Monday—thus publicly assuming
that prisoner would be convicted by the jury
then absent from court! Isn’t this just about the
record judicial faux pas ?
Another Australian vice-regal story. This
particular Ex., a golf enthusiast, had been playing
nis first game at the local links, and, when the
time arrived for tipping the “ caddies,” he asked
one of the company what was regarded there as a
fair tip. On being told that 6d. was quite enough,
but that they usually got Is., he calmly turned
round to his friend and asked him for change of
“ a shullin.”

The condemned man Butler would have
exercised a lawful right had he declined to plead
while ABtley Cooper and members of Government
House party occupied seats on the judgment
bench. An accused person is legally entitled to a
Bench constituted of proper law officials only, just
as he is entitled to a jury consisting solely of the
12 men who pronounce upon his case. What if
the jury foreman invited half-a-dozen respected
grocers and tailors to share the jury-box with his
11 associates ?
Dear Bulletin, — As something of a be-
liever in David Syme, I don’t imply blame in the
following (possible) secret history of recent occur-
rences. “By sending rifle-team to England, I
show publio spirit, score with the ‘silken-bonds ’
people, and make offer of a title reasonably cer-
tain. Then, by refusing said title, show more
public spirit, score again with the Democratic
crowd, vindicate the Symic Tradition, and lose
nothing that I value a tinker’s damn ! ” David
the Wily is quite capable of seeing thus far ahead
of his nose !—A.M.
“ Waihoa ” to The Bulletin :
Re Henry Lawson’s appointment as master of a native
school in Hawke’s Bay. There is here a boundless field
opened for Lawson’s genius, to wit, the introduction of
the Maori into literature. As far as my reading goes, this
has never yet been done, with the single exception of
Manning’s glorious book, “ Old New Zealand.” Indeed,
it seems to be as difficult to get the Maori into fiction as
to keep the wattle out of Australian poetry. It has often
been attempted, but the product has seldom risen much
above “ Deadwood Dick ” style, with Maori names sub-
stituted for Indian, or feeble variations on the worn-out
Hinemoa theme. It is strance that the finest savage race
on earth should be so intractable on paper. The material
is here ; if Lawson can supply the workmanship the world
should hear of the result.

John Alex. Dowie, the bearded little par-
son who flourished his umbrella in Sydney 15
years ago or so, has long been in full' blast at
Chicago as a faith-healer, where he runs a “ re-
ligious ’’ paper called the White Dove. Therein
we read a brief autobiography of himself, in which
he says :
In Sydney I was offered by Sir Henry Paries the port-
folio of Minister of Education in his Government, and I
could have been, he said, Premier within a few years if I
had only given myself to polities.
But he didn’t, thank Heaven ! John Alex, is
still the same old John Alex., for in the White
Dove (how eternal is the fitness of things !) he
characterises pressmen as “dirty, stinking little
pots of whisky and tobacco.”

That well-known jour-
nalist, Charles Otho Montrose,
once professionally attended
a big Maori meeting at Whati-
whatihoe wearing an ancient
beiltopper with its nap in a
violent state of protest. Wa-
hanui, the Maori King’s
Prime Minister, made a re-
mark to Hori Grey about
Montrose which convulsed
the great pro-consul and a
number of listening Maoris.
“What did he say, Sir
George,” said Montrose, who,
though a good Maori linguist,
had missed the quip. “ Ah,
Montrose,” replied Grey, “he
has just nick-named you
‘ Munga-nui-potai-riri ’” (Big
black hat in a rage).
Kinnaird Rose, formerly
editor of Brisbane Courier, is
getting credit for good work
as a war - correspondent with
the Greeks. His solid qualities
and Scotch energy find a
better field “at the front ”
than in the Courier sanctum,
where he did not notably
shine.

look.” “The patriot's
ofty soul inspiring ! ”
While the late Melb.
City organist’s coffin was
being lowered into the
grave at the General
Cemetery, a spruce in-
dividual with soulful eyes
approached Mayor Strong,
who stood silently, hat in
hand. “ A terrible thing,”
whispered the stranger in
accents tremulous and
low, during a lull in the
burial service. “It is,”
replied Strong. The other
then fumbled in his pocket
and, shoving a card into
the hand of the Mayor,
observed hoarsely : “I am
a candidate for the posi-
tion of city organist ren-
dered vacant by this
calamity.” And,ereStrong
had time to collect words
suitable to the occasion,
the enterprising musician
was on the other side of
the grave buttonholing
a couple of councillors.
The Australian record reign LL.D. ’s should
be grouped in a special part of the University
calendars to keep the publio acquainted with the
circumstances in which the titles were given.
Many Australians who use the D.D. title received
it years ago when Trinity College, Dublin, con-
ferred it for merely commemorative purposes.
Dear Bulletin, —Your borrowed defini-
tion, “A newspaper man is one who wears two
shirts one week. A journalist is a man who wears
one shirt two weeks,” is not satisfactory. A
journalist is a man who prefers to wear no shirt at
all rather than don the unlaundried, second-hand
garment of a toad-eating and hypocritical boss.—S.
According to Sydney Truth, a discharged
confinee, who acted as Butler’s wardsman in
Dailinghurst, possesses a precious treasure —-
Butler’s whiskers, cut off at the prisoner’s own
request on the first day of his trial. “ I wrapped
them up, he says, “in this piece of paper, and
1 in going to get a good price for them from some
barber or other. I’m sorry T didn't keep the piece
of wadding that staunched the blood.”
Guy Boothby, in a recent instalment of
lus “ Prince of Swindlers, ” in Pkarson’s Maga-
ZTNE, shows his boss swindler airily getting away
at night with 50,000 sovereigns enclosed in a metal
casket. He has one man to heip him, and they
tread so lightly as they depart with the plunder
that no one in the house is disturbed. Fifty thou-
sand sovereigns weigh just about 7cwt., or over
one-third of a ton ; and next day the gorgeous
r* e, Y bt'hos all this to the station with the rest
of the luggage without noticing anything remark-
able. And yet Guy Boothby was once private
secretary to money-lender Cohen, of Adelaide !
However, perhaps he used to draw his salary in
notes—one note weekly. In recent .story
in an Fngnsh magazine, two reduced British swells
find a nugget in Australia. The first finder, in his
joy, throws it 20ft. in the air and catches it when it
comes down His mate says it is only mundio,
and disgustedly aims it at a passing crow. Finally
it proves to be almost solid gold, and they are rich
beyond the dreams of avarice. They had been
carelessly heaving half-a-ton of solid gold into the
atmosphere. Somehow the average compiler of
Australian horrors never can get it into his head
that gold is heavy, and when he starts to slam
together his crude goldfields shriek his hero is
always running for his life with 2cwt. of metal in
his waistcoat pocket, or swimming across a trou-
bled river with of it strapped round his
waist.
SYDNEY TO HIM.
(With apologies to Professor Marshall-Hall).
We wist you love us well, thou wight
Of honey-lipped, cupidous kisses.
In the drear-faced longing of earth’s far space
We have weirdly felt a presence and a yearning.
*Twas thee 1 We wust it not, but the bliss is ours.
Ah me, and ah us 1
Ah you, and ah thee, and ah men 1
SMIER.
SOMETHING WANTING.
There is something wanting sadly
In this diamond jubilee :
Folks are jubilating madly,
Millions go upon the spree ;
To south, east, and west, and nor’ward,
Gas-jets flame and guns explode ;
But Fitz Gibbon isn’t forward
With the customary ode 1
Serious Catastrophe Averted
Whenever a railway accident occurs,” said a
railway official, “people begin to talk of the
dangers of travel. They ask themselves which
route, or what part of the carriage, is the safest
and the timid bid each other a tearful good-bye
and purchase an insurance magazine before taking
the risk. Occasionally a derailment occurs yet
these are not the accidents which figure so largely
m making up the year’s loss of life.
The most horrible, heart-rending and cruel
catastrophes, however, are those so often resulting
from criminal carelessness. Those which by a
little foresight and care might easily be averted.
In travel by rail, there is, of course, a bit of
clanger, but not so much as is involved in the
every-day movements, pleasures, labors, "-oing
and coming of life at home.
The individual hair-breadth escapes; how
many we all have : some of which we may n«t
w eri i° e aware , a t least, until the danger is past
We have just had our attention called to one
which, unless averted, would certainly have re-
sulted in loss of life.
Mrs. Mary Ann O’Brien, Mariner’s Reef, Mary-
borough, Victoria, who is well-known, having li-
dded in the district over forty years, writes : “3
am pleased to state that Warner’s ‘Safe’ Cure
was of great value to me when laid up with in-
fluenza and low fever. I had to take to my bed
and for some time was unable to do my household
work, and became very low and depressed in
spirits. After trying various remedies of no
benefit, I was advised to try Warner’s * Safe ’
Oure, and did so, with wonderful results, for after
taking a few bottles of the medicine I began to
improve m health and spirits, and in a few weeks
was quite well again.”*

MELBOU RNIANA.


TO WHOM IT MAY CONCERN.—NO MORE PROSPECTING!


POLITICAL
POINTS
Q. Labor-party has been recessionally
howling for electoral reform. Boss Tozer proposes
to register all electors !
N.S. Wales charges only 3 per cent, suc-
cession duty on .£20,000. Queensland charges 10
per cent, on this sum, and Victoria 5 per cent.
Q. Parliamentary positions were unchanged
on the Opening Day. Ministerialists, disaffecteds,
cross-benchers, and Labor members all sat as of old.
N. : “As soon as an Australian leader
basks in the smiles of Britain, he begins to
dwindle. There is not even one exception to
prove the rule.”
A N.S.W. Minister of the Crown lately
said that, if Govt, popularised and cheapened
Hansard to Id., M.’sP. would deliver advertise-
ments as speeches.
Little Hawaii, which is now being taken
over by the U.S. and which lately refused to allow
a cargo of 400 Japs to land, has a total popula-
tion of 109,000—native Hawaiians, 31,000; Japs,
24,407; Chinese, 21,000 ; remainder, miscellaneous
foreigners.
It is fortunate for Acting-Premier Tozer,
in view of the publicly-strained relations between
the Gym pie law-firm of Tozer and Jamieson and
local warden Towner, that he should be able to
explain that the removal North of Mr. Towner is
a departmental promotion.
“THE BULLETIN'S" AMATEUR ART-
GALLERY.
NEW ANGLICAN BUSH PARSON: “Is he one of my
flock, madam, or one of Father Ryan’s ?”
M ADAM: "Indade, an’ he’s nayther; he’s Tim
O’Driscoll’s !”
In his speech “ agin ” Federation, Jawbone
Neild concluded by appealing to the N.S.W.
Freetraders to say whether they were prepared to
allow a Federal Parliament to place a burden of
£5,000,000 a year on the shoulders of the people
through the Customs-house and the excise officer.
Yet excise is a Freetrade device to counteract a
tariff levied for revenue purposes !
S.M. Herald old gag :
Thus the free breakfast table of the British citizen is to
be taxed unless he will consent to buy Queensland sugar
instead of the foreign product.
Now Britain, with big taxes on tea, coffee, cocoa
and chocolate, never had a “free breakfast table.”
The only genuine attempt at a free breakfast table
was N.S.W. Protectionist See’s foolish removal of
the tea-duty.
Q. Boss Tozer never let Gov. Lamington
say a word in his opening speech to Parliament
about “harbors and rivers.” Cos why? Cos his
mate, Treasurer Philp, not long ago made a
rousing speech in the North about how Brisbane
was a thief and a robber, and the Brisbane port
was making the North pay for its maintenance,
Some of the Labor members may be relied upon
to go at it, however.
The adjournment of N.S.W. Assembly
was lately moved by Frank Farnell to direct the
attention of the nation to the riotous behaviour of
dogs in the Centennial Park, Sydney. Then the
House wrestled with dog till it grew weary, and
finally, at Crick’s suggestion, the mongrel was
adjourned, having been talked of in every possible
aspect, and members proceeded with the compara-
tively trifling subject of Federation.
Leading-citizen Nathan, of
Aucklf .d, just back from Lon-
don, si.ys Maoriland is a laugh-
ing - stock in England. “It
would be a great blessing for us
all if this country were made
a Crown colony, managed by
a man of the Lord Cromer
stamp and half-a-dozen compe-
tent civil servants.” Lord—ah
—Cromer is at present, The
Bulletin believes, bossing the
Egyptians—a race against whom
the Nathan clan has a historical
grudge.
How the Q. Land Commis-
sion can justify its proposal to
let the tenure of grazing farms
remain at 30 years, instead of
limiting it to 21 years, is hard
to see. The Commission admits
that “such a reduction would
not limit the number of appli-
cants ”; but thinks it would
militate against improvements.
How so, when outgoers are
paid for all improvements ?
Many changes may happen in a
young, uprising country in 21
years, which is long enough
tenure in all conscience.
Germany s present con-
cern for the Boers is deeper than
is generally believed. The
founder of the Zollverein (Cus-
toms union) and the real maker
of the German empire, always
declared that Holland would be
necessary for Germany when,
having created her manufactures
by Protection, she would seek
to become a great maritime na-
tion. The succession question
in Holland is likely to be soon
complicated. So Germany is ap-
pealing to the Dutch through
their Boer relatives against her
only manufacturing and mari-
time rival —Britain.
An effort is to be made this session to
establish the Referendum in South Australia, and
to push through a Closer Settlement Bill. A law
of this kind, with power for the compulsory pur-
chase of large estates, is much needed also in
Yiotoria, Tasmania and N.S. Wales. What has
become of the shady-looking N.S.W. bill which
loomed up a year or more ago, and then faded
suddenly away without any explanation whatever?
Archibald, M.L.C. (Q.), seconder of the
address-in-reply, although a newly-turned creation
of the Nelsontozer lathe, was an irritating horse-
fly on some sore spots. He inveighed against
Tozer’s latest Federation countermarch, protested
against the importation of “ hordes of Japanese,”
and, anent the statement that the revenue would
exceed the expenditure, he remarked plainly : “I
don’t believe in selling Crown lands to provide
revenue. ”
S.A. Government will make another effort
this session to reduce the Leg. Council franchise,
and put that institution on a liberal basis. If one
or two Fat members happen to be laid up with
gout the measure should get through, but it will
be a tight fit. For a year or so the Liberal party
had a very small majority in the Council, but it
missed its opportunity, and now Fat is again
dominant. It isn’t quite the old, rampant Fat
party, but even with a moderate Fat party the
chances of reform are problematical.
“Between the Old Country and Australia,
her greatest colony, which is more English than
the mother, and ten times more fervently loyal, it
is difficult,” &c., &c.— (London Referee). Aus-
tralia is at last becoming famous everywhere for
the effusive “loyalty” of the men who run its
political, municipal and society shows. Consider-
ing the comparative lack of excuse for it there is
ten times more jubilee hysteria in this new
country at the present moment than in poor old
Hingland, which has Queen Victoria right under
its public nose.
N.S.W. OPPOSITION LEADER LYNE
The semi-ennobbled A. C. Gregory, M.L.C.,
holds an anomalous position in Q. Council. A
Tory of the Tories, his alleged politics is twin
with the Government’s; yet, by historical acci-
dent, and the stupidities of Government by con-
tradiction, he is nominal leader of the Opposition
in that cockloft. He only speaks thus, however,
as a matter of form, and never means anything.
In short, there is no “ Opposition ” in the Q.
Council, and, up to date, only a labor “Opposition”
in Q. Assembly, though the apparently smooth
surface may prove treacherous later on.
Probable that one of Q.
boss Tozer’s secret reasons for
releasing the Coombemartin
prisoners was to show his
great love for Attorney-Gene-
ral Byrnes, to demonstrate
that the latter’s bark was
worse than his bite, and to
prove that the violent opposi-
tion that Tom joe offered
against any suggestion of re-
lease was only assumed.

SONNT: “ We broke that winder with the ball !”
OLD G ENTLEMAN: “ Oh, never mind; perhaps the lady isn’t

SONNY : “ ’Tain’t that—she won’t give us the ball back !”
The explanations as to a recent alleged
“ irregularity ” by an Australian Minister con-
clusively prove that it is undesirable for a Minister
of the Crown to at one and the same time act as
agent for a lessee, and use his influence in securing
“ concessions ” from a brother Minister.
Britishah : “Desiring Queen Victoria to
live long, and not wishful of seeing the Prince of
Wales m the saddle, I protest against the mis-
chievous and killing excitement of this Jubilee
Hullabaloo. Suppose my mother was 78, would 1
desire her to be hawked even through the Melb.
streets amid shouting crowds ?
How the subject of Federation enthrals
N.S.W. Assembly ! “ Mr. Black spoke to empty
benches, which necessitated the bells being rung
two or three times in order to secure a quorum.
Mr. Black candidly admitted that he had notread
the Federal Convention debates, hence he was in
the dark as to the reasons which influenced the
Convention in agreeing to many of the provisions
in the bill. Precisely at midnight there were loud
cries of ‘adjourn,’ and two minutes later mem-
bers were hurrying down the street to catch their
trams.”—S.M. Herald.
Newell, M.P. for Woothakata (N.Q.),
snorted at the “ Governor’s-speech ” reference to
Lamington’s visit up North, and “hoped to see
the time when the Governor of Queensland would
have his permanent residence up North, and be
able to tell the Legislature how he had enjoyed a
visit to the South. ” This is the first parliamentary
toot on Northern Separation, and yet Newell was
the Tozer nominee to second the “ address-in-
reply.” Treasurer Philp should take the hint to
marshal his “ Northern Party ” and raise the
standard of revolt against the Rehoboam of
Queensland.
How reminiscent of the Capitoline wolf
with the twins at her dugs is the picture of the
kangaroo unearthed by Petherick and published in
connection with his alleged discovery of a “ past ”
for us ! In fact, one can imagine a myth-maker of
the future framing from this picture, aided by the
scraps of truth that chance will have saved from
the historian’s dye-pot, the story of one Melbulus,
deserted by his Moloch-worshipping mother,
Britannia, nurtured by a kindly kangaroo, and,
on reaching man’s estate, gathering around him
the outlaws of his race in an asylum at Botany
Bay, finally founding and giving his name to the
capital of Atlantis Resurgens.
At last, even the stammering sup-Press
Brisbane Telegraph admits that “it is under-
stood” that “Nevermind” Nelson “will not
return as Premier to Queensland ; or that, if he
should so return, it will be only that, with more
propriety, he may lay down his office to take up
another less responsible ’’—meaning, of course, the
Council’s Presidency. Govt, whip Hamilton is
now about the only M.P. who believes that Bully
Palmer won’t resign for anybody, and that Nelson
must wait till the old man dies. Meantime,
Griffith says sweetly to his own heart: “ Anyway,
if Nelson gets the Council Presidency, he won’t
get the Lieutenant-Governorship; that’s for the
Chief Justice.”
Half-a-dozen expressionless Chows were
caught by the North-Western Q. police crossing
surreptitiously from the Northern Territory of
S.A. into Q. via Camooweal. They were fined
,£5O apiece under the Act. Their six purses only
panned out £4, and “ allee same go to plison.”
But the police necessary to drag them the long
journey to Normanton could not be spared, and it
was sought to cause them to return to Port
Darwin. “ Mok-a-hi-lo! No feeah! Countly
all dly; no suey (water). Allee same go back;
allee same killee. Takee plenty plison and no go
back. Topside chow chow ! ” At time of writing,
Tozer was considering whether the only alterna-
tive was to set them at liberty !
Judging from the loud cackle which E. M.
Clark raises in nursing the St. Leonards (Sydney)
bike-vote, he is better fitted to represent a con
stituency with votes in its legs than one whoa*
votes are in its head.
The other day a deputation interviewed
the N.S.W. Minister for Works about the awful
condition of the road over the Blue Mountains,
and was curtly informed that it was inadvisable to
keep the said highway in repair, as it diverted
traffic from the railway. Premier Reid accused
his predecessors of shamefully neglecting their
duty by not voting enough money to keep the
roads in order, but the God-fearing Ministry haa
cut the vote down <£300,000 below Dibbs 1892
figures, and it now says it neglects the roads on
principle. If business has to be forced on to the
railways by making the roads impassable it would
seem that the meat horse is ahead of the iron one
when they compete on equal terms.
N.S.W. Parliament does not believe in the
old maxim de minimis non cur ant leges. Nothing
is too high or too low for it. Federation and race-
course betting, land settlement and the regulation
of dancing saloons ; in fact, anything from pitch
and-toss to dogs in the Centennial Park. But
what a commentary on its capacity for legislation
is this income-tax arrears muddle ! The Income-
tax Act, fathered by a Government four of whose
members are lawyers, and passed by Assembly and
Council, many of whose members are lawyers, is so
loosely framed on this particular point that two
“ eminent counsel ” (Campbell and Pring) give an
opinion one way, and two others (the Attorney-
General and C. B. Stephen) flatly contradict their
legal brethren.
Q. nigger-drivers recently received a nasty
knock from Griffith, C. J. Some 16 months ago—
in order to keep down the price of the “ cheap and
reliable ” —a number of kanaka regulations were
framed compelling time-expired boys to re-engage
at the close of one month ; laying also certain re-
strictions on the islander’s power of travelling
coastwise ; and imposing penalties on anyone who
harbored the time-expired, and on any captain
who conveyed him down or up the coast without a
permit. And now the Chief-Justice declares the
first of these regulations illegal, and gives a broad
hint that the others are in the same category,
wherefore, generally speaking, the Fatman is ra
the fire. Q. planters were just within hailing dis-
tance of a nice little Fugitive Slave Law, and now
discover with astonishment that though you may
employ very shady means to get a nigger into
Australia, there are yet some limits to your power
of handling him when here. And Tommy Tanna
'■nows it.
“HOW TO BE HAPPY THOUGH
MARRIED.”
The other night, in N.S.W. Assembly,
Opposition leader Lyne gravely rose to ask the
Acting-Premier
Whether it was the intention of the Government to sub-
mit to the House a congratulatory address to Her
Majesty on the record reisrn ? Similar addresses had been
arranged by one or two of the other Australian Parlia-
ments. If an address were adopted, it would have to be
passed that night. Mr. Brunker reminded Mr. Lyne that
the House had already decided to send an address to the
Queen, and that he (Mr. Lyne) seconded the motion to
that effect. (Great laughter.)
Which incident tends to oast a lurid light on the
formality, hollowness, and selfishness of the publio
Jubilee noise. Out-in-the-cold Lyne was quite
remembersome of his Gracious Sovereign’s jubilee
considered as a means of getting some kind of
kudos for himself and embarrassing the fellows
who held the loaves and fishes ; apart from that he
was utterly oblivious of the whole concern. Of
course, there are other alternatives.
Granuma for Health ,
Fodah saveth Wealth,
Hotnah for the very best of Bread ;
And, if long life you’d see,
Just stick to Billy Tea,
And — there’s really nothing further to be said,
Except try Qramma Biscuits.

N. : “The cooking apparatus of Herbert
Booth and co.’s Melb. Workmen’s Metropole has
had to be enlarged already, and the night before
last there were 170 sleepers.”
Commerce depends on two governing fac-
tors : the sale of commodities, and the receipt of
the cash (or other commodities) which they repre-
sent. In times of depression like the present, both
processes are difficult of accomplishment—espe-
cially the latter ! But merchants who can sell
their goods need not be apprehensive of getting
paid for them if they will hand their accounts to
the Victorian Debt - Collecting Agency (Holds-
worth & Co.), 101 Swanston-st., Melbourne.*
There is some trouble in Melbourne over a
ship with an alibi. Captain Fisher, of the Or-
lando, alleges that he was nearly run into by the
steamer Time, and it was only by promptly going
astern that a collision was averted. The captain
of the Time responds that he was so far away
from the Orlando that she could only be identified
with a telescope, and the distance between them
was never less than five miles. The local Marine
Board reckons that somebody must have been
drinking, but can’t quite make out who it was—or
else that the Orlando was frantically dodging a
*i i irage.

At the instance of a Deutscher clergyman
named Kaibel, who is good enough to violently
disapprove of The Bulletin’s opinions, the com-
mittee of Natimuk (Vic.) Mechanics’ Institute
has decided to exclude this paper from its reading-
room. The Bulletin proprietary, which, like
the rev. Mr. Kaibel, keeps at least one eye on the
collection-plate, absolutely loathes those who read
this valuable organ on the cheap, and is much
obliged to the ghostly comforter named ; it will
be glad, as a token of gratitude for his godly
exertions, to send The Bulletin free for a year to
him or anyone he may nominate.
Bendigo Art Gallery—which, by the way, the
W.C.T.U. is now trying to boss with a view to
the expulsion of a very innocent nude entitled
“ The'Spanish Ma-iden ” (the flat-chested sister-
hood declares the picture to be “an insult to the
womanhood and motherhood of Bendigo ”) —is
associated with the local library, and in the latter
institution there is a wondrous course pursued re-
garding this journal. The library committee
allowed The Bulletin on the reading-room table,
but only after it had passed a special committee
of expurgation, whose duty it is to carefully black
out, on the Russian principle, all “objectionable ”
utterances. After a trial this system was found
unworkable —so much, presumably, had to be
blacked out—and the committee was empowered
to reject whole copies deemed capable of injuring
the glorious cause of Respectability as the word is
understood at Bendigo. Now, The Bulletin re-
joices to state, no one is ever sure that he will be
able to read this paper free at Bendigo library.
A Boon to Sufferers.—A well-known stock-
dealer and storekeeper announces that, if written
to confidentially, he will reply and state the plan
he pursued by which he was restored to health
and vigor after years of suffering from Nervous
and Physical Weakness, Loss of Vigor, etc. He
writes i “ I have no scheme to extort money from
anyone whomsoever. I was victimised by quacks
and empirics until I lost faith in mankind ; but,
thank heaven, I am now well, vigorous, and
strong, and anxious to make this certain means of
cure known to all. Having nothing to sell, I
want no money.” Address—J. KILIAN, Store-
keeper, Daveyston, S.A.*

THE AUSTRALIAN GEM TRADE.
“ I -would lead the Turks anywhere, to do
anything."— Williams, of Kars.
The other week, in an outlying region of
Victoria, a J.P. was requested to call at the near-
est apology for a police-court and deal with one
small case of “drunk and disorderly.” He sent
back word that the culprit might as well be carted
to his private residence, and, after a lapse of 36
hours or so, the case duly came on. The prisoner,
quite sober by this time, arrived in the custody of
the arresting constable, who now happened to be
very drunk, so whilst the prosecutor was hiccup-
ping his evidence the prisoner winked at the
“beak,” and the “beak” gazed at the ceiling.
Then, having heard the evidence, he besought the
prisoner (discharged without the option of a fine)
to see the constable back to the township, and
take warning by his awful example.
H. Newman, the well-known jeweller of 175
Elizabeth-street, Melbourne, is the largest buyer
of opal in Australia, and opal miners will find it
to tneir advantage to send their opal direct to
him. The firm has a reputation for straight deal-
ing extending over a period of 40 years, which is
a sufficient guarantee to consignors that they will
be honorably treated. H. Newman is also a
buyer of all other Australian gems.*
Viator: “Dr. Singleton, that often mis-
chievous but ever even Christian, used to make a
good point, urging that in Melb. no thought is
given to lodgings for poor women, whereas the
law-maker of the Creation caters most abundantly
and cheaply for himself. So in all other Austra-
lian cities. Now Melb. has its Workmen’s Metro-
pole, with men’s 3d. beds, nice and snowy. Where
can a woman get any such a bed, even for a shil-
ling? Let me give two examples of Melb. experi-
ence. One night, about 10, a poor sick woman,
not old, crippled by an accident, hardly able to
drag her bandaged foot before the other, appealed
to me, and the doors seemed to be of brass, even
before money. We walked at least a mile ere
any would open to her. Another time, near mid-
night, I was appealed to by a woman with baby
in arms, and another child at foot. Her husband
had failed to keep an appointment to meet her,
on arrival of the last train from far the bush.
After a sickening lot of refusals, ‘No women
taken in here,’ although I offered ample payment,
Is. 6d., she had to be content with a respectable
Little Bourke-street hotel, as a great favor. And
let me add that proprietors of great hotels do all
they can to evade giving lodgings, except to people
who will drink.”
The Eye-sight measure per post by the in
ventors, Messrs. Greenfield and Barraelough, the
well-known opticians of George-st., Sydney, enables
eye-sufferers in the country to ascertain the data
necessary for obtaining proper spectacles. The
subtler forms of sight-defect cannot, of course, be
measured by the instrument, but it is thoroughly
effective for the more usual forms of eye affection —
long sight, short sight, old sight, 1 ull particulars
from Messrs. Greenfield and Barraelough.’
Melb. Dental Board is now worrying Govt,
for a bill to amend the Dental Act, so as to enable
the board to fix a curriculum for the students’ ex-
amination. This is a long-felt want which the
enterprising E. L. Oldfield pointed out previous
to starting the Dental College of Victoria ‘ on
his own,” with the result, of course, that. he lost
his seat on the board at the first opportunity.

The late “ Billy ” Jenkins tried, over two years ago, and
without success, to form an advertising syndicate in Mel-
bourne, to work an innovation of his, which consisted in
opeiating with a perambulating limelight. After dark,
Jenkins and Co. Ltd. were to drive along the busiest city
streets in a trap, containing the paraphernalia necessary
to project the various ads. in enormous letters upon the
upper (and, of course, unlighted) storeys of houses en
route. Speculators, however, wouldn’t bite at remunera-
tive rates. Now, in Paris, a co. is coining money by
pavement flashlights, projected by a camera with slides
moved by clockwork, situated on the third floor of a
central bureau. The law, as at present constituted, is
powerless against this startling invasion of the privaoy of
the peripatetic philosopher.
Talking of “ Billy ” Jinkins, deceased, and his com-
mercial style —is the old type of sealskin-collared showman
quite extinct ? The showman who, not being an actor
himself, aspired to look like ten gaudy actors rolled into
one, even as the purser of a passenger steamer endeavors
to appear more nautical than all the navigating staff—
what has become qf that appalling identity? Geo. Tallis,
the Firm’s business manager, is strictly unpurfeshional at
all points. Louis Lohr might be a squatter, and Edwin
Geach the bookkeeper on his station : Fred Aydon, with
Rickards in Melbourne, is a sombre, undemonstrative
man who has an air of being engaged in hatching a new
variety of steam engine, and Bland Holt’s lieutenants are
likewise indifferent to limelight effects. Not one of the
crowd would look happy with a fur-lined cloak and half-
a pound of castor-oil in his hair.
Next to Coroner Youl, Melb.’s most comic institution is
the Rose of Denmark Lifeboat crew, a brass band which
performs much on all public rejoicings. The members
are uniformly attired in blue serge sailors’ toggery, and,
between performances, they swagger around in real
nautical style. As a matter of fact, the so-called lifeboat
crew hails from the inland suburb of Collingwood, and the
majority of the members have never been on the sea in
their lives. The deeds of daring they are understood to
perform must necessarily take place on that noble stream,
the Reilly-street drain.
“ The Growler ” on the English-company question :
'* Dear Bulletin, —Re the comparative failure of ‘ Zenda,’
one begins to doubt the wisdom of even such an experi-
enced stager as George Musgrove in sending out a com-
pany the ability of which could certainly be equalled by
native-born actors. It may be argued that, assuming the
Australians could play the piece as well as those imported,
they would not draw; but, on the other hand, did the
Englishmen draw? Surely such a run was not a brilliant
one, even in these ‘ busted ’ times ? Australian actors are
too rarely seen in a brand-new drama, with ‘ all appliances
and means to boot.’ They generally get their chance in a
dreadful something ‘by arrangement with W. J. Hol-
loway.’”
“ It is curious,” writes “ Pat ” to The Bulletin, “ that
no play of literary, or even lasting, merit by an Aus-
tralian author has ever been originally produced in Aus-
tralia.” Not a curious thing. There is no money for the
author in an original Australian production, and there
has been no successful Australian playwright, save Haddon
Chambers.
Another reverend slanderer of the stage has made the
notable discovery that the actor must' inevitably be
utterly demoralised “ by having to impersonate the worst
passions of human nature.” At which rate, the mummer
who has to be histrionically of superhuman and incor-
ruptible virtue should eventually develop into a high-
grade angel. Perhaps alternate doses of heavy villain
and immaculate hero would about serve to balance
things; though, as far as The Bulletin’s experience goes,
the avenger of injured innocence is seldom any more
saintly than he has any need for, and the First Murderer
is mostly about as mild and harmless as they make ’em.
Paradis : “ Howard Yernon, taking that Thespic chame-
leon, Melb. Alexandra, changes the name from Her
Majesty’s to the Queen’s, reviving memories of Melb.’s
first noteworthy dramatic temple, where Coppin and
Brooke made their successive debuts. Why not call it
the National ? Are we to be for ever choked with Royal,
Princess’s, Queen’s ? ”
Haresfoot: “ How is it that never an Hebraic name
appears on the stage ? Theatric genius is saturated with
Hebrewism, concealed under Smith, or a much more
sonorous name of Gentile-ty. Now young Whelan, the
clever mimic, in the Melb. Princess Co., turns out to be
Waxman, but must needs be Irish. The Kembles, includ-
ing Mrs. Siddons, were all Jews, Solomon or something,
and the interminable catalogue comes even down to little
Flegeltaub, who won’t allow she has two other names.”
A brother of Elizabeth and Henrietta Watson is on hi 3
way out to Australia to stage-manage “ The Geisha
Musical Comedy Co.” which M. and M. have engaged.
Britishah: “I wonder if Williamson and Musgrove
would stage my Original Jubilee Patriotic National Play,
entitled ‘ Victoria and Albert ?’ In the first act—all being
in blank verse, by the way—l show Cumberland as the
villain, employing his machinations to substitute himself
for the fair young Princess Alexandrina Victoria, her full
name, while Greville burrows away as the industrious
chronicler. Then comes the Coronation, which obviously
whacks all precedent, in spectacle, with Queen Victoria
riding on a white horse through London City. In act 3
I treat with suitable delicacy the wooing of Prince Albert
by Queen Victoria, according to royal rule, after which
comes the Marriage ! There’s a finale for you. West-
minster Abbey, or St. George’s, Windsor, according to
choice.”
Carlyle Smythe, who is still in England, has an excel-
lent criticism in the Argus on Ibsen’s last play, “ John
Gabriel Borkman,” recently produced in London by the
New Century Theatre Co. The cast included Genevieve
Ward, Elizabeth Robins, Mrs. Beerbohm Tree, W. H.
Vernon and James Welch.
R. S. Smythe is a very devout man, but not
a bigot, and if he thinks a celebrity will draw
more money in a theatre than in a hall, he puts
him there. So when the agent of Sydney Royal ad-
vised him that he could have that house for Harry
Furniss, Smythe wired back that he would take it,
but he was afraid that many people did not care to visit
a theatre. The agent wired that the unco guid loved the
Theatre Royal. That is, he intended to send that
message, but the intelligent female telegraph-clerk de-
clined to transmit it, on the ground that the words
“ unco guid ” were not English, and they were not
French, as she had looked in the dictionary, so they must
be cypher words. The telegraph employes fortunately
included a Scotchman, and the message was sent.
Music-teachers should not be allowed to write musical
criticisms re their professional brethren and sisters who
also teach, any more than teaching or practising artists
should be allowed to select pictures for the National
Gallery.
Jules Simonsen has a farewell-benefit at Sydney Town
Hall on July 3 prior to his final departure for Enniore, or
some place.
Two large photographs in one frame form a de-
pressing memento in the d.c. entrance of Melb. Theatre
Royal. One shows poor little Violet Varley as Peep 80,
and the other Arthur Dacre in a “ Bunch of Violets.”
While the backbone of “The Sign of the Cross” is
Massinger’s Virgin Martyr, its ribs are from Corneille’s
“ Polyeucte,” operatised by Gounod.
SPORTING NOTIONS .
Handicapper Dakin must have a poor opinion
of the “form ” shown in last year’s G.N. Hurdle Race at
Flemington. Buzzi and Zouroff, the win ner and runner-up,
are let in again this time at 9st. 111 b. and9st. 101 b., an
advance of only lllb. and 41b. respectively. Yet both
horses have been going on well of late.
* * * *
The Ballarat gelding So-and-So strikes one as a
fairly dangerous candidate for the G.N. Hurdle Race. A
handy son of Fryingpan, he can go very fast on the flat
and jump cleverly. Whether he can stay three miles
under the burden of list. 21b. is a question for the betting-
market to answer after he has been tried.
* » * •
Cedric, a jumper from up-country that was ex-
pected to shine at Flemington next month, has been
scratched for the G.N. Hurdle and Steeple.
* * * *
N.S.W. hurdler, Jack Hanwoll, has been very
nicely treated in V.R.C. Grand National with lOst. 91b.,
and as he has never before been in such good plight as he
is now, he should have a big say on Saturday next.
His most dangerous opponents appear to be his com-
patriots, Irish Stew and His Grace, whilst the best of
Messrs. Manifold and Miller should be in the leading
division. In the big Steeplechase, Larrikin, Tagus, Cam-
paspe, and Vigil are a quartet of likely-looking customers.
* * * *
Bookmakers are themselves rapidly killing the
“ little double ” by the extremely short prices they lay.
Both in N.S.W. and Vic. a hundred to one is the offer for
the Grand Nationals, whereas ’tis a good 400 —1 against
anyone, barring a Delphic oracle, spotting the two
winners.
• * • *
A sample of the prices on offer for forthcoming
races:—G.N. Hurdles, 10 to 1 each, Soult, Black Harry,
Tagus, and So-and-So ;12to 1, Don John; 15 to 1 any-
thing else that has a “paper” chance. G.N. Steeple,
6 to 1, Larrikin ; 10 to 1, Doondiah, Vigil and Dondi, with
12 to 1 against anything else that can raise a gallop.
V.R.C. Derby, 6 to 4 against Aurum ; 6to 1, Fleet Admiral;
10 to 1 bar two. What earthly, or any other, show, have
backers of holding their own at sur-h a game ?
• * » *
The well-known N.S.W. sprinter, Moorefield
(Cheviot —Bengal Light), was fearfully cut about in his
box whilst being trained to Rosehill last Saturday. This
was a bitter blow to his owner, who greatly fancied the
horse’s chance for the Cup, over which event he had been
laid a most satisfactory portion of the big “ sweep.”
Doubtful whether Moorefield will ever be able to race
again.
* * * *
At the Rosehill (N.S.W.) meeting last Saturday
betting was conducted as briskly as of yore. Unfor-
tunately, a false start, from which a number of competi-
tors ran the whole course, somewhat discounted the
chances of several well-backed ones for the Cup, which
fell to the much-improved Kelso (Kingsborough—a Fop
mare). The handicapper well concealed the winners, as
can be seen by the following starting-prices : —6—1,12 —1,
20 —1, 6 —l, 10—1. In the Cup another Australian record
was established, as no less than 43 horses faced the start-
ing-machine.
• * * *
Even in sport John Bull is being cut out of the
foreign markets. The Paris Grand Prize was this year
won by the home-bred Doge, and it is suggestive that for
the past 11 years the French have managed to keep the
money in the country.
* * * *
Melb. councils again interview Ministers with
reference to attempted “ steal ” of public rights by the
V.R.C., with as little result as usual. The public has
used Flemington Flat as a park for 35 years, but now
fences have been put up, and visitors must get a permit
from a lodge-keeper, who will probably soon be ordered
not to be at home when anyone calls. The object of the
club’s move is said to be in the direction of charging ad-
mission to the Flat on race-days. The present committee
denies the alleged intention, but then it isn’t perpetual.
The original deed from the Crown and the Act of ’7l cer-
tainly appear to give power to Charge for admission, hence
the necessity of the public’s insisting on any rights it has
acquired by prescription or otherwise. An awkward
thing for the public is the fact that the club has numerous
secret allies in the municipal bodies themselves. Isaacs
gave a temporising answer, so that the club will probably
score again in the final.
• * * •
R. B. Echlin, manager of Charleville (Q.) Times
has invented a new totalisator and scratching board,
which Rockhampton Jockey Club, having tested, seems
inclined to buy. The tote is on the “ block” system, and
the ticket-seller doesn’t retain possession of the money,
but, standing in an enclosure outside, merely receives it
and passes it on to the man in the office, who immediately
registers it on the board outside by means of a mechani-
cal, automatic counting-machine. When a race starts,
the board, by means of the window-weight system, is
pushed up out of reach of anyone. The scratching board
is so fitted that, as horses are scratched, their names are
made to disappear without taking down the board.
* * • •
Ed. Bulletin, — Here, in Maoriland, polo be-
comes more popular every season ; but why does it seem
necessary to crop the manes and dock the tails of ponies
in such tar-brush fashion ? The local English chappies
declare that they would not ride at the game unless their
mounts were docked ; one of them vowed he would per-
form the operation, even if he had to steal an axe. The
“ colonial” players must be weak down to the knees, for
they imitate this cruel practice, in the same absurd way
that M.L. jockeys are adopting English flapping-sail
breeches and billy-pot boots. Do the Prevention of
Cruelty to Animals’ societies not know what horses’ manes
and tails are for ?—Carrington Smith.
* * »
If racing is to be pursued at all, it will be a big
mistake to wipe out the ponies altogether. There would
be nothing the matter with a properly-'-'".itested pony-
meeting each Wednesday by the pro tary clubs in
turn, barring the soup - plate cours ;. Breeders of
thoroughbred stock find the game none too good with the
sale of ponies and galloways thrown in, and it will mean
a big thing to them if ail their stunted stock, though
bred on lines equal to anything on four legs, are thrown
upon their hands as worthless.

The names of ’Stralian horses at ’Ome are to be
included in future issues of the exclusive Stud Book,
but, as in Newhaven’s case, explanatory notes are to be

Cantle : “ Apropos the departed Sam Davis,
someone catalogues the yore-time Australian jockeys.
What a lesson to the present woefully decadent genera-
tion 1 Sam Waldock, the man of the perfect hands ;
Jimmy Ashworth, on Tait’s Volunteer, steady as a die;
Cutts, the straight pilot of Archer, the racehorse ; Hig-
gerson, the incomparable pigskinist of Verio and De
Mestre’s Tarragon ; Sam Holmes, for ever identified with
Tomboy ; Joe Morrison, who ferried Charon to his vic-
tories, and so on.”
* * * *
In a certain Queensland town an ancient
clergyman requested his parishioners to hold a race-
meeting on St. Patrick’s Day in aid of the church funds.
The O’Briens and the O’Houlahan 3 , the O’Neils and the
O’Flahertys decided that such a meeting should be held.
The rights in connection with the day’s sport were dis_
posed of to Catholics —who paid more than the value of
same just for the good of the church ; and his reverence
went to the races, and perched himself close to the
booth—the better to take stock of those members of his
flock who visited it too often. The result was that the
O’Briens and the O’Houlahans swear they never spent
such a dry day since the local brewery went bung, and
the bhoy who bought the booth swore so much at his
pastor that he has since been afraid to go to confession.
•** • •
At a recent W.A. back country race-meeting all
the ladies were black. Fanny, in a white petticoat hung
over her shoulders ; Polly, in a man’s shirt; Biddy, with
a pair of man’s trousers tied round her neck, and several
in the back-ground with nothing on.
• • » •
Amid all the racecourse betting prosecutions
Chinese gamblers in Sydney are enjoying a well-earned
rest. One afternoon lately' writer counted six fan-tan
and pak-ah-pu shops in full swing near Belmore Markets,
and within a radius of 100yds. On the opposite side of
the street strolled the oblivious policeman.

The N.S.W. cast-off, Dora, continued her win-
ning career by annexing the Cambria Plate at Caulfield
last Saturday ; she was followed home by Lady' Loch and
Beau Brummel, both ex-Sydneyites. Two other Walers,
Lieutenant and Athlone, also ran in the field of 10. Vic.
is drawing heavily on the mother province for her race-
horses.

The valuable Ascot Cup was won last week
by the Prince of Wales’ Derby and St. Leger victor,
Persimmon (St. Simon —Perdita II.), who easily defeated
the heavily-backed Cambridgeshire and Lincoln Handicap
winner, Winkfield’s Pride (Winkfield —Alimony)—a decided
victory for classic over handicap form.

The S.A. lacrosse team, which visits Melb. in
the beginning of July, has a goalkeeping marvel in Julian
Ayers, one of the stoutest athletes in Australia. Ayers,
although he weighs about I6st., is an active cricketer,
tennis-player and lacrosseur.
* • • •

have adopted Melbourne as their home, are likely to be
chosen in the Vic. lacrosse team.
* * * *
Billiard-champion John Roberts has been put
on the London financial market as “ John Roberts,
Limited,” with a capital of £70,000, the idea being to ex-
ploit his skill with the cue. So Gilbert, in “ The Gon-
doliers,” was a prophet, when he floated the Duke of
Plaza-Toro. The risks are, of course, a sudden “go off”
in form or death of Roberts. The former cannot be pro-
vided against, but they have insured the human asset
against death in three years for £30,000.
* * • •
The German billiard champion, Herr Karkau,
would seem to be even a finer cannon-play’er than Ives,
the ’Murkan. Playing in London lately' on a (presum-
ably) English pocket-table, and with (presumably) 2 l-16in.
balls, Karkau scored 700 cannons at his first break. He
worked the balls nearly three times round the table, com-
pleting the 700 cannons in 36min. If this sort of thing
can be done under English billiard conditions (but was it ?)
the old style of game is doomed. The greatest proficiency
in the art of nursery-cannoning can only be acquired by
practice on the small, originally French, table, so when
the small-table champions can adapt their skill to the big
tables, avoiding the pocket pitfalls, there’s an end to the
English “hazard” striker’s supremacy at his own game.
Already Roberts has gone a long way towards reducing
the “ all-round ” game to a mere manipulation of cannons.
* * * *
The threatened football revival in Melb. and
Adelaide comes as a shock to hotelkeepers. The average
visiting twenty, when they descend upon a city, generally
play blue sheol. The writer can recall three instances—
two in Adelaide, one in Melb. In one case the visiting
team wrecked four hotels at which they successively
stayed ; in the other, a course of rioting ended in the
pub. being surrounded by the police, and the offenders
being locked up in the cells to recover. In another case,
in Melb., the details were utterly unfit for publication.
Collingwood, the premier Melb. football team,
played South Adelaide what was virtually an Australian
premiership match on Adelaide Oval, June 12. Colling-
wood kicked 8 goals 5 behinds (53 points) to 6 goals 4
behinds (40 points). Melb. football teams always beat
Adelaide twenties in the handball game, and this match
was no exception. The play' was rough and the language
at times sulphurous—facts hailed by supporters as an
indication of a revival in the noble sport. The attendance
was 5000—a record for Adelaide for two vears. Umpire
Coffee, by the way, complained of the “ rough behavior
and blackguardly language of several of the players
towards him. ’ Truly, football is reviving : umpires will
soon again have to carry guns and hire police escorts.

When the amateur cyclist leaves the lily-white
ranks and becomes a pro. he is usually a verv strong pro
indeed. Some of the most daring heat-rigs during last
season’s Melb. cycle-racing were “ put up ” by ex-
shamateurs.
* * * *
How they handicap cycle road-races in Melb.
In the E. Melb. road-race recently, A. A. Middleton did
the distance (20 miles) in what is reputed to be world’s
record-time (55min., out and back, unpaced), and yet
failed to get a place, the winner, with 14min. start, doin'-
the journey in 61min. Which means that to win Mid”,
would have had to wheel in something under 49min.
* * * *
Amateur cycling in Melbourne is a truly comic
affair, and columns are drivelled over the thrillin'- news
that Mick Mosenstein punctured his tyre four miles from
Footscray on Saturday, or that Ishmael Savinsky’s copious
nose has not entirely recovered from its collision with
the earth on Tuesday week. But the climax was reached
when a cycling journal published a full-page photo, print
of the soulful, inuch-bebarbered youth who runs the
amateur department in the same rag, accompanied bv a
two-col. interview with “ our amateur editor.” One of
the exclamations of surprise used by the interviewee is
Oh diamonds ! which, as the soulful scribe is a
• Ve^' da , y , ': fe ’. is particularly appropriate.
Had he been in the old clo line he would have probably
remarked “Oh, veskits!” 1 y
®y the way, what is the connection between
Orientalism and amateurism ? Though the professional
cycling trades are entirely destitute of the Chosen, the
average amateur meeting resembles the eagle cage at the
Zoo There is only one inference, and that is that there
is more cash in amateurism than there is in professionalism.
