Several Tas. country pub.-keepers now offer
prize-trophies (value, £2 to £5) for cycle races to be run
to their doors. About live o’clock 30 odd cyclists hot and
dusty thirsty and tired, will land in Bung's verandah
with thirsts worth pounds. The whole township will be
collected in front of Bung’s door; also, the majority of
the riders will stay to tea and ride back by moonlight.
Result to publican : Good business, good advt., and
splendid increase of reputation for generosity, likewise,
with luck, portrait in the weekly paper.
If sculler Wray makes a pile in England, Lam-
bert, of the Nepean, now married and in retirement,
missed a chance. He could have had plenty of backing in
England, for he got a splendid advt. when Bubear, who
is supposed to know the ropes, barred him in a challenge
to the world, including Stanbury.
* * * *
The mother of the well-known Punch family, of
Sydney, died last week aged 90. Of eleven children, five
sons still survive.
« * * •
Sculler “Mick” Rush was asked by a fellow
Irishman on the morning of his final race with Trickett
on the Parramatta whether the race was to be a straight
one, as he wanted to put his money on it. “ Yes,” said
Rush, “ it is to be as straight as that,” and with a mighty
blow he laid the questioner on the grass.—Ex.
* * • •
Wray seems to have I.ad a very easy win ovet
Haynes on the Tyne course ; he led shortly after the start,
and was never afterwards asked to row. Wray now chal-
lenges the world over the Tyne. Gaudaur is not likely to
rush matters, and Wray’s most likely opponent looks to be
Towns, his fellow-Australian. Towns’ showing with
Chris. Neilson, however, scarcely places him upon a foot-
ing with Wray.
• • • •
Boxers Maher and Sharkey met recently in
New York, but the police stopped the fight. Both men
are smashing hitters, Sharkey being of the rough-and-
tumble order —a bad sort when the police are “down ”on
the game. He must be a tough customer, having now
met three of the very best and biggest men —Fitzsim-
mons, Corbett, and Maher—without sustaining defeat.
* * * *
Australian boxer Billy Maber is in gaol in
America, awaiting trial for an attempt to murder. Bar-
ring a partiality-for fire-water, Billy was, when in Aus-
tralia, the makings of a very poor murderer. Perhaps J.
Hennessy was at the bottom of the trouble.
* * * •
Steve O’Donnell has gone into the saloon biz. in
the States, and it is to be hoped he will give the ring best,
for he was ever a good boxer and a poor fighter. A very
clever lithographic printer, he tried long and hard to get
employment in the Sydney Government Printing Office,
and it was only for absolute want of something better to
do that he went to the States and tackled pugilism.
» • * *
Young Rodd, who graduated under the pheno-
menal George Scale, is now in England, and may try his
hand on some of the English amateurs. Rodd is a splen-
didly-built young fellow, and if he shows up to his
Australian form should make some of them sit down.
As The Bulletin anticipated, Major Jaw-
bone Neild’s “grand patriotic oration” at the
Sydney Town Hall concert made the miserable
audience snort. Jawbone is now busy denying
press statements that he was cut short by the
audience’s restiveness ; he claims that he succeeded
in getting it all off his chest.
Melb : “ On Jubilee Sabbath, one panted
for a corner to worship God. ”
Will some member of N.S.W. Assembly
kindly call for an official list of the previous con-
victions of the man sentenced to three years’ hard
labor for larceny of a ninepenny tin of baking-pow-
der, in order that the public may judge for itself
as to the validity of Judge Gibson’s excuse that
the sentence owed its severity to the prisoner’s
incorrigibility.

Westward, ho ! Sixty bulls are being
shipped at Newcastle this week for the land of
the Black Swan. They were bred by the A.A.
Co., Gloucester, and purchased at eight guineas
per head, through Pitt, Son and Badgery, Ltd.,
by a prosperous Kimberley cattle-owner. This is
the second year the A.A. Co.’s bulls have been
sold to go to Westralia, through the characteristic
enterprise of the agents in following up new
outlets.*
A GRAND LITERARY INSTITUTION.
Cole’s Book Arcade now runs from the centre
of the block in Bourke-street to the centre of the
block in Collins-street, Melb., a distance of 660 ft.,
and contains the following 17 departments, mostly
literary and artistic: 1, New Books; 2, Second-
hand Books ; 3, Stationery; 4, Music ;5, Eancy
Goods ; 6, Toys; 7, Perfumery; 8, Pictures ;9,
Artists’ Materials ; 10, an Ornamental Exhibition;
11, a Japanese Exhibition ; 12, a Wonderland ;
13, a Smiling Gallery; 14, a Child’s Picture
Gallery ; 15, a Bargain Room ; 16, a Tea Room ;
17, a splendidly arranged new Wholesale Book
Department, containing half-a-million books. The
main walks of the establishment are half-a-mile
long.*
The proprietors of the “ Three Castles ”
cigarettes—“ Three Castles” tobacco was eulogised
by Thackeray—have got on the Jubilee racket
both plain and colored; They have prepared
a series of brilliantly - coloured portraits of
the English inonarchs of the last eight
centuries—presumably taken by Falk or some
other very old-established photographer—and
one glittering monarch is enclosed in each
packet of “ Three Castles ” cigarettes. The pur-
chaser, of course, has no choice in the matter ; he
buys his packet and gets his monarch, and if the
potentate served out to him was a weak, diseased
person who died amid general execration that is
his misfortune.*

When “Rev. George Stewart, of N.S.
Wales,” assured the Assembly of the Free Church
of Scotland the other day that horse-racing is the
cancer of Australia, he forgot to mention that it
only affects six days a week, and that the cancer
of the seventh is the Calvinistic hypocrisy which
seeks to make the Sabbath a liell-upon-earth.
Look at the Melbourne Sunday !
The “busted” Melb. Harbor Trust now
acknowledges that the dredging of the Yarra and
the building of wharves and docks along that
dreadful stream was all a mistake. The harbor
and the docks should be where Nature intended
them to be—at Port Melbourne and Williains-
town, and the Yarra should have been left to its
own devices all along. It is a trifle late to think
about that, now, however. The present trouble is
that the Trust is practically insolvent, and that,
despite its ruinous exertions, the silt has for some
years past been depositing faster than it could be
removed. Therefore, the Yarra is getting back to
its original condition, and as no one has suggested
a day of prayer and humiliation for the deepening
of the river it is difficult to say what will happen.
Ah Ming, of Little Bourke-streefc, Melb.,
got a release of his estate from insolvency, the
other week. The heathen was not represented by
a solicitor —he couldn’t afford it—but he put in an
affidavit stating that he was in a large way of
business until his customers fell victims to depres-
sion. He was finally broken up through endorsing
Chong Hing’s promissory-notes, and now, being
refused further time to meet his engagements, he
was compelled to file —“ a course I was very sorry
to take,” said Ah Ming, in his affi., “as it is
looked upon by my fellow-countrymen as a great
i disgrace.” Judge Molesworth said he was glad to
I hear that Chinese merchants regarded the in-
! ability to pay 20s. in the £ as a disgrace. The
Bulletin would have told the abashed heathen
not to worry. He is merely moving with civili-
sation :
A WORD OF SYMPATHY.
Why hide your yellow face, Ah Ming',
Why hide your face so yellow,
And brood on your “ disgrace,” Ah Ming?
’Tis not disgrace, old fellow.
’Tis no disgrace, in any case,
To cast aside your trading debts
In Melbourne. Stow your sad regrets,
Absurd repiner !
You dwell among a Clistian race ;
The city of the missing inace,
Where honesty seems out of place,
Is not like China.
Breathe not the old-world wheeze, Ah Ming—
We are not taking any.
Our boomsters live in ease, Ah Ming,
Who paid their “ bob,” or penny.
Look cheerful, please 1 it’s quite the cheese
To file your schedule, allee same
As they, who feel no sense of shame —
Restore your smirk sweet 1
And tell your friends heyond the seas
That “ going bung” (the new disease)
Has spread, from men of high degrees,
To Lilly Buck-street.

The A.N.A. Board of Directors has gone
cap in hand to the A.N.A. metropolitan com-
mittee, begging the latter to do the splashing for
the association in the Melb. jubilee procession.
What is the quid pro quo ? Disloyal anti-gamblers
in the association phrase it thusly : “ You do our
grovel, we’ll tolerate your gamble.”
Melbourne has landed another leper- A
Ping—who has been living for some time in the
heart of the city, only venturing out to take air
after dark. Acting upon information, the ponce
took Ping from his washtub, and he is now at tne
lazaret. Ping was washing a shirt when arreste ,
and the arresting constable said: 1 here were
sores on his hands, with the typical hard lump
between the thumb and forefinger, always presen
in true leprosy.” The Melbourne girl should abso-
lutely refuse'to rest her golden head upon any
local manly bosom, until she sees its propnetoi s
laundry-certificate.

Rheumatism Held the Fort »
A 15 YEARS’ SIEGE.
DR. WILLIAMS’ PINK PILLS TO THE
RESCUE AND THE ENEMY ROUTED.
Mr. Ed. Barsley, of 19 Waverley-road, Wool-
lahra, said a reporter who was sent to interview
him :
“lama pianoforte tuner by profession, and am
well-known throughout the colony ; this latter fact
should add weight to my statements.
“ Now, to deal with the subject of your visit. I
have on and off for the last 15 years been a martyr
to rheumatism and, indeed, have quite recently
recovered from a very severe attack My discovery
is that Dr. Williams’ Pink Pills for Pale People are
an unfailing remedy for this torturing disease, and
to emphasise my case I must tell you that each of
my ailments —for I had more than one, as you will
learn later—were so formidable as said to be in-
curable by the hospitals and doctors by whom I
was treated. Towards the end of April last year
I had a very bad attack of rheumatism, and this
was accompaned by a swelling in the groin, but
without pain. A chemist gave me some liniment
to rub on the affected part, but this brought tem-
porary relief only. After this I went to work
again for two or three days, but was soon com-
pelled to give in, being unable to continue any
longer. I then consulted a doctor.”
“ Was his treatment successful ? ”
“ No, he told me mine was an exceptional case,
and he could give me but little assistance ; that I
was suffering from a complex trouble —hip joint
disease with locomotor ataxia. My eyesight was
also failing; this, he said, often accompanied
cases of locomotor ataxia. An eminent oculist
told me that it was a case of optic atropy.”
“ What did you do then, Mr. Barsley ? ”
“ I went to three different hospitals in all, and
came out no better than I went in. A friend I
met in one hospital asked me if I had given Dr.
Williams’ Pink Pills a trial. I said I had not. My
friend at once advised me to do so, adding that he
knew of some wonderful cures they had effected in
cases of locomotor ataxia.”
“ Did you follow his advice ? ”
“ I am rejoiced to say I did.”
“ With what result ? ”
“ Well, by the time I had taken three boxes I
felt better, and for this reason determined to go on
, with them. The benefits derived from continuing
I the treatment are very evident. I can now move
about fairly well, getting up and down stairs with-
! out any assistance, whereas when I left the hos-
pital I could only crawl about with difficulty. I
am returning to my usual avocation of pianoforte
tuning, and that, as you know, requires a clear
head and the use of one’s limbs. ”
“ And you feel equal to it, Mr. Barsley? ”
“ I feel quite equal to it, and more too, for I
shall be constantly travelling through the colony,
and of this I shall be doubly glad, as it will afford
me a valuable opportunity to bring sufferers and
sceptics face to face with practical proof of the
wonderful curative powers possessed by Dr. Wil-
liams’ Pink Pills for Pale People.”
For the information of sufferers with hip disease,
we would especially mention that in connection
with the above marvellous cure that the hip bone
was dislocated, necessitating the muscles and tis-
sues doing extra duty for it.
Dr. Will lams’ Pink Pills have cured numerous
cases of paralysis, locomotor ataxy, spinal disease,
rheumatism, and sciatica ; also of diseases arising
from impoverishment and vitiated humors of the
blood, which cause scrofula, rickets, chronic ery-
sipelas, consumption of the bowels and lungs,
anaemia, pale and sallow complexion, general mus-
cular weakness, loss of appetite, palpitations, pains
in the back, nervous headache, early decay, all
forms of female weakness, and hysteria. These
Pills are not a purgative. They are genuine only
with the full name, Dr. Williams’ Pink Pills for
Pale People, and are sold by chemists and by Dr.
Williams’ Medicine Company, Sydney, who will
forward (post paid) on receipt of stamps or post
order, one box for 2s. 9d., or half-dozen for 14s. 9d.
They are unrivalled as a tonic for both sexes.*

“Next time you hear a beer-chewer
termed a beast on account of his thirst, ” writes a
N.Q. correspondent to The Bulletin, “ don’t go
away with the idea of the beast being maligned.
There is in Townsville a goat (the property of a
genial host) who can put away long beers with
any in an that (say) ‘Scotty the Wrinkler ’ could
nominate. His usual method is to rub acquaint-
ance with your legs while you are discussing the
fluid yourself, and upon being shouted for he will
take the glass in his mouth and slug it in real
‘chewer ’ fashion —afterwards licking any he may
have spilt in his eagerness to touch bottom. On a
night when the bowl has been particularly strong,
he makes it a rule to touch nothing till about
noon next day.”
Overheard at a discussion on the Grseco-
Turkish war from a dyspeptic doctor. “Hum!
Heredity be damned ! Look at Giuseppe Gari-
baldi, and then at his boy, Ricciotti. Oliver
Cromwell, and his neurotic product. Where’s
Alexander’s progeny, Csesar’s, Akbar’s, Attila’s,
Raleigh’s, Hawkins’s, Frobisher’s, Drake’s, Davis’s,
Walker, of Nicaragua’s, Bolivar’s, Dunn’s —piles
of others. Look at the best of the dead Aus-
tralians, too, and their progeny—just run over the
list. Those men lived 10 years in every calendar
one, and spent, each of them, the nerve-forco of
several generations.”
They were mates and had been such for
years. Respective names, Bill and Jim. Fact!
Bill was chief pilot, and did all the “magging.”
Jim corroborated Bill’s statements—when appealed
to by Bill. Otherwise he was respectfully silent.
Shearing at Fleeceback’s was over, and before
“ doing in ” their cheques at Cleanout’s pub, they j
(Bill) decided to renovate their travelling para- !
phernalia at Hooker’s general store. They had
two drinks meanwhile, then began the renovating
part of their programme. Bill did the “ buying.’
Jim was not consulted, but was satisfied. Bill
thought they could do with a new tucker-bag, and
said so. Jim agreed. Old Hooker produced the
material needed for building the article, also re-
marked that a yard would suffice. Bill concurred.
Jim did not; for the first time in eight years he
disputed the point and said it would take
“a yard an’ a narf.” Bill was thunder-
stricken at Jim’s audacity; told him he was
always putting in his ‘ ‘ mag, always nag-
ging ” and growling. He (Bill) knew how much
ft took to make a tucker-bag. Anyhow, the chap
behind the counter oughter know—fee was a better
scholar than him (Jim). Jim still reckoned that
it would take a “yard an’a narf. Whereupon
Bill unbosomed himself thus: “Don’t speak to
me ! I’ve had enough of yez ! Shut up! yer no
good ! Yer know where yer swag is I
know where mine is ! Wouldn’t tramp another
m ile with yer ! ” The mates prepared to
“bash” each other. Hooker interfered at this
stage and Jim left. Ten minutes after they met
“up the street” and agreed to “stand each
other for another trip out-back.

To Save His Soul.
[F OR THE BULLETIN.]
** Lister, is he not coming ? ”
She turned on her elbow and looked up piteously
as her attendant entered the room. “ Ah ! I see
by your face that you have failed again. He will
not come. He is a hard man, my brother; he
cannot forgive me; men never can forgive a
woman—for injuring herself. No, he will not
come near me, not though I am dying, dying !
He loved me once ; but now ”
There needed no further explanation. The bare
room, the tarnished walls, the shrill cries and
laughter floating up from a dim network of back
yards far below—what were they but the symbols
of a soiled life and a broken heart? With a low
sob the girl turned her face to the wall once more.
Nurse Agatha had removed her bonnet and
veil, and was stooping to replenish the half-dead
fire. There was so much to do that she had but
time for a hasty glance at her patient; yet she was
wondering, as she had wondered ever since the
committee sent her to this wretched place, what
strange force of circumstances had led to this poor
girl’s downfall. Surely hers was not a common
story of easy betrayal. Else that pure brow and
white, small-featured face were not true index of
the soul within. Even in the girl’s manner Nurse
Agatha fancied at times a subtle consciousness of
at least comparative innocence; true, Jeannie’s
lips never sinned like the rest of them. She had
confessed it many times with tears.
* » * *
To-night she lay stiller than usual, face inclining
towards the pillow. The oft-repeated disappoint-
ment was wearing hope away, and with hope the
acuteness of despair. At last she turned round
once more and faced the brightening fire.
“ Shall I live till to-morrow, nurse ? ” Then,
without waiting for an answer, she went on in a
voice of curious quietness. “ There are worse sins
than mine, nurse Agatha. There is pride and self-
righteousness ! Do you know that I used to be-
long to the Salvationists long ago, long before I
went to keep house for my brother, long before
* ? I learnt that lesson in the Army. The
world says it too, but never acts up to its belief.
My brother was more sincere. He never prac-
tised mercy and never preached it. Oh ! how can
such men dare to think of Heaven ! ”
Nurse Agatha tightened her lips and made no
answer. The Pharisee might be wrong, but it
was hardly the Magdalene’s place to rebuke him.
“ He was specially hard on women, women who
were not good. He would hear no excuse. I re-
member,” she added, slowly, her gaze still intent
on the fire, as though aware of no presence but
her own, “there was a girl in his factory went
wrong, and he dismissed her. I remember how I
pleaded with him to give her another chance. I
told him how young she was, how unprotected.
All in vain. He told me to remember what my
own position was when he was away making his
little fortune, how young I had been, how unpro-
tected. And yet I nad kept good. ‘ Temptation
and circumstances had nothing to do with it,’ he
said ; ‘ it was in some women to be wicked, as it
was in others to be good.’ That was always his
argument I had been good; so he used me, as
good women are used every day, to damn their
sisters to everlasting perdition. Is it not a judg-
ment on him,” she cried with quick, inexplicable
passion, “that the sister whom he put to so vile a
use But, oh, if he would only come ! ” Her
voice broke into wailing. “Nurse, you don’t
know what his coming means. It means his sal-
vation. For his own sake, I pray him to forgive
me. Perhaps he may when I am dead. Let me
die quickly, then—quickly What noise is
that ? ”
* * * *
There was a footstep on the stairs. The
doctor s ? No; this tread was heavier, at once
more eager and more hesitating. ‘ * Archie ! ” she
cried. Someone stood in the doorway looking
round the poor room, now hidden, now revealed
by the flashing fire.
“ Archie ! ” she cried once more, and he stumbled
forward, only to sink kneeling by her side.
“ Archie, you forgive me ? ” she whispered in a
rapture.
He put his arm round her, and, at sight of her
worn features he wept. The roughness of the man’s
nature was broken. . . . “And take you back
with me, and where folks will never know of this.
And —and—don’t die, Jeannie, Jeannie, my girl,
don’t die ! ”
“ And you forgive me ? ” she persisted. It was
an effort for her to speak. “ Say it clearly,
Archie, clearly, so that I may carry the exact
words to Heaven.”
“ I forgive you, Jeannie, and ”
“ What more ? ” she whispered. Her eyes were
watchful.
“ May God forgive me ”
“Your ”
“ Pride and hardness of heart.”
“That is all, Archie,” she whispered, with a
little smile of triumph, and consciousness left her
for a time.
When she awoke Nurse Agatha was bending
over her.
“ Is Archie gone? ”
“Yes, dear, but he’s coming back to-morrow.”
“He will not need to come to-morrow,” she
answered, with strange peacefulness. “It’s ail
right now. He will be merciful to sinners after
this. He can never think of these poor women
now without remembering that his sister was one
of them. It’s like God, you know. I’ve always
had a feeling that He didn’t care so very much for
us till He looked down and saw that Christ was
one of us.”
Nurse Agatha poured a stimulant into a cup.
This was her business, not the controverting of
amazing heresies.
* * * *
Towards morning Jeannie woke again. It was
the death-hour when courage dies and life is at its
lowest ebb. Perhaps this was why she told what
she had never intended for any mortal ear.
The nurse heard her sobbing, and hastened
across the dim-lit room to her bedside.
“ Nurse, I want to tell you something. Don’t
let Archie know. It would undo everything. But,
oh ! I must let someone know I have not, have
never been ”
“Yes, yes, dear,” soothingly; “lie down and
tell me it all to-morrow.”
“ Let me speak to-night. I may not live till
to-morrow. Nurse, I am as pure now as on the
day I was born. I only pretended otherwise —to
'ak his heartK P' save his soul ! ”
Maoriland.
CONSTANCE CLYDE.
La Revanche.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
Carnac limped down the vine-skirted track, and
listened. A splinter of light from the shed located
Kellermann—singing and picking amongst the
piled-up grapes on the floor. Carnac groped
nearer, and the old sabre-wound across his hip
wrung a sharp cry from his lips : Kellermang
might have heard had he not been chantinn
through his nose:
Lieb Vaterland, magst rnhig sein !
Lieb Vaterland, magst ruhig sein 1
Fest steht und treu
Die Wacht, die Wacht am Rhein !
Carnac stopped his ears, and stole back to the
house. Three months ago Kellermann came to
him footsore and begging work ; he was a German
vigneron, but Carnac, the cosmopolitan, gave him
a trial, and the wine he made was equal to his
own Chantilly. Bit by bit, the innocent Keller-
mann had told him the story of bis life —the
campaign of ’7O and ’71 —with flushes of pride that
he had not learned to conceal. In return, Carnac
said nothing ; but here, in the Australian wilds,
Sedan came back to him—the crowd of beaten
lions trapped into a hole, and butchered by Ger-
man mathematicians.
Der Deutsche bieder, fromm und stark,
chanted Kellermann. Carnac swung round in
the track, with the blood leaping to his
listening face. A sudden moisture gathered on
his temples: the ferocious pain across his hip
brought with it the memory of a Uhlan, and a
back-handed sabre-lick under Moltke’s smoking
guns. The sworder was a countryman of Keller-
mann’s, possibly a relative. Kellermann fought
at Sedan, in the front line of Uhlans ; it might
have been Kellermann !
* * • *
Carnac staggered through the long grass, and
the moisture from his face dripped to his sleeve.
He reached the house and brought out a polished
sabre—keen-edged and unfleshed since Sedan. No
sound about the house : Helene—his little girl
Helbne—had been away since dusk. She had gone
over to neighbor Brougaud’s to play with his
children; it was good for Helene to meet other
little folk She would stay there till ten; she
always did. What luck !
His pain-dragged body quivered ; his guard-arm
flew right and left until the sabre sang behind his
ears. Hblbne might miss Kellermann, but he
could say that he had gone west, and she would
soon forget.
The night wind had opened the shed-door ; the
splinter of light was now a white beam against
the darkness, and Kellermann’s neck was moving
in it, sideways and forward to the slow rythrn of
his fulsome hymn. The neck attracted Carnac —
it was so full, so sinewy and round, within arm’s-
length of the doorway, and Carnac knew a certain
cut to fit the narrow space —a short-armed slash
that would send the napeless head bumping over
the wine-bin.
A dingo howled on the near ranges, and in the
stillness that followed Carnac heard the stones
clittering over the rain-flushed creek below.
* * * *
Kellermann was talking—to himself apparently,
and his voice was soft and very winning.
Carnac crouched nearer until his torpedo-shaped
beard brushed the door. Kellermann’s neck
swung across the light, but something was around
it, something white and dimpled like a child’s
arm. Kellerman spoke again.
“ You creep, und creep in here, und you frighten
me like a leedle ghost. Where haf you been,
Helene ? ” His colossal form heaved above the
child’s, while she explained in a whisper that
mother Brougaud was teaching her to sew. She
nodded sleepily from the vine-covered bench, and
Kellermann laughed at her in his big bass.
“ Himmel! I tink dot I should die in dis wilder-
ness if it were not for you, Hel&ne ! —I tink dot I
should die ! ”
He took her up and placed her on his shoulder.
“ Mein work is done, Hel&ne ; led us go to your
fader.” Her hand rested quietly in his sunburnt
fist. He took his pipe from a shelf and tramped
up the silent track under the windless eucalypt that
barred out a red half-risen moon.
IJblbne laughed until her long curls shook about
him like smoke - wreaths. Her piping treble
chorused to his deep bass.
Die Wacht, die Wacht am Rhein !
Carnac crouched back and stumbled; something
clashed on the stones ; he stooped, and his blind-
ing tears fell upon the sword.
ALBA DORION.
A story of the love and marriage of May
and December, and the untimely death of Decem-
ber, and the way May helped it on, is agitating
Maoriland society just now. The marriage took
place several years ago, December being then 60
while May was about 23. Things turned out about
as badly as might have been expected ; May was
ardent and romantic, and full of life, and Decem-
ber was dull and grizzled, and senile, and his feet
were horribly cold in the middle of the night. He
died after seven years married life, and May be-
came the unauthorised bride of a younger man,
whose eye wasn’t quite so rheumy, and whose
hairs were not so few, and whose feet didn’t
register below zero. The new arrangement lasted
three years, and then the paramour drowned him-
self. He left a letter behind him, with instructions
that it wasn’t to be opened till three months
after his death—probably so as to give him tiifie
to make sure that he was quite dead ; and in this
document he stated that he and May had jointly
made away with December by slow poison. The
old man’s ghost had thereupon taken up his resi-
dence with them, and made life unendurable.
Local opinion is divided as to whether the old man
really was poisoned, or whether the young man
was only a lunatic ; anyhow, December is to be
dug up and analysed, but as he has been three
years in the cemetery the chance of any evidence
being found there —unless mineral poison was
used—is limited.

The Whaler's Pig.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
We shipped him at the Sandwich Ti b s—
’Fore God, he’s mostly nose—
We’ve fetched him full eight thousand miles
To fatten in the floes.
The Arctic wind may whistle down
The ice-strewn Baffin Sea,
Our “ passenger ” don’t care a darn
A whaler’s pig is he.
The blubber which the brute devour
Hard fruit of our harpoon—
He merely holds in trust; ’tis ours,
Fresh pork ! God send it soon !
Now, when her sloppy deck’s amuck
With stale cetacean spoil,
The glutton wallows in the ruck,
His paunch a-drip with oil.
When from the crow’s-nest rings the shout,
Clean-echoed “ There she blows ! ”
“Jeff Davis ” lifts his grizzled snout
To let us know he knows.
The white ash blades drop down and rise,
The royal chase begins,
He watches with his wicked eyes,
And multiplies his sins.
With critic squint he stands betide
The harpooneer prepares,
And, if the erring steel goes wide
In swinish tongue he swears
(Great Heavens ! how he swears!)
But when we strike her good and fair,
Before the line runs hot,
He’ll lift a hoarse hog cheer out there
With all the strength he’s got.
And when he sees the steerer take
The bold boat-header’s place,
A gourmand smile will slowly break
Like sunrise round his face.
Around the loggerhead that line
Grows taut as taut may be—
Three turns to hang your life and mine
High o’er Eternity !
Who thinks of that ? Not I, not you,
Not he who most complains,
When like hell’s fire the blood swirls through
Our thumping hearts and veins,
’Tis “ Fast she is ” “ Now ! . . Let her go
Our college stroke-oar yells ;
This hour is worth a life to know ;
’Tis now the savage tells.
They maybe shared (ere progress rose)
Who sired first earls and dukes,
A kindred ecstacy with those
Who dodge a “ fighter’s ’’flukes.
So felt our simian sires who tied
Their sheet-o’-bark canoes
To some mosasaur’s slimy hide
With only life to lose.
But this Kanaka hog will see
The whetted lance succeed ;
Glad epicure, he grunts in glee,
Fore-knowledged of his feed.
Thus will his belly teach his tongue
What eloquence it may
(Some noble songs by poets sung
Have been inspired that way).
So will he squeal approval when
Our six-hour fight is done,
And lord it bravely in his pen
O’er quarry chased and won.
So will he join the chanty free
That echoes as she tows,
To add his porcine jubilee
And glad his adipose.
It is not clean or nice of taste,
This episode of trade,
That lurches with indecent haste
Towards the blubber spade.
But still it goes that man made sail,
Invented rig on rig,
And God Almighty made the whale
That feeds the whaler’s pig.
This sorry beast which might have drowned,
As hogs and humans can,
He also made, so runs the round,
To feed the whaler-man.
The whaler-man will get his “ lay,”
The whaler’s pig his share —
First whale, then pig, then man, some day
The worm will make it square.
E. J. BRADY.
Troedel and Co. (Melb.) have outraged
The Bulletin’s feelings by submitting, for report,
two huge pictures representing their Gracious
Queen. They are seemingly meant to be hung on
a clothes-line, or somewhere, and illuminated from
the rear in the interests of jubilee loyalty. Inas-
much as these gaudy “ transparencies ” are likely
to suit any humble householder who wishes to
rejoice The Bulletin regards them more in
sorrow than in anger, merely remarking that
the royal purple tint on Her Majesty’s cheek is
rank sedition.
Joshua Beard, of Streaky Bay (S.A.), is
to be hanged. The charge brought against
Joshua was that he had a habit of going away with
another man and coming back without him. The
two victims who were dug up were both men of no
wealth; apparently Beard killed them for a
second-hand silver watch, a red handkerchief, a
knife, some clothes, and the remains of a plug of
tobacco. The respectable old Joshua was quite
calm all through the proceedings, and he seems to
be the sort of man who will find religion in a
stolid, grumpy fashion and make an edifying end.
He is one more evidence that it is worst kind of bad
luck to have a nice scriptural name like Boaz, or
Ramoth-Gilead, or Seth Galilee Kerenhappuch,
tagged on to you when you are young. Consider-
ing the comparative scarcity of people called
Joshua, and Jabez, and Jeremiah, the number of
them who drift into the dock is something
appalling. Joshua Beard’s case made no particular
sensation, chiefly because there were no dramatic
circumstances at all about his capture. There
was nothing of the breezy rover about Beard ; he
was taken in a dull, stupid, agricultural fashion,
and he didn’t make one really exciting statement
to anybody ; and, instead of being found armed
to the teeth, as a desperado should be, he had
nothing on him more dreadful than a rather old
and soiled “ wipe ” and an invoice for some butter
A Tip from the Stable .
[F OR THE BULLETIN.]
Bunkins always fancied himself as a sportsman
with an especial gift for picking the winner ; but
he never could induce his friends to see that, on
the numerous occasions on which the ‘ dead birds,
he had put them on to had failed to get home, it
was some stroke of ill-luck or turf villainy that
was the cause, and not his own lack of insight into
the mysteries of weight and distance. Their
sneers grew frequent and bitter, and he began to
grow loth to admit the fact when he backed an-
other loser. , , ,
One day, however, Bunkins got what he had
always hoped for —a real good tip, straight from
the stable, and coinciding with his own opinion of
the particular animal, and the particular race for
which it was tipped. Dreams of a fortune filled
his brain ; and, abandoning the cautious rule he
had hitherto observed of not going beyond a
pound in his bets, he pawned everything available,
and by hook or crook raised the sum of £5O. Then
he hastened to “ Bob,” a friend who attended
racecourses, and who had often put Bunkins
money on for him, and gave instructions to place
the £5O on Sold Again for the last race on the
programme. The horse was quite unknown and
likely to start at a good price, Bunkins thought,
so he would net £5OO at least if it won.
• * • *
Soon after the hour for which the race was
timed, Bunkins rushed round to the hotel-bar he
frequented to cool his fever with a whisky-and-
soda. There he met some chums ; and while en-
deavoring to sustain a conversation, with one eye
on the clock and his thoughts with the crowd of
punters returning from the racecourse, a man
entered with dust on his clothes and a pair of
field-glasses by his side, evidently just from the
course. As the newcomer moved to the counter
and called for a beer, Bunkins could no longer
restrain himself, but stepping forward enquired
in trembling tones who had won the last race.
“The favorite!” said the stranger, gloomily;
and down went poor Bunkins’ heart into his boots
as he thought of his watch and chain, and the
little chance of their redemption.
The “ chums ” were round him directly.
“Hello, Bunkins ! Been backing another
ner ? What a knowing beggar you are ! ”
Bunkins covered his feelings with Spartan forti-
tude.
“ No, gentlemen, I assure you I haven’t a penny
on the race ; ” and so reiterated and decided were
his denials that for once he was believed.
* * * *
Just then entered Bob himself, who strolled
towards them in his usual careless way.
“You’ve heard the result?” said he.
“Yes —the favorite ! ” answered Bunkins, eager
to prevent Bob from exposing his mendacity.
“ Lucky I had nothing on the race, wasn’t it ? ”
Here he winked violently at Bob to put him up to
the game.
“ Oh ! —ah ! —ye-e-s; ” said Bob, looking at him
carefully.
“I made up my mind after that last affair,
when the horse I backed was got at, not to make
another bet; and I’ve stuck to my resolution.
Haven’t I, Bob ? ”
Bob corroborated his statement with great will-
ingness, and, as he turned to order his drink, said
quietly: “Rum thing, Sold Again being made
such a hot favorite ! ”
“ What ! ” said Bunkins, turning first pale, and
then red.
“Yes,” went on Bob ; “ they rushed him at the
last moment, and brought him to three to one.
Why, those in the know got their money on at
twenties. And how did he win ? Hands down,
bigod ! ”
* * * *
Bunkins was dizzy and breathless until he could
get Bob aside.
“ Thank God ! I’m a made man ! ” he cried.
“ What price did you get for me, old man ? ”
Bob stepped back and looked at him coolly.
“ What are you raving about, Bunkins ? ” he
said.
‘ ‘ Raving ! ” answered Bunkins, his eyes nearly
out of his head. “Why! Bob, that £5O I gave
you to put on for me. You ”
“£5O be d d!” said Bob. “You’ve just
told everybody you hadn’t a penny on the race.”
Bunkins nearly fainted. “ But you can’t mean
to treat an old friend like that, Bob ? ”
“ You’re drunk, man ! ” was the only answer.
Rage filled Bunkins’ soul. “ Bob ! I’ll expose
you to everyone.”
“ Bunkins ! they all heard you swear you didn’t
bet now.”
“ Bob ! I’ll break your neck for you unless you
square up ! ”
“ Bunkins ! I’ll get damages if you do.”
The two glared at each other for a few minutes.
Then, as Bunkins realised the hopelessness of the
situation, he turned slowly and despairingly
away, and solaced himself with the unholy com"
forter, whisky.
The next morning Bob banked £lOOO.
• * • •
Now two old friends pass each other in the
street with a stony stare; and while Bob is rapidly
blossoming into a leviathan fielder, Bunkins is
saving so much a week to get back his watch and
studs and put the petty cash right.
F. REA.
Brisbane (Q.) Total Abstinence Society, a
sort of federation of all the local never-touch-it
leagues, recommends the sale of the old Temper-
ance Hall and adjoining property. The water-
swillers have got into debt without the aid of
beer. In ’BB they had paid off all the mortgage
claim, and had ,£3OO to the good. Then the rail-
way came from Roma-street slap through the end
of their property to the central station. The
department gave them £6400 for that. Where-
upon they got swelled-head badly, and bought
land in Turbot-street for £12,000, while they
